Haqiqah and Majaz: Real vs Metaphorical Existence
Definition of haqiqah and majaz in existence with Quranic proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul distinction between haqiqah and majaz to analyze Quranic proofs for the derivative existence of contingent beings.

Core Idea
Only Allah has real existence (haqiqah); all contingents exist only metaphorically (majaz), derived from Him.

This lesson explores a foundational concept in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the distinction between haqiqah (real) and majaz (metaphorical) as applied to existence itself. The author argues that only Allah possesses true, self-subsistent existence, while all created things have only a derived, metaphorical existence. This understanding is supported by multiple Quranic verses and a hadith, and it serves as a metaphysical basis for linguistic categories in usul. The passage also introduces the literary device of bara'at al-istihlal, where the author previews key terms from the introduction that will be elaborated later in the book.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories: haqiqah and majaz
Read the passage and note how the author defines haqiqah as true, self-subsistent existence belonging only to Allah, and majaz as derived, dependent existence for all contingents. Understand the ontological distinction.
The tanwin on 'kull' is explained as compensating for a deleted possessive, emphasizing that 'every contingent thing' is majaz.
2
Locate the Quranic proofs and analyze their wajh al-dalalah
Identify each Quranic verse cited: 28:88, 55:27, 13:16, 2:28. For each, determine how the verse indicates that contingent existence is not real but derived. For example, 'Everything is perishing except His Face' implies that everything other than Allah is subject to annihilation, hence not truly self-subsistent.
The hadith 'Allah existed and nothing else existed with Him' is also used to show that other existences are later and derived.
3
Understand the literary device bara'at al-istihlal
Recognize that the author's mention of haqiqah and majaz in the introduction is a deliberate device to signal that these terms will be discussed in detail later in the book. This illustrates how classical texts often preview core concepts.
This step is not essential for the fiqh ruling but shows the textual methodology of the author.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ربنا : منصوب بتقدير حرف النداء "يا" ، والمقصود بالنداء هو الكلام الآتي. وتقديم الجار والمجرور (لك للحصر والاختصاص، يعني الوجود الحقيقي الثابت الواقعي، والحقيقة النفس الأمرية الثابتة إنما يكون في الحقيقة الله تبارك وتعالى، لا لغيره من الموجودات، لأن وجوده بذاته غير مستفاد من الغير، بخلاف وجود غيره من الممكنات، فهو موجود لذاته، وأما وجود غيره من الممكنات في حد ذاته، فهالكة الذات باطلة الحقيقة في وقت من الأوقات، لقوله تعالى : كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه ) وقوله تعالى : ( و يبقى وجه ربك ذو الجلال والإكرام . وكل مجاز : وتنوين "كل عوض عن المضاف إليه المحذوف، أي كل الممكنات وجميعها مجاز ، والمجاز بفتح الميم ضد الحقيقة، يعني وجود الممكنات وحقائقها ليست بحقيقية، بل وجودها وجود مجازى مستفاد من الغير وهو الواجب جل مجده، لأن وجودها وحقائقها منسوبة إلى جاعلها (خالقها)، وليس لها وجود وحقيقة بدون النسبة إلى الخالق تعالى ، وإنما يثبت لها الوجود بعد خلق الله إياها؛ لقوله تعالى : الله خالق كل شي)، و قوله تعالى : وكنتم امواتا فأحياكم أي معدومين فأوجدكم ، وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : كان الله ولم يكن شي غيره . فائدة وفي ذكر الحقيقة والمجاز صنعة براعة الاستهلال، وهي عبارة عن إيراد ألفاظ في الخطبة والديباجة يبحث في أصل الكتاب عن معانيها وأحكامها، سواء كانت
His statement: 'Our Lord' – it is in the accusative due to the implicit call 'O'. The intended call is the following speech. The prefacing of the prepositional phrase 'to You' is for restriction and specification, meaning that true, fixed, real existence and the reality in itself is only in truth Allah, Blessed and Exalted, not other existents, because His existence is by His essence, not derived from another, unlike the existence of other contingents. So He exists by Himself. As for the existence of other contingents in themselves, they are perishing in essence, null in reality at some times, according to His statement: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (28:88) and His statement: 'And there remains the Face of your Lord, Owner of Majesty and Honor' (55:27). And 'everything is metaphorical' – the tanwin on 'kull' substitutes for the deleted possessive phrase, i.e., all contingents, all of them are metaphorical. 'Al-majaz' (with fatha on the mim) is the opposite of 'al-haqiqah', meaning that the existence of contingents and their realities are not real, rather their existence is a metaphorical existence derived from another, which is the Necessary, may His glory be exalted, because their existence and realities are attributed to their Maker (Creator), and they have no existence or reality without the attribution to the Creator, Exalted. Rather, existence is affirmed for them after Allah creates them, according to His statement: 'Allah is the Creator of everything' (13:16), and His statement: 'And you were dead, so He gave you life' (2:28), meaning you were non-existent, so He brought you into existence, and his statement ﷺ: 'Allah existed and nothing else existed with Him.' Benefit: In mentioning al-haqiqah and al-majaz there is the literary device of 'bara'at al-istihlal', which is the use of words in the introduction and preamble whose meanings and rulings are discussed in the body of the book, whether they are...
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a usul definition and theological explanation of the concepts of haqiqah (real) and majaz (metaphorical) as applied to existence itself. The author, commenting on the matn's phrase 'Our Lord' and 'everything is metaphorical', argues that true, real existence belongs only to Allah. All other beings (contingents) have only metaphorical existence because their existence is derived from and dependent on the Creator. The tanwin on 'kull' (every) compensates for the deleted possessive, meaning 'every contingent thing'. The author provides Quranic proofs (dalil naqli) from Surah al-Qasas, Surah al-Rahman, Surah al-Ra'd, and Surah al-Baqarah, along with a hadith, to support this. He also notes that discussing these terms at the beginning is a literary device (bara'at al-istihlal) that previews topics to be covered in the book. This passage is crucial for understanding the Hanafi metaphysical foundation of the created world's contingent nature.

Key Terms
حقيقة (haqiqah): True, real existence; self-subsistent reality.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical or derived existence; opposite of haqiqah.
براعة الاستهلال (bara'at al-istihlal): A literary device where the introduction previews terms discussed in the book.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the status of the existence of contingent beings?
A) They have independent, real existence like Allah.
B) Their existence is metaphorical and derived from Allah.
C) They are eternal but not divine.
D) They have both real and metaphorical existence simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Only Allah has real existence (haqiqah); all contingent beings exist metaphorically (majaz) because their existence is derived from and dependent on Him.

The Definition of Salat in Language and Shariah
Defines salat linguistically and technically with hadith example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the linguistic and technical definitions of salat and apply the correct meaning in different scriptural contexts.

Core Idea
Salat means supplication in language but specific pillars in law — context determines meaning.

In usul al-fiqh, understanding the precise definition of terms is crucial. This passage clarifies the term 'salat' (prayer) in both its linguistic and technical senses, and shows how its meaning shifts based on the subject — whether referring to humans, angels, or Allah. By mastering this distinction, you will avoid misinterpretations when encountering the word in the Quran and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
Isolate the definitions
Identify the two definitions given: linguistic (supplication) and technical (specific worship with known actions and pillars).
The technical definition is the operative one in fiqh.
2
Analyze the contextual shifts
Note how the meaning changes when attributed to humans (supplication), angels (seeking forgiveness), and Allah (praise or mercy).
This demonstrates linguistic flexibility in revelation.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The hadith about the man who prayed without sending salutation shows that the ritual prayer includes sending blessings, linking linguistic and technical meanings.
The Prophet's response 'You have hastened' indicates incompleteness.
4
Apply to Quranic interpretation
The verse (Quran 33:56) is interpreted as a metaphor for bestowing abundant good, integrating both linguistic and technical nuances.
This shows how usul principles guide tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الله تعالى ، ثم يسلم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ثم يدعو لحاجته ما شاء ، وكذالك جاء في الحديث أن رجلاً جاء إلى المسجد (المسجد النبوي) ، فصلّى ، ولم يصل ويسلّم على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : "عجلت (1) . (۱) ٢- والصلاة في اللغة الدعاء ، وفي اصطلاح الشرع عبارة عن الأفعال المعلومة والأركان المخصوصة ، بل عبارة عن عبادة هي أساس الدين. وإذا أسندت إلى العباد يكون معناها الدعاء ، وإذا نسبت إلى الملائكة تكون بمعنى الاستغفار وطلب الرحمة ، وإذا كانت مسندة إلى الله تعالى يراد بها الثناء كما في البخاري تفسير سورة الأحزاب وعند غيره يراد الرحمة ، وهنا الاخبار بمعنى الدعاء أي أاللهم صل وسلم على سيدنا محمد . والمراد بالصلاة في الآية ان الله وملائكته يصلون على النبي طلب افاضة الخير الكثير بطريق عموم المجاز . (1) لم أجد الحديث بهذا اللفظ الراقم.
May Allah the Exalted, then send salutation upon the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, then supplicate for his need as he wishes. And likewise it came in the hadith that a man came to the mosque (the Prophet's Mosque), prayed, and did not send salutation upon the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. So the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said to him: 'You have hastened (1). (1) 2. And salat in language is supplication, and in the terminology of the Law it is an expression for the known actions and specific pillars; rather it is an expression for a worship that is the foundation of the religion. And when it is attributed to the servants, its meaning is supplication; and when it is attributed to the angels, it is in the sense of seeking forgiveness and asking for mercy; and when it is attributed to Allah the Exalted, what is intended by it is praise, as in al-Bukhari in the explanation of Surah al-Ahzab, and according to others, what is intended is mercy. And here the report is in the sense of supplication, i.e., 'O Allah, send blessings and peace upon our master Muhammad.' And what is meant by salat in the verse is that Allah and His angels send blessings upon the Prophet, seeking the outpouring of abundant good by way of a general metaphor. (1) I did not find the hadith with this wording from the scribe.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the term 'salat' (صلوة). The author begins by referencing a hadith about a man who prayed without sending salutations upon the Prophet, and the Prophet told him he had hastened, indicating that the prayer includes the salutation. Then the author defines salat in two ways: linguistically, it means supplication (دعاء); technically, in Islamic law, it refers to the well-known actions and specific pillars of prayer, and is the foundation of religion. The author further explains how the term is used differently depending on the subject: when attributed to humans, it means supplication; to angels, seeking forgiveness; to Allah, it means praise or mercy. The verse (Quran 33:56) is interpreted as Allah and His angels sending blessings upon the Prophet in a metaphorical sense of bestowing abundant good. This demonstrates the importance of understanding terms in their different contexts, a key principle in usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
صلاة (salat): Linguistically: supplication. Technically: the known actions and specific pillars of Islamic prayer, a worship that is the foundation of religion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The known actions and specific pillars of worship
C) Both supplication and the specific worship
D) Seeking forgiveness

Key Takeaway
The word 'salat' in Islamic texts must be interpreted according to its context — linguistic or technical — a key principle in usul al-fiqh.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Definition of Fiqh as Knowledge of Subsidiary Sharia Rulings
Begins the definition of fiqh from Musallam al-Thubut.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh and explain why a muqallid is not considered a faqih.

Core Idea
Fiqh is active knowledge from evidence, not passive memorization.

In this lesson, we examine the first definition of fiqh from the Hanafi usul text Musallam al-Thubut. The definition emphasizes that fiqh is knowledge of subsidiary (far'iyya) rulings derived from Sharia through ijtihad. This immediately disqualifies the muqallid—one who follows without understanding the proofs—from being called a faqih. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping the Hanafi concept of legal competence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition components
Break down the phrase 'knowledge of subsidiary Sharia rulings' into its three key parts: 'knowledge' (hikma), 'subsidiary' (far'iyya), and 'Sharia rulings' (ahkam shar'iyya).
'Subsidiary' distinguishes fiqh from usul al-fiqh, which deals with principles.
2
Understand the condition of ijtihad
The definition implicitly requires that this knowledge be obtained through ijtihad—independent reasoning from detailed evidence. Without ijtihad, one merely possesses imitation (taqlid).
The term 'faqih' thus implies the capacity (taqa) to derive rulings.
3
Recognize the exclusion of the muqallid
The text explicitly says the muqallid is not called a faqih because he falls short of the required capacity. Reflect on why memorizing rulings is insufficient.
This does not condemn taqlid; it simply defines technical terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف المضاف إليه (الفقه)
و بعد الفراغ عن تعريف المضاف الأصول جمع أصل ) شرع المصنف في تعريف المضاف اليه وهو الفقه وقال:
التعريف الأول للفقه
والفقه : حكمة فرعية شرعية ، فلا يقال فلا يطلق الفقيه على المقلد لتقصيره عن الطاقة (المشروط في الفقيه
وأعلم أنّ المصنف ذكر أربعة تعريفات للفقه في نفس الكتاب، وأشار إلى
Definition of the Mudaf Ilayh (Fiqh)
And after finishing the definition of the mudaf (usul, plural of asl), the author began defining the mudaf ilayh, which is fiqh, and said:
The First Definition of Fiqh
And fiqh: knowledge of subsidiary Sharia rulings. So it is not said—so the term faqih is not applied to the muqallid due to his falling short of the capacity (required in the faqih).
And know that the author mentioned four definitions of fiqh in the same book, and he indicated...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of an usul definition of fiqh. After defining usul (the first part of the compound 'usul al-fiqh'), the author now defines fiqh. He gives the first definition: 'Fiqh is knowledge of subsidiary Sharia rulings.' The term 'subsidiary' (far'iyya) indicates that fiqh deals with practical, branch rulings derived from usul principles. He then adds an important condition: a muqallid (one who blindly follows without understanding the evidence) is not called a faqih. This is because the faqih must have the capacity (taqa) to derive rulings directly from the detailed proofs, which the muqallid lacks. The passage notes that the author (actually the matn author of Musallam al-Thubut) provided four definitions in his book, and the commentator will likely elaborate on them.

Key Terms
حكمة فرعية شرعية (hikma far'iyya shar'iyya): Knowledge of subsidiary (branch) rulings that are derived from Sharia sources, as opposed to usul (foundational principles).
المقلد (al-muqallid): A person who follows a mujtahid without knowing the evidence or reasoning behind the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first definition of fiqh, why is a muqallid not called a faqih?
A) Because a muqallid does not know any rulings.
B) Because a muqallid lacks the capacity to derive rulings from evidence.
C) Because a muqallid follows a different madhhab.
D) Because a muqallid is not a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is knowledge of subsidiary Sharia rulings that requires ijtihad, excluding the muqallid.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The First Definition of Fiqh: Subsidiary Shariah Wisdom
Giving the first definition of fiqh with linguistic and terminological conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of fiqh, explain each of its three qualifiers (hikma, far'iyya, shar'iyya), and justify why a muqallid is not called a faqih according to this definition.

Core Idea
Fiqh is not just following; it is a faculty of knowledge derived from effort in the Shariah branches.

In this lesson, we examine the first terminological definition of fiqh given in Musallam al-Thubut. The author defines fiqh as 'subsidiary Shariah wisdom' and carefully unpacks each word to distinguish true jurists from mere imitators. Understanding this definition is key to grasping the Hanafi epistemology of ijtihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its components
The definition is 'fiqh: hikma far'iyya shar'iyya'. Break down each word. Hikma is firm knowledge with action; far'iyya means subsidiary to fundamentals; shar'iyya means derived from Shariah.
2
Distinguish linguistic from terminological meaning
Linguistically, fiqh means understanding, as in Quranic verses. Terminologically, it is a specific type of knowledge: subsidiary Shariah wisdom.
The linguistic meaning is broader; the terminological meaning specifies the subject matter.
3
Analyze 'hikma' as firm knowledge leading to action
Hikma is not Greek philosophy but beneficial knowledge that requires implementation. It is al-'ilm al-rasikh that does not admit error and leads to action, as per al-Suyuti's explanation.
This distinguishes Islamic fiqh from speculative philosophy.
4
Understand 'far'iyya' and the exclusion of muqallid
Far'iyya means these rulings branch from foundational beliefs (usul). A muqallid lacks the scientific faculty (malaka) resulting from effort, so he cannot be called a faqih.
The condition of badhl al-taqa (expending effort) is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف المضاف إليه (الفقه)

الحكمة .
فعلم واعتقادية، فالفقه حكمة أي علم راسخ مع العمل به، و يتعلق بفروعات الشريعة الإسلامية ، وليس من الحكمة اليونانية التي اخترعها العقول، وخصصوا موضوعها بالموجودات الخارجية ؛ إذ الفقه الأسلامى أعلى وأرفع من تلك الحكمة، وأهم موضوعاتها الاعتقادات الحقة والأخلاق الوجدانية.
أن الحكمة الأصلية هي الأحكام الشرعية فرعية كانت أو أصلية
والمراد بالعلم المفهوم من الحكمة الملكة العلمية الحاصلة من بذل الطاقة في حصول المسائل الفقهية ومن هنا لا يطلق الفقيه على المقلد ؛ لأن الفقيه من له ملكة فقهية، والمقلّد من لم يكن له ذلك، فكيف يقال : للمقلّد فقيه ؟
۳- و فرعية : معناها يتفرع وجوبها والعمل بها بعد وجوب الإيمان وقبول الأصول (أي العقائد الصحيحة الثابتة بالنصوص) وكذا يتفرع التكليف بالفرعيات
Definition of the Addendum (Fiqh)
And after finishing the definition of the addend (usul, plural of asl) the author began defining the addendum, which is fiqh, and said:
The First Definition of Fiqh
And fiqh: subsidiary Shariah wisdom, so the mujtahid (faqih) is not called a muqallid due to his deficiency in capacity (the condition required in the faqih).
And know that the author mentioned four definitions of fiqh in the book itself, and indicated a fifth definition in his commentary. He corrected the first definition, preferred it, and refuted the other three, and remained silent about the fifth.
Explanation of the Conditions:
1- Fiqh linguistically: understanding and comprehension, as in His statement: 'They almost do not understand (yafqahoon) a hadith' and His statement: 'O Shu'ayb, we do not understand (nafqahu) much of what you say.'
And terminologically, it is subsidiary Shariah wisdom.
2- Wisdom (hikma) linguistically: mastery of knowledge and action. It is said: firm knowledge that does not admit error.
And in the first Jalal (al-Suyuti, p.42) under the interpretation of His statement 'He gives wisdom to whom He wills' it is: beneficial knowledge that leads to action.
And Allah mentioned in Surah Bani Isra'il six prohibitions and three commands, all related to subsidiary rulings, and Allah called all of them wisdom, and said: 'That is from what your Lord has revealed to you of wisdom.'
So it is knowledge and belief. Thus fiqh is wisdom i.e., firm knowledge with action, and it pertains to the branches of Islamic Shariah. It is not from Greek philosophy invented by minds, whose subject they specified as external existents; indeed Islamic fiqh is higher and loftier than that wisdom, and its most important subjects are true beliefs and felt morals.
That the original wisdom is the Shariah rulings, whether subsidiary or original.
And by 'knowledge' understood from wisdom is meant the scientific faculty (malaka) resulting from expending effort in acquiring fiqh issues. Hence the faqih is not called a muqallid, because the faqih is one who has a fiqh faculty, while the muqallid is one who does not possess that. So how can one say that a muqallid is a faqih?
3- And 'subsidiary' means that its obligation and action branch after the obligation of faith and acceptance of the fundamentals (i.e., correct beliefs established by texts), and likewise the obligation of subsidiary matters branches.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage introduces the definition of fiqh (jurisprudence) as the addendum (al-mudaf ilayh) in the phrase 'usul al-fiqh'. The author presents the first definition: fiqh is 'subsidiary Shariah wisdom' (hikma far'iyya shar'iyya). He notes that the original author (of Musallam al-Thubut) gave four definitions, corrected the first, and refuted the others. The commentary then explains each condition. Linguistically, fiqh means understanding, supported by Quranic verses. Terminologically, it is 'hikma'—firm knowledge combined with action, not the rational philosophy of the Greeks. 'Far'iyya' means it pertains to subsidiary rulings that follow after foundational beliefs. The key point is that a muqallid (blind follower) cannot be called a faqih because he lacks the faculty (malaka) derived from effort. This underscores the Hanafi emphasis on ijtihad and the capacity to derive rulings.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): Terminologically: subsidiary Shariah wisdom (firm knowledge of practical rulings derived from detailed evidence).
الحكمة (al-hikma): Firm knowledge (al-`ilm al-rasikh) that leads to action, not subject to error.
فرعية (far'iyya): Subsidiary: rulings that branch from foundational beliefs (usul) and are contingent upon them.
المقلد (al-muqallid): One who follows another's opinion without having the faculty (malaka) to derive rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the term 'faqih' should not be applied to a muqallid?
A) Because a muqallid does not act on his knowledge
B) Because a muqallid lacks the scientific faculty (malaka) derived from effort
C) Because a muqallid does not believe in the fundamentals
D) Because a muqallid only follows Greek philosophy

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is not mere knowledge but a faculty earned through effort, excluding the mere imitator from the rank of faqih.

The Definition of Fiqh: Hikma Far'iyya Shar'iyya
Explaining the definition of fiqh with genus and differentiae

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of fiqh as hikma far'iyya shar'iyya, identify the sciences excluded by each restriction, and justify why the exclusion of kalam by convention is acceptable.

Core Idea
Fiqh is practical wisdom from Shari'a, excluding foundational theology and non-shar'i crafts.

Building on the classification of knowledge, this lesson unpacks the precise definition of fiqh as 'hikma far'iyya shar'iyya'. You will see how the genus 'hikma' splits into theoretical and practical, and how the two restrictions narrow the scope to a derived, shar'i science of actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and its types
The definition starts with 'hikma' as a genus. Within it, there is theoretical wisdom (like kalam) and practical wisdom (like fiqh). Recognizing this division is the first step.
Theoretical wisdom aims at knowledge for its own sake; practical wisdom aims at action.
2
Understand the restriction 'far'iyya'
'Far'iyya' means derivative or branch. This excludes 'ilm al-kalam, which is an original science (asliyya) and does not branch from another discipline. Fiqh, by contrast, derives its rulings from the four sources.
The author notes that this exclusion is unproblematic because it was a recognized convention ('urf ma'ruf).
3
Understand the restriction 'shar'iyya'
'Shar'iyya' means relating to Shari'a. This excludes any practical wisdom that is not connected to Islamic law, such as crafts and trades (al-sana'i' wa al-hiraf). These are worldly skills, not religious rulings.
The term 'new wisdom' (al-hikma al-jadida) may refer to secular arts.
4
Appreciate the conventional nature of excluding kalam
Although theoretically possible to include kalam under practical wisdom, the early scholars agreed to treat kalam as a separate discipline. This convention is accepted; only the restriction to sensory matters is criticized.
This step prepares for the next lesson on the critique of al-hissiyat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد التكليف بالعقائد والأصول التي سماها المتقدمون من العلماء علم الكلام، فعلم الكلام حكمة أصلية، لا يتفرع على شيء آخر. ومعنى شرعية: أي تدل على ثبوت تلك الفروع والعمل بها الأدلة الشرعية (الأربعة التي يبحث عنها في أصول الفقه). فالحكمة جنس تشمل الحكمة النظرية التي غايتها النظر والفكر دون العمل كعلم الكلام، والحكمة العملية التي المطلوب منها العمل، كعلم الفقه، فبقيد "فرعية" خرج علم الكلام، وبقيد شرعية خرجت الحكمية العملية التي ليس لها تعلق بالشرع كالصنائع والحرف الجديدة، أي الحكمة الجديدة. ٥- ولا يطلق الفقيه على المقلد لتقصيره عن الطاقة أى طاقة الاجتهاد المشروطة للفقيه المجتهد، ومن أجل اعتبار الملكة العلمية في الفقيه (المفهومة من لفظ) خرج فقه المقلّد عن تعريف الفقه، وخرج المقلد نفسه عن تعريف الفقيه المعتبر فيه وجود طاقة الاجتهاد. والتخصيص بالحسبات احترازاً عن التصوف في تعريف الفقه حديث محدث، وكلام جديد لم يكن هذا التخصيص في المتقدمين نعم الاحتراز عن الكلام عرف معروف. وأما التقييد بقيد "فرعية" لإخراج علم الكلام عن تعريف الفقه فلا بأس به لأنه عرف معروف. وقال المصنف في تعليقه على قوله : "بالحسيات": اعلم أن الفقه في القديم كان متناولاً لعلم الحقيقة، وهي العلم بالصفات الالهية وأفعالها واسمائها وأحكامها، ومتناولاً لعلم التزكية والتربية، وهي مباحث المهلكات والمنجيات، ومتناولاً لعلم الشريعة الظاهرة، ومن ثم عرّفه أبو حنيفة الامام بـ (معرفة النفس مالها وماعليها) وسمى كتابه في العقائد بـ ( الفقه الأكبر) وقال تعالى اشارة الى هذا المعنى العام فلو نفر من كل فرقة منهم طائفة ليتفقهوا في الدين ) وقال النبي مشيراً إلى ذلك العموم: "من يرد الله به خيراً يفقهه في الدين". ثم لما تصدّى قوم للبحث عن العقائد، وسموا العلم الكافل بذلك بـ "علم الكلام" أطلق الفقه على المطالب العلمية الشاملة للتصوف أيضاً، وهو علم الأخلاق والتزكية، ثم حدث في زمان لاحق اختصاص الفقه بالأحكام الظاهرة الحسية، ومن ثم تری كتب الفقه للمتأخرين خالية عن علم التزكية. انتهى.
After obligation with beliefs and principles which the early scholars called 'ilm al-kalam, for 'ilm al-kalam is original wisdom, not branching from anything else. And the meaning of shar'iyya: that it indicates the establishment of those branches and acting upon them by the shar'i proofs (the four that are investigated in usul al-fiqh). Al-hikma is a genus encompassing theoretical wisdom whose goal is contemplation and thought without action, like 'ilm al-kalam, and practical wisdom which requires action, like fiqh. By the restriction 'far'iyya', 'ilm al-kalam is excluded; by the restriction 'shar'iyya', practical wisdom not related to the Law, such as new crafts and trades (i.e., new wisdom), is excluded. 5. The term faqih is not applied to the muqallid because of his deficiency in the capacity (i.e., the capacity for ijtihad required of a mujtahid faqih). Because of considering the scientific faculty in the faqih (understood from the word), the fiqh of the muqallid is excluded from the definition of fiqh, and the muqallid himself is excluded from the definition of faqih, which considers the existence of the capacity for ijtihad. And restricting by 'al-hissiyat' to avoid tasawwuf in the definition of fiqh is a recent innovation, a new statement; this restriction was not present among the early scholars. Yes, the avoidance of kalam (by [the restriction]) is a recognized practice. As for the restriction by 'far'iyya' to exclude 'ilm al-kalam from the definition of fiqh, it is fine because it is a recognized practice. And the author said in his commentary on his statement 'bi al-hissiyat': Know that fiqh in early times encompassed 'ilm al-haqiqa – which is knowledge of the divine attributes, acts, names, and rulings – and encompassed 'ilm al-tazkiya wa al-tarbiya – which are the investigations of things that destroy and save – and encompassed the science of external shari'a. Hence Imam Abu Hanifa defined it as 'the soul's knowledge of what is for it and against it' and named his book on creed 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar'. And Allah the Exalted alluded to this general meaning: 'Why should not a group from every party go forth to become learned in the religion' and the Prophet, peace be upon him, indicated that generality: 'Whoever Allah desires good for, He grants him understanding of the religion.' Then when some people devoted themselves to investigating beliefs and named the science covering that 'ilm al-kalam, fiqh was applied to the scholarly demands inclusive of tasawwuf as well – that is, the science of ethics and purification. Then at a later time, specialization of fiqh to external, sensory rulings occurred. Hence you see the fiqh books of the later scholars empty of the science of purification. End.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the definition of fiqh and its scope. It begins by explaining the term 'hikma far'iyya shar'iyya': 'hikma' is a genus that includes theoretical wisdom (like kalam) and practical wisdom (like fiqh). The restriction 'far'iyya' excludes kalam because it is a foundational science, not a branch. The restriction 'shar'iyya' excludes non-shar'i practical sciences like crafts. The passage then addresses who is called a faqih: only a mujtahid, not a muqallid, because the faqih must have the capacity for ijtihad. Next, it critiques the restriction 'bi al-hissiyat' (external/sensory actions) as an innovation when used to exclude tasawwuf (spiritual purification) from fiqh. The author argues that early scholars like Abu Hanifa included spiritual knowledge in fiqh, as seen in his 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar' on creed and his definition 'knowledge of the soul's rights and duties'. The restriction to external rulings only occurred later. However, excluding kalam via 'far'iyya' is acceptable because it was a recognized practice among early scholars. The passage thus clarifies the historical evolution of the term fiqh and defends the broader definition while accepting the exclusion of kalam as a useful conventional distinction.

Key Terms
حكمة فرعية شرعية (hikma far'iyya shar'iyya): A practical wisdom that is derived from shar'i sources and deals with actions
فرعية (far'iyya): Derived from another discipline; fiqh is a branch of usul al-fiqh and based on the four proofs.
شرعية (shar'iyya): Pertaining to Islamic law; distinguishes fiqh from secular practical sciences.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the restriction 'far'iyya' exclude from the definition of fiqh?
A) Non-shar'i crafts and trades
B) 'Ilm al-kalam (theology)
C) Both kalam and crafts
D) Nothing; it confirms the inclusive nature

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is defined as practical wisdom (hikma) that is both derivative (far'iyya) and shar'i (shar'iyya), excluding theology and worldly crafts.

Who is a Faqih? The Ijtihad Requirement and the Scope of Fiqh
Debating the faqih's qualifications and the scope of fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a faqih and a muqallid, critique the later restriction of fiqh to sensory matters, and explain the historical evolution of the term fiqh from the early inclusive definition to the later specialized one.

Core Idea
A faqih is a mujtahid; restricting fiqh to external actions is a later innovation contrary to early practice.

After establishing the definition of fiqh, the passage addresses two critical points: who qualifies as a faqih and whether fiqh should be limited to external actions. This lesson examines the ijtihad requirement and defends the original inclusive scope that included creed and spirituality.

Guided Path
1
State the condition for being called faqih
The passage states that a faqih must have the capacity (taqa) for ijtihad. A muqallid, who merely follows without evidence, is not a faqih because he lacks the independent reasoning ability needed for derivation.
The term 'faqih' is reserved for mujtahids; later Hanafis used 'faqih' more loosely, but the classical definition stands.
2
Explain why muqallid is excluded
The fiqh of a muqallid is not considered fiqh proper because it lacks the component of ijtihad. The author says 'they are deficient in capacity' (li-taqsirihim 'an al-taqa).
This exclusion is based on the verbal noun 'fiqh' implying understanding derived directly from evidence.
3
Analyze the critique of restricting fiqh to sensory matters (al-hissiyat)
The passage calls the restriction 'bi al-hissiyat' a recent innovation (hadith muhdath) when used to exclude tasawwuf (spiritual purification). Early scholars included both external rulings and inner states.
The author cites Abu Hanifa's definition 'ma'rifat al-nafs ma laha wa ma 'alayha' and his book 'al-Fiqh al-Akbar' on creed.
4
Understand the historical evolution
Originally fiqh encompassed three areas: 'ilm al-haqiqa (creed), 'ilm al-tazkiya (spirituality), and 'ilm al-shari'a al-zahira (external law). Later, when kalam became a separate discipline, fiqh lost creed but still included tasawwuf. Only recently was it narrowed to external sensory rulings.
This historical account shows that the modern restriction is a departure from early practice.
5
Recognize the conventional exclusion of kalam vs. the problematic exclusion of tasawwuf
The exclusion of kalam via 'far'iyya' is accepted as an 'urf ma'ruf (recognized convention). But the exclusion of tasawwuf via 'hissiyat' lacks early precedent and is therefore criticized.
This distinction clarifies why one exclusion is permissible and the other is not.

The Second Definition of Fiqh: Testing Its Scope
Presentation of a definition and a logical objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the second definition of fiqh by applying the standards of jami' (comprehensiveness) and mani' (exclusiveness) using the two counterexamples from the passage.

Core Idea
A correct definition must include all faqihs and exclude all non-faqihs.

In this passage, the author presents a standard definition of fiqh — knowledge of shar'i rulings from detailed evidences — and then subjects it to a rigorous logical test. The test reveals that depending on how we interpret the word 'ahkam' (rulings), the definition either fails to cover all faqihs or fails to exclude muqallids. This exercise sharpens our understanding of what it truly means to be a faqih in the Hanafi tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the second definition of fiqh: knowledge of shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences. Recognise that it has three components: ‘ilm (knowledge), al-ahkam al-shar‘iyya (shar‘i rulings), and min adillatiha al-tafsilīyya (from detailed evidences).
This definition is more specific than mere knowledge of rulings.
2
Understand the two interpretations of 'ahkam'
The objection hinges on the scope of 'ahkam'. It can be interpreted as 'all rulings' (jami‘) or 'any ruling' (mutlaq). Each interpretation leads to a different problem.
The word is ambiguous; the definition does not specify.
3
Apply the standard of jami' (comprehensiveness)
If 'ahkam' means all rulings, then only someone who knows every shar‘i ruling would be a faqih. But scholars like Abu Hanifa and Malik sometimes said 'la adri' (I don't know), showing they did not know all rulings. Yet they are still considered fuqaha. Thus the definition fails to include all actual faqihs.
This shows the definition is not comprehensive.
4
Apply the standard of mani' (exclusiveness)
If 'ahkam' means absolute rulings (any ruling), then a muqallid who knows some rulings but follows without evidence would be included, because he has knowledge of some rulings. But a muqallid is not a faqih. Thus the definition fails to exclude non-faqihs.
This shows the definition is not exclusive.
5
Conclude the requirements for a proper definition
A valid definition must be both jami‘ and mani‘. The objection exposes that this definition, as stated, is ambiguous. To fix it, one must specify the scope of 'ahkam' or add a condition like 'that qualifies one for ijtihad'.
This exercise illustrates a core usul al-fiqh skill: testing definitions against counterexamples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التعريف الثانى للفقه وعرفوه بأنه العلم بالأحكام الشرعية من أدلتها التفصيلية، وأورد الإشكال على هذا التعريف بأنه: ١- إن كان المراد ((بالأحكام) الجميع (جميع الأحكام) فلا ينعكس التعريف (أي لا يكون التعريف جامعاً لجميع أفراد المعرف) لثبوت لا أدرى" (أي اظهار العجز عن علم بعض المسائل من بعض الأئمة، حنيفة ومالك) فلا يكونان فقيهاً. ٢- وإن كان المراد مطلق الأحكام (سواء كان كلها أو بعضها فلايكون التعريف مانعاً عن دخول غير المعرّف في أفراد المعرف) وهذا شرح قوله: (أو المطلق فلا يطرد) وإنما لا يكون التعريف مطرداً (مانعاً عن دخول الغير) لدخول المقلد العالم (في تعريف الفقيه).
The second definition of fiqh: They defined it as knowledge of the shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences. An objection was raised against this definition: 1. If by 'rulings' is meant all (all rulings), then the definition is not convertible (i.e., it does not encompass all individuals of the defined) because the statement 'I don't know' is confirmed (i.e., showing inability to know some issues from some imams, Abu Hanifa and Malik), so they would not be faqihs. 2. If what is meant is absolute rulings (whether all or some), then the definition is not exclusive (preventing entry of non-defined into individuals of the defined). This is the explanation of his statement: 'or the absolute is not universally true', and the definition is not universally true (preventing entry of other) because of the inclusion of the learned muqallid (into the definition of faqih).
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the second definition of fiqh: knowledge of the shar'i rulings from their detailed evidences. The author then raises a logical objection regarding the scope of 'ahkam' (rulings). The objection is that the definition either fails to be inclusive (jami') or fails to be exclusive (mani'). If 'ahkam' means all rulings, then scholars like Abu Hanifa and Malik who sometimes said 'I don't know' about certain issues would not be considered faqihs, which is false; thus the definition does not cover all actual faqihs. If 'ahkam' means any rulings (absolute), then the definition would include a muqallid who knows some rulings, but a muqallid is not a faqih, so the definition fails to exclude non-faqihs. The passage clarifies that a correct definition must be both comprehensive (including all faqihs) and exclusive (excluding non-faqihs). This is a typical usul al-fiqh exercise of testing definitions against counterexamples.

Key Terms
الأحكام الشرعية (al-ahkam al-shar'iyya): The rulings derived from the Law
لا أدرى (la adri): An expression of inability to know a ruling, attributed to imams like Abu Hanifa and Malik
مطلق الأحكام (mutlaq al-ahkam): Rulings in an absolute sense, whether all or some
مقلد عالم (muqallid 'alim): A person knowledgeable of some rulings but follows without knowing evidence; not a faqih

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the objection to the second definition of fiqh when 'ahkam' means absolute rulings (مطلق)?
A) The definition excludes scholars who said 'la adri'.
B) The definition would include a muqallid who knows some rulings, but not all.
C) The definition requires knowledge of all rulings, which is impossible.
D) The definition ignores the condition of ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
A definition of fiqh must be both comprehensive (include all faqihs) and exclusive (exclude muqallids), and the meaning of 'ahkam' must be precisely specified.

The Second Definition of Fiqh: Responding to the 'La Adri' Objection
Presents the second definition of fiqh and responds to an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the second definition of fiqh, identify the logical objection ('la adri'), and apply the Hanafi response that 'knowledge' means the faculty (malaka).

Core Idea
Fiqh is the faculty to derive rulings, not memorization of every detail.

The second definition of fiqh is compact yet powerful: 'knowledge of the legal rulings from their detailed evidences.' But does this mean a jurist must know every ruling? The objection of 'I do not know' from great imams challenges this. This lesson unpacks the definition, the logical problem, and the Hanafi resolution through the concept of malaka (faculty). Understanding this will sharpen your grasp of what it truly means to be a faqih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and its components
Read the definition: 'knowledge of the legal rulings from their detailed evidences.' Break it down: 'knowledge' (al-ilm), 'legal rulings' (al-ahkam al-shar'iyya), 'detailed evidences' (al-adilla al-tafsiliyya). Reflect on what each term contributes.
The definition is a classical logical definition (hadd) – it specifies a genus (knowledge) and differentia (legal rulings from detailed evidences).
2
Unpack the objection based on 'all' vs. 'absolute'
The objection argues two possibilities: If 'rulings' means all rulings, then imams like Abu Hanifa who said 'I do not know' for some rulings would not be jurists – definition fails to cover all individuals (ghayr insikak). If 'rulings' means any rulings (some), then a learned muqallid who knows some rulings but cannot derive would be included – definition fails to exclude non-jurists (ghayr tard).
This is a standard logical objection to definitions: testing for completeness (jami') and exclusivity (mani').
3
Examine the first response: knowledge as faculty
The answer reinterprets 'knowledge' in the definition: it is not actual knowledge of every ruling, but the firm faculty (malaka) of deriving rulings from evidence. A faqih has this faculty even if he occasionally lacks knowledge of a specific ruling – 'la adri' does not negate being a faqih.
Malaka is a central Hanafi concept: a stable trait in the soul enabling ijtihad.
4
Examine the second response: evidences as indications
The second answer treats 'evidences' (adilla) as 'amarat' (indications) – signs that point to rulings, not necessarily exhaustive detailed proofs. This allows the definition to cover the mujtahid's work without requiring complete knowledge of all evidences.
This aligns with the probabilistic nature of ijtihad: most fiqh rulings are zanni (speculative), not qat'i.
5
Clarify the scope of 'legal rulings'
The passage specifies that 'legal rulings' means subsidiary rulings (furū') of fiqh – not creedal matters (usul al-din) or necessary rational beliefs like God's existence. This distinguishes fiqh from kalam.
This exclusion prevents the definition from being too broad and keeps fiqh within practical jurisprudence.

(۱) فـ (العلم ) في تعريف الفقه جنس يشمل جميع العلوم، وبقوله بالأحكام الشرعية، خرج جميع الأحكام غير الشرعية، وكذلك خرج بهذا القيد علم الكلام؛ لأن المراد بالأحكام الشرعية الأحكام الشرعية الفرعية، فعلم الكلام علم بالأحكام
الأصلية الاعتقادية ومعنى الأحكام الشرعية : الأحكام التي عُلمت بالشرع، أي التي يتوقف علمها على الشرع، سواء كان للعقل فيها دخل أم لا، فالعلم بوجوده تعالى واجب عقلي، ولو لم ينزل الشرع، فإن أصحاب العقول السليمة كلهم قائلون بوجوده تعالى، وأما الدهريون فهم أيضاً قائلون بوجود موجود واجب الوجود الذى ينتهي إليه سلسلة وجود الكائنات، ولكن أخطئوا في تعيين مصداق ذلك الموجود دائم الوجود، فجعلوه مادة لاحياة لها ولا علم ولا شعور ولا قدرة بل ذلك الموجود هو الله تعالى.
The second definition of fiqh: They defined it as: knowledge of the legal rulings from their detailed evidences. An objection was raised against this definition, namely:
1. If by 'rulings' is meant all rulings (all rulings), then the definition is not convertible (i.e., the definition does not encompass all individuals of the definiendum) because of the occurrence of 'I do not know' (i.e., the display of inability regarding knowledge of some issues from some imams, such as Abu Hanifa and Malik) — then they would not be jurists.
2. If by 'rulings' is meant absolute rulings (whether all or some), then the definition is not preventive (excluding non-definiendum from entering into the individuals of the definiendum). This is the explanation of his statement: 'or absolute, then it does not apply consistently'. It is not consistent (preventive of entry of others) because the learned muqallid would enter into the definition of jurist.
Then he answered the objection in two ways and said: It is answered first that 'I do not know' does not harm, because what is meant by 'knowledge' in the definition of fiqh is the faculty (malaka), so it is permissible for knowledge to be absent regarding some issues. Second, that by 'legal evidences' is meant indications (amarat), i.e., signs.
Shaykh al-Martunki said: Since 'knowledge' can be used to mean its issues, or assent to the issues, or the faculty resulting from assent, this definition became subject to the following objection: What is meant by rulings? All of them or some of them? This is based on the assumption that by 'knowledge' is meant the issues themselves or assent to the issues. But if by 'knowledge' is meant the faculty resulting from assent, then the objection does not arise, as the author indicated in the answer. This is unlike the first definition; for the wisdom mentioned therein is a faculty, so that objection does not arise.
(1) Thus 'knowledge' in the definition of fiqh is a genus encompassing all sciences. By his statement 'legal rulings', all non-legal rulings are excluded. Likewise, theology is excluded by this restriction because what is meant by 'legal rulings' are subsidiary legal rulings; theology is knowledge of original creedal rulings. The meaning of 'legal rulings' is: rulings that are known by revelation, i.e., whose knowledge depends on revelation, whether reason has a role in them or not. For knowledge of His existence, the Exalted, is intellectually obligatory, even if revelation had not been sent; for people of sound intellect all affirm His existence. As for the materialists, they too affirm the existence of an existent wujud wajib al-wujud from which the chain of existence of things originates, but they erred in identifying the referent of that eternal existent, so they made it a dead matter with no life, knowledge, consciousness, or power — rather that existent is Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents the second definition of fiqh commonly used in Hanafi usul: 'knowledge of the legal rulings from their detailed evidences.' The author then discusses a logical objection concerning two possible interpretations of 'rulings'. If it means all rulings, then the definition fails to include all jurists (e.g., Abu Hanifa and Malik sometimes said 'I do not know'), so they would not be jurists. If it means any rulings, then a learned muqallid (one who follows without full evidence knowledge) would be included, which is undesirable. The author provides two answers: First, 'knowledge' here refers to the jurist's faculty (malaka) of deriving rulings, not actual knowledge of every single ruling, so occasional ignorance does not negate being a jurist. Second, 'evidences' are treated as 'indications' that enable ijtihad, not necessarily complete detailed knowledge. The commentator (Shaykh al-Martunki) elaborates that the objection only arises if 'knowledge' is taken as the set of issues or assent, but if taken as the faculty, it is resolved. Finally, the passage clarifies that 'legal rulings' in the definition means only subsidiary rulings (furū'), excluding theology and necessary rational beliefs. This distinction is important for keeping fiqh within its proper domain of practical law.

Key Terms
العلم (al-'ilm): The faculty (malaka) of deriving rulings from evidence; includes occasional ignorance.
الأحكام الشرعية (al-ahkam al-shar'iyya): Rulings known by revelation; specifically subsidiary (furū') rulings in fiqh definition.
الأدلة التفصيلية (al-adilla al-tafsiliyya): Detailed evidence for each ruling (Quran, hadith, ijma, qiyas); sometimes interpreted as indicators (amarat).
الملكة (al-malaka): A firm faculty in the soul enabling derivation of rulings.
لا أدرى (la adri): Statement of 'I do not know' used by imams like Abu Hanifa and Malik for rulings they were unsure about.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because 'I do not know' was said only rarely
B) Because 'knowledge' in the definition means the faculty of deriving rulings, not actual knowledge of all cases
C) Because the imams were not considered jurists in later times
D) Because the definition applies only to beginners

Key Takeaway
Fiqh is defined as the faculty (malaka) of deriving legal rulings from their detailed evidences, allowing for occasional ignorance without disqualifying the jurist.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Definition of Fiqh and Exclusion of the Muqallid
Defining fiqh and excluding the muqallid through potential knowledge and zanni evidences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two ways the Hanafi definition of fiqh excludes the muqallid and explain the role of zann and ijma in the mujtahid's authority.

Core Idea
The muqallid's knowledge rests on the mujtahid's definitive statement, not on his own probabilistic reasoning.

In this passage, al-Badakhshani expands on the definition of fiqh from Musallam al-Thubut. He clarifies that the mujtahid is a faqih even if not all rulings are actualized, and that the muqallid is excluded because his knowledge is not from detailed evidences. The text then defends against an objection by distinguishing between certainty of the ruling and certainty of obligation to act, invoking consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of the definition
The passage gives two defenses for excluding the muqallid: interpreting 'knowledge' as potential faculty (malakah) and interpreting 'evidences' as probabilistic (zanni).
These two aspects ensure the definition includes the mujtahid and excludes the muqallid.
2
Understand the mujtahid vs muqallid distinction
The muqallid's basis is the definitive statement of his mujtahid, not his own zann or the mujtahid's zann. The mujtahid's zann leads to certainty of obligation, but the muqallid cannot claim that.
The text uses ijma to affirm that only the mujtahid's zann obligates action.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection: zanni evidences yield only probability, but fiqh is definitive. The response: zann leads to definitive knowledge of the obligation to act (wujub al-amal), not certainty of the ruling itself.
This distinction is critical in Hanafi usul.
4
Recognize the use of ijma as proof
The consensus (ijma) that only the mujtahid can obtain such knowledge is invoked as a qat'i proof.
Ijma in Hanafi usul is a definitive source.
5
Consider the definition type: hadd vs rasm
The passage notes that if fiqh is defined as knowledge of obligation, it is a definition by necessary property (rasm), not an essential definition (hadd), which is permissible.
This resolves a potential terminological issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالعلم الملكة الحاصلة من التصديق بالمسائل، فلا شك أن أبا حنيفة ومالكاوكل فقيه عنده ملكة فقهية للاستنباط يستطيع العلم بجميع الأحكام لأجل تلك الملكة، وإن لم يكن حاصلاً له بالفعل، فالمراد بالعلم بالأحكام هو العلم بالقوة دون الفعل، فشمل تعريف الفقه علم هؤلاء الفقهاء، وصار التعريف جامعاً لأفراده .

وحاصل الجواب باعتبار الشق الثاني : أن المراد بالأدلة في قوله ( من أدلتها التفصيلية الأمارات والعلامات أي الأدلة الظنية، فيكون التعريف مانعاً عن دخول الغير، وهو فقه المقلّد ؛ لأن تحصيل العلم بالأحكام لوجوب العمل من الأدلة الظنية إنما يكون عن خواص المجتهد، وليست هذه المرتبة حاصلة للمقلّد بالاجماع.

فإن المقلّد لو حصل له العلم بوجوب العمل بمسألة شرعية عن دليل ظني لا يكون له سنداً ، ولا يجب عليه العمل بتلك المسألة، وكذا لا يكون سنداً لغيره، ولا يجب على غيره العمل بها أيضاً، فإن الشند للمقلّد قول المجتهد القطعي، لا قول المقلّد نفسه على الظن ، ولا قول المجتهد المبنى على الظن، وإنما يكون دليل المقلّد القول القطعى للمجتهد ، وهذا معنى قوله : " وأما المقلد فمستنده قول مجتهده لاظنه ولاظنه.

و يرد على هذا الجواب إشكال : وهو أن الدلائل الظنية مفيدة للظن، والظن لا يكون موصلاً إلى العلم القطعى، والفقه علم قطعى ، فكيف يصح أن يراد من الأدلة الأمارات والأدلة الظنية ؟ والجواب عنه أن الظنّ هنا يفضى إلى العلم القطعى بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية، فالظن قد يفضي إلى العلم بوجوب العمل، وان لم يفض إلى الجزم بثبوت الدليل. وتقدير العبارة في الأصل هكذا : "بأنه العلم القطعى بتقدير الصفة) بالأحكام، أي بوجوب العمل ( بتقدير المضاف والمضاف اليه بالأحكام الشرعية.

ثم أراد المصنّف تفصيل الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني وقال:

و تحصيل العلم بوجوب العمل بتوسط أي بواسطة) الظنّ من خواص المجتهد إجماعاً و أما المقلد فمستنده (دليله) قول مجتهده (أي القول الثابت بالقطع عن مجتهده) لا ظنّ المقلّد أي لا علمه المبنى على الظنّ) ولا ظنّ المجتهد أي القول المظنون له فاعرف الفرق بين المجتهد والمقلّد، حتّى لا تقول مثل من قال: كما أن مظنون المجتهد واجب العمل عليه ، كذلك وجب العمل على مقلده بمظنونه ، فهما سيّان متساويان في وجوب العمل بالظن )

أي تحصيل العلم القطعى بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية بواسطة العلم الحاصل من الأدلة الظنية من خواص المجتهد بالاجماع ؛ لأن العمل بمقتضى الظن واجب على المجتهد ،بالاجماع، فإن المجتهد إذا قال : هذا مظنوني مجتهداً (أي علمته بالظن حال كوني مجتهداً) وكل ما هو مظنون المجتهد فهو واجب العمل" ينتج أنّ هذا واجب العمل .

فالصغرى (هذا) مظنوني بديهية، ودليل الكبرى وكل ما هو مظنون المجتهد فهو واجب العمل الاجماع

وأما المقلّد فلا يصلح ظنه أن يكون دليلاً يجب العمل به؛ لأنه ناقل عن المجتهد، ومن ثم قال المصنف : وأما المقلّد فمستنده ودليله قول مجتهده، لا ظنه، أي لا علمه المبني على الظن ولا ظنّ مجتهده أي لا قول مجتهده المبنى على الظن ؛ فان الظن لا يغنى من الحق شيئاً، أي لا يحصل اليقين من الظن، سواء كان ظن المقلد نفسه أو ظن مجتهده . ولما كان يرد الإشكال على الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني أشار إليه المصنف بقوله:

نعم يلزم أن يكون (الفقه) عبارة عن العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية، لا العلم بها (بالأحكام الشرعية) (ثمّ أشار إلى الجواب عنه فقال : إلّا أن يقال إنه تعريف الفقه) رسم (لا حد فيجوز التعريف باللازم) ( ثم أشار إلى اعتراض آخر وقال وفيه ما فيه
By knowledge is meant the faculty (malakah) resulting from assenting to the issues; there is no doubt that Abu Hanifah, Malik, and every jurist (faqih) has a juristic faculty for deduction, enabling him to know all rulings because of that faculty, even if it is not actually present for him. So what is meant by knowledge of rulings is knowledge in potentiality (al-ʿilm bil-quwwah) not in actuality (al-fiʿl). Thus the definition of fiqh includes the knowledge of these jurists, and the definition becomes comprehensive of its individuals.

And the gist of the answer with respect to the second aspect: that what is meant by 'evidences' in his statement 'from its detailed evidences' are indicators and signs, i.e., probabilistic evidences (al-adillah al-ẓanniyyah). Therefore the definition prevents the entry of others, that is, the fiqh of the muqallid; because obtaining knowledge of rulings regarding obligatory action from probabilistic evidences is only for the specialists among the mujtahids, and this rank is not attained by the muqallid by consensus (al-ijmāʿ).

For if the muqallid obtains knowledge of the obligation to act upon a legal issue from a probabilistic evidence, it would not be a proof (sanad) for him, nor would he be obligated to act upon that issue; likewise it would not be a proof for others, nor would others be obligated to act upon it either. Rather, the proof for the muqallid is the definitive statement of the mujtahid—not the muqallid's own opinion based on probability, nor the mujtahid's opinion based on probability. The muqallid's evidence is only the definitive statement of the mujtahid. That is the meaning of his statement: 'As for the muqallid, his basis is the statement of his mujtahid, not his own probability, nor his probability.'

And an objection is raised against this answer: that probabilistic evidences yield probability (ẓann), and probability does not lead to definitive knowledge (al-ʿilm al-qaṭʿī), while fiqh is definitive knowledge—so how can it be valid that what is meant by 'evidences' are indicators and probabilistic evidences? The answer to it is that the probability here leads to definitive knowledge of the obligation to act upon the legal rulings. Thus probability may lead to knowledge of the obligation to act, even if it does not lead to certitude of the evidence's establishment. The estimation of the expression in the original is: 'that it is definitive knowledge by estimating the attribute' of the rulings, i.e., of the obligation to act (by estimating the annexation of the annex and annexed) to the legal rulings.

Then the author intended to detail the answer by choosing the second aspect, and said: 'And obtaining knowledge of the obligation to act through (i.e., by means of) probability is an exclusive characteristic of the mujtahid by consensus. As for the muqallid, his basis (his evidence) is the statement of his mujtahid (i.e., the statement definitively established from his mujtahid), not the muqallid's own probability (i.e., not his knowledge based on probability), nor the mujtahid's probability (i.e., the mujtahid's probable statement). So know the distinction between the mujtahid and the muqallid, so that you do not say like those who said: 'Just as the probable opinion of the mujtahid is obligatory to act upon for him, so also it is obligatory for the muqallid to act upon his probable opinion'; they are equal in obligatory action based on probability.'

That is, obtaining definitive knowledge of the obligation to act upon legal rulings through the knowledge obtained from probabilistic evidences is an exclusive characteristic of the mujtahid by consensus; because acting upon probability is obligatory for the mujtahid by consensus. If the mujtahid says, 'This is my probable opinion while being a mujtahid (i.e., I know it by probability in the state of being a mujtahid), and everything that is the probable opinion of a mujtahid is obligatory to act upon,' it follows that this is obligatory to act upon.

So the minor premise '(this) my probable opinion' is self-evident, and the proof of the major premise 'everything that is the probable opinion of a mujtahid is obligatory to act upon' is consensus.

As for the muqallid, his probability is not valid as evidence to be acted upon, because he is a transmitter from the mujtahid. Hence the author said: 'As for the muqallid, his basis and evidence is the statement of his mujtahid, not his own probability (i.e., not his knowledge based on probability), nor the probability of his mujtahid (i.e., not the statement of his mujtahid based on probability); for probability avails nothing against the truth, i.e., certainty is not obtained from probability, whether it is the muqallid's own probability or the probability of his mujtahid.' And since the objection is raised against the answer by choosing the second aspect, the author pointed to it with his statement: 'Yes, it follows that (fiqh) is an expression for knowledge of the obligation to act upon the legal rulings, not knowledge of them (the legal rulings). Then he pointed to the answer to it, saying: 'Except that it is said that it is a definition of fiqh by description (rasm), not a definition by essential nature (ḥadd), so definition by necessary property is permissible.' Then he pointed to another objection and said: 'And in it, there is something in it.'
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary (sharḥ) on the matn of 'Musallam al-Thubūt', where the author (al-Badakhshānī) expands on the definition of fiqh (jurisprudence). The base definition is that fiqh is knowledge of the legal rulings from their detailed evidences. The question arises: does this definition include the muqallid (a lay follower who does not derive rulings himself)? The text responds by first reinterpreting 'knowledge' as the potential faculty (malakah) in the mujtahid, not actual knowledge of all rulings. Thus a mujtahid who has the ability to derive rulings is a faqih even if he hasn't deduced every ruling. The second response redefines 'detailed evidences' as probabilistic (ẓannī) proofs; only the mujtahid can obtain knowledge from them that obliges action. The muqallid's knowledge rests on the definitive statement of his mujtahid, not on his own probabilistic reasoning. The text then addresses an objection: probabilistic proof yields only probability, whereas fiqh is definitive. The answer is that the probability leads to definitive knowledge of the obligation to act (wujūb al-ʿamal), not necessarily to certainty of the ruling's truth. The author then notes that if fiqh is defined as knowledge of obligation to act, it is a definition by necessary property (rasm), not an essential definition (ḥadd), and hence permissible. Finally, he gestures to another objection ('and in it there is something in it'), leaving room for further debate. The passage is a classic Hanafi usul discussion, using consensus (ijmāʿ) to affirm that only the mujtahid's probabilistic reasoning obliges action; the muqallid must follow the mujtahid's definitive statement.

Key Terms
مجتهد (mujtahid): A scholar who possesses the faculty (malakah) to derive shar'i rulings from detailed evidences through probabilistic reasoning.
مقلد (muqallid): A person who follows a mujtahid and whose basis (mustanad) is the definitive statement of his mujtahid, not his own probabilistic reasoning.
ظن (zann): Probabilistic preponderance (tarjīḥ) obtained from ẓannī evidences; it leads to definitive knowledge of obligation to act for the mujtahid.
علم قطعي (ʿilm qaṭʿī): Definitive knowledge that is free from doubt; in fiqh, it refers to certainty of obligation to act, not necessarily to the ruling's truth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the source (مستند) of the muqallid's knowledge?
A) His own probabilistic reasoning (ظنه)
B) The mujtahid's probabilistic opinion (ظن المجتهد)
C) The definitive statement of his mujtahid (قول مجتهده القطعي)
D) Direct evidence from Quran and Sunnah

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's religious obligation is based on the mujtahid's definitive statement, not on probabilistic evidence, which only the mujtahid can use to derive definitive obligation.

The Mujtahid-Muqallid Distinction: Binding Conjecture by Ijma
Explaining the mujtahid-muqallid distinction and the role of ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the mujtahid's conjecture (zann) is binding upon himself by ijma and why the muqallid's conjecture is not a valid basis for action.

Core Idea
The mujtahid's conjecture is binding by consensus; the muqallid's conjecture is not evidence.

This passage from 'Izalat al-Rahbut' clarifies a critical epistemological distinction in Hanafi usul: the mujtahid is obliged to act upon his own conjecture, while the muqallid is not. The author warns against the common error of equating the two, and proves the mujtahid's obligation via a syllogism grounded in ijma.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actors
Recognize that the passage contrasts the mujtahid (one who exerts effort) with the muqallid (one who follows). Each has a different relationship to conjecture (zann).
The muqallid is a transmitter (naqil), not an independent reasoner.
2
Note the common error being corrected
Some mistakenly equate the muqallid's conjecture with the mujtahid's, arguing both must act on their own conjecture. The passage explicitly refutes this.
The error stems from misunderstanding the role of ijma.
3
Analyze the syllogism for the mujtahid
The mujtahid's obligation is proven by a syllogism: minor premise ("this is my conjecture" is self-evident) and major premise ("every conjecture of a mujtahid is obligatory to act upon" is proven by ijma).
The conclusion: the mujtahid must act on his conjecture.
4
Understand the muqallid's reliance
The muqallid's basis is the statement of the mujtahid, not his own conjecture. His own zann is not a valid proof.
The passage quotes the author: "the muqallid's basis and proof is the statement of his mujtahid, not his conjecture."
5
Recognize the consensus proof
The major premise is established by ijma (consensus of scholars). This is the key evidence that differentiates the two cases.
Ijma is a qat'i proof in Hanafi usul.

الثابت بالقطع عن مجتهده لا ظنّ المقلّد أي لا علمه المبنى على الظنّ ولا ظنّ المجتهد أي القول المظنون له فاعرف الفرق بين المجتهد والمقلّد حتّى لا تقول مثل من قال كما أن مظنون المجتهد واجب العمل عليه باختيار الشق الثاني أشار إليه المصنف بقوله نعم يلزم أن يكون الفقه عبارة عن العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية لا العلم بها بالأحكام الشرعية ثمّ أشار إلى الجواب عنه فقال إلّا أن يقال إنه تعريف الفقه بالأحكام الشرعية فلا يكون هذا التعريف تعريفاً للفقه بل يكون تعريف الفقه هكذا هو العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية وتفصيل الجواب عن هذا الإشكال أن لزوم كون تعريف الفقه هو العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية ليس بممنوع يعني لو كان الفقه هو العلم بوجوب العمل بها لا بأس به إذ لازم العلم بالأحكام الشرعية وغرضه هو وجوب العمل بها فوجوب العمل بالأحكام من لوازم العلم بها وتعريف الفقه ليس بحد أي تعريفاً بذاتيات الفقه بل هو تعريف بالرسم وإنما يكون التعريف بالرسم باللازم وهذا تعريف للفقه بلازمه وهو وجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية فإذا علم المجتهد الأحكام من أدلتها وجب العمل بها عليه بعلمه ووجب العمل على بها ولكن ليس بلازم جاز التعريف به لأن اللازم على نوعين ما يكون محمولاً على الملزوم نحو الإنسان ضاحك و ما لا يكون محمولاً عليه كالكتابة والضحك فإنهما لا يحملان على الإنسان بالاشتقاق وإنما يكون التعريف باللازم المحمول لأن معرف الشيئ ما يكون محمولاً عليه بالاشتقاق أو المواطاة كالحيوان الناطق للإنسان ووجوب العلم بالأحكام الشرعية ليس بمحمول على العلم بالأحكام الشرعية فلا يصح أن يقال العلم بالأحكام الشرعية هو العلم بوجوب العمل بها
What is established with certainty from the mujtahid, not the conjecture of the muqallid—i.e., not his knowledge based on conjecture—nor the conjecture of the mujtahid, i.e., the speech that is conjectured for him. So know the difference between the mujtahid and the muqallid, so that you do not say like the one who said: 'Just as the mujtahid's conjecture is binding to act upon, likewise the muqallid is bound to act upon his conjecture, and they are equal in the obligation to act upon conjecture.' That is, acquiring definitive knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings by means of the knowledge obtained from evidentiary conjectures is a special quality of the mujtahid by consensus, because acting in accordance with conjecture is obligatory upon the mujtahid by consensus. For if the mujtahid says: 'This is my conjecture as a mujtahid (i.e., I know it by conjecture while being a mujtahid), and everything that is the conjecture of the mujtahid is obligatory to act upon,' then it follows that this is obligatory to act upon. The minor premise ('this is my conjecture') is self-evident, and the evidence for the major premise ('everything that is the conjecture of the mujtahid is obligatory to act upon') is consensus. As for the muqallid, his conjecture is not valid as a proof that must be acted upon, because he is a transmitter from the mujtahid. Hence the author said: 'As for the muqallid, his basis and proof is the statement of his mujtahid, not his conjecture—i.e., not his knowledge based on conjecture—nor the conjecture of his mujtahid, i.e., not the statement of his mujtahid based on conjecture. For conjecture does not avail anything against the truth, i.e., certainty is not obtained from conjecture, whether it is the conjecture of the muqallid himself or the conjecture of his mujtahid.' And since an objection arises against the answer by choosing the second alternative, the author pointed to it by his saying: 'Yes, it follows that fiqh is the knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings, not knowledge of them (the Shar'i rulings).' Then he pointed to the answer to it by saying: 'Except that it is said that the definition of fiqh is a rasm (description), not a hadd (definition), so definition by necessary concomitant is permissible.' Then he pointed to another objection and said: 'And in it is something.' And the detail of the objection against the answer by choosing the second alternative is that if what is meant by 'adilla' are signs and conjectural evidences, it would necessarily follow that fiqh is not knowledge of the Shar'i rulings, so this definition would not be a definition of fiqh. Rather, the definition of fiqh would be this: it is knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings. And the detail of the answer to this objection is that the necessary consequence of the definition of fiqh being 'knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings' is not forbidden; meaning, if fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act upon them, there is no problem, since the necessary concomitant of knowledge of the Shar'i rulings and its purpose is the obligation to act upon them. Thus the obligation to act upon the rulings is among the necessary concomitants of knowledge of them. And the definition of fiqh is not a hadd (i.e., a definition by the essential properties of fiqh) but rather a definition by rasm (description). And definition by rasm is only by means of a necessary concomitant. So this is a definition of fiqh by its necessary concomitant, which is the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings. So when the mujtahid knows the rulings from their evidences, acting upon them is obligatory for him by his knowledge, and acting upon the muqallid is obligatory by his (the mujtahid's) statement and his declaration of the obligation to act upon him. And the detail of the objection that the author pointed to by his saying 'And in it is something' is in two aspects: First: I do not concede that knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings is a necessary concomitant of knowledge of them, because the muqallid obtains knowledge of the rulings from the statement of the mujtahid, but he does not obtain knowledge of the obligation to act upon them merely by knowledge of the rulings. So if it were a necessary concomitant, he would also obtain knowledge of the obligation to act upon them. Second: I concede that knowledge of the obligation to act upon the rulings is a necessary concomitant of knowledge of them, but it is not a necessary concomitant that permits definition by it, because the necessary concomitant is of two types: one that is predicated on the necessary concomitant's subject, such as 'man is laughing', and one that is not predicated on it, like writing and laughing; they are not derived from man. Definition is only by the predicated necessary concomitant, because the definiens of a thing is what is predicated on it by derivation or by accordance, like 'rational animal' for man. And the obligation of knowledge of the Shar'i rulings is not predicated on knowledge of the Shar'i rulings, so it is not correct to say: 'Knowledge of the Shar'i rulings is knowledge of the obligation to act upon them.'
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from 'Izalat al-Rahbut' explaining the definition of fiqh and the crucial distinction between the mujtahid and the muqallid in obligations of acting on conjecture. The author begins by emphasizing what is established with certainty from the mujtahid, making it clear that for the muqallid, his own conjecture (zann) is not a valid basis for action — rather, he must rely on the statement of his mujtahid. This guards against a common error: mistakenly equating the muqallid's conjecture with the mujtahid's conjecture, when the mujtahid's conjecture is binding by consensus (ijma) because it is his ijtihad that he is obliged to follow. The key syllogism is presented: the mujtahid's conjecture is obligatory to act upon, and this is proven by ijma. The muqallid, being a transmitter (naqil), cannot use his own conjecture; his proof is the mujtahid's statement. Then the definition of fiqh is addressed: if fiqh were defined as knowledge of the rulings derived from conjectural evidences, it would necessitate that fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act, not just knowledge. The commentator defends this by stating that this is a definition by rasm (description) rather than hadd (essential definition), and it is permissible to define something by its necessary concomitant (lazim). Two objections follow: First, the knowledge of obligation is not necessarily concomitant to knowledge of the rulings for the muqallid. Second, even if it is, it may not be a predicated lazim suitable for definition. The commentator shows that the muqallid's knowledge of rulings comes from the mujtahid's statement, but he does not automatically know the obligation to act — this must be declared by the mujtahid. The second objection is that a lazim must be predicated (hamil) to be used in definition, but 'obligation to act' is not predicated on 'knowledge of rulings'. The discussion underscores the Hanafi position that fiqh is ultimately about obligation to act, not mere knowledge, and that definitions can be by rasm using necessary concomitants.

Key Terms
مجتهد (mujtahid): One who exerts effort to derive rulings from conjectural evidences and whose conjecture is binding upon himself by ijma.
مقلد (muqallid): One who follows the mujtahid; his own conjecture is not a proof and he must rely on the mujtahid's statement.
ظن (zann): Conjecture or predominant opinion that does not yield certainty; for the mujtahid it is binding, for the muqallid it is not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Quran
B) The Sunnah
C) Ijma (consensus)
D) Qiyas

Key Takeaway
The mujtahid's conjecture is binding by ijma; the muqallid's conjecture is not a valid proof, and he must follow the mujtahid's statement.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Defining Fiqh: Hadd vs. Rasm and the Use of Lazım
Defending the definition of fiqh as knowledge of obligation via rasm and necessary concomitant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a hadd (essential definition) and a rasm (description) and apply the Hanafi principle that fiqh can be defined by its necessary concomitant (lazım).

Core Idea
Fiqh is defined by its necessary concomitant: the obligation to act upon Shar'i rulings.

This passage continues the commentary by addressing an objection: if fiqh is knowledge from conjectural evidences, the definition becomes knowledge of obligation, not knowledge of rulings. The author defends this by explaining that the definition is a rasm (description) using a necessary concomitant (lazım). Two further objections about the nature of lazım are examined, refining the Hanafi position on definitional methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection against the definition
The objection: if fiqh is knowledge derived from conjectural evidences, then the definition would be 'knowledge of the obligation to act' instead of 'knowledge of the rulings'. That seems to change the subject.
The objection arises from the phrase 'by means of the knowledge obtained from evidentiary conjectures'.
2
Understand the defense: rasm and lazım
The author responds that this is a definition by rasm (description), not hadd (essential definition). In rasm, it is permissible to use a necessary concomitant (lazım) of the thing being defined.
The lazım here is 'obligation to act upon the rulings', which is a necessary consequence of knowing the rulings.
3
Examine the two types of lazım
The passage distinguishes between predicated (mahmūl) and non-predicated lazım. A predicated lazım can be used in definition because it can be asserted about the subject (e.g., 'man is laughing'). A non-predicated lazım (e.g., 'writing' for man) cannot be used in definition.
The examples are from logic: 'laughing' is predicated of man; 'writing' is not, even though it is a necessary property.
4
Analyze whether knowledge of obligation is a predicated lazım
The second objector concedes that obligation to act is a lazım of knowledge of rulings, but argues it is not predicated: one cannot say 'knowledge of rulings is knowledge of obligation'. The author clarifies that this objection is valid, indicating a limitation of the rasm definition.
The passage ends without fully resolving, but the Hanafi reliance on rasm is established.
5
Conclude the Hanafi position on definition
The Hanafi school accepts definition by rasm using necessary concomitants, even if not fully predicated, as long as the connection is clear. The definition of fiqh as 'knowledge of the obligation to act' is therefore a valid rasm.
This reflects Hanafi pragmatism in usul methodology.
