رسم لا حد فيجوز التعريف باللازم ثم أشار إلى اعتراض آخر وقال وفيه ما فيه وتفصيل الإشكال على الجواب باختيار الشق الثاني أنه لو كان المراد بالأدلة الأمارات والأدلة الظنية يلزم أن ألا يكون الفقه هو العلم بالأحكام الشرعية فلا يكون هذا التعريف تعريفاً للفقه بل يكون تعريف الفقه هكذا هو العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية وتفصيل الجواب عن هذا الإشكال أن لزوم كون تعريف الفقه هو العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية ليس بممنوع يعني لو كان الفقه هو العلم بوجوب العمل بها لا بأس به إذ لازم العلم بالأحكام الشرعية وغرضه هو وجوب العمل بها فوجوب العمل بالأحكام من لوازم العلم بها وتعريف الفقه ليس بحد أي تعريفاً بذاتيات الفقه بل هو تعريف بالرسم وإنما يكون التعريف بالرسم باللازم وهذا تعريف للفقه بلازمه وهو وجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية فإذا علم المجتهد الأحكام من أدلتها وجب العمل بها عليه بعلمه ووجب العمل على مقلّده بقوله واظهاره وجوب العمل عليه وتفصيل الاعتراض الذى اشار إليه المصنف بقوله وفيه ما فيه على وجهين الأول أنا لا أسلّم أنّ العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية لازم للعلم بها لأن المقلّد يحصل له العلم بالأحكام من قول المجتهد ولا يحصل له العلم بوجوب العمل بها بمجرد العلم بالأحكام فلو كان لازماً الحصل له العلم بوجوب العمل بها أيضاً والوجه الثاني أني سلمت أن العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام لازم للعلم بها ولكن ليس بلازم جاز التعريف به لأن اللازم على نوعين ما يكون محمولاً على الملزوم نحو الإنسان ضاحك و ما لا يكون محمولاً عليه كالكتابة والضحك فإنهما لا يحملان على الإنسان بالاشتقاق وإنما يكون التعريف باللازم المحمول لأن معرف الشيئ ما يكون محمولاً عليه بالاشتقاق أو المواطاة كالحيوان الناطق للإنسان ووجوب العلم بالأحكام الشرعية ليس بمحمول على العلم بالأحكام الشرعية فلا يصح أن يقال العلم بالأحكام الشرعية هو العلم بوجوب العمل بها
What is established with certainty from the mujtahid, not the conjecture of the muqallid—i.e., not his knowledge based on conjecture—nor the conjecture of the mujtahid, i.e., the speech that is conjectured for him. So know the difference between the mujtahid and the muqallid, so that you do not say like the one who said: 'Just as the mujtahid's conjecture is binding to act upon, likewise the muqallid is bound to act upon his conjecture, and they are equal in the obligation to act upon conjecture.' That is, acquiring definitive knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings by means of the knowledge obtained from evidentiary conjectures is a special quality of the mujtahid by consensus, because acting in accordance with conjecture is obligatory upon the mujtahid by consensus. For if the mujtahid says: 'This is my conjecture as a mujtahid (i.e., I know it by conjecture while being a mujtahid), and everything that is the conjecture of the mujtahid is obligatory to act upon,' then it follows that this is obligatory to act upon. The minor premise ('this is my conjecture') is self-evident, and the evidence for the major premise ('everything that is the conjecture of the mujtahid is obligatory to act upon') is consensus. As for the muqallid, his conjecture is not valid as a proof that must be acted upon, because he is a transmitter from the mujtahid. Hence the author said: 'As for the muqallid, his basis and proof is the statement of his mujtahid, not his conjecture—i.e., not his knowledge based on conjecture—nor the conjecture of his mujtahid, i.e., not the statement of his mujtahid based on conjecture. For conjecture does not avail anything against the truth, i.e., certainty is not obtained from conjecture, whether it is the conjecture of the muqallid himself or the conjecture of his mujtahid.' And since an objection arises against the answer by choosing the second alternative, the author pointed to it by his saying: 'Yes, it follows that fiqh is the knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings, not knowledge of them (the Shar'i rulings).' Then he pointed to the answer to it by saying: 'Except that it is said that the definition of fiqh is a rasm (description), not a hadd (definition), so definition by necessary concomitant is permissible.' Then he pointed to another objection and said: 'And in it is something.' And the detail of the objection against the answer by choosing the second alternative is that if what is meant by 'adilla' are signs and conjectural evidences, it would necessarily follow that fiqh is not knowledge of the Shar'i rulings, so this definition would not be a definition of fiqh. Rather, the definition of fiqh would be this: it is knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings. And the detail of the answer to this objection is that the necessary consequence of the definition of fiqh being 'knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings' is not forbidden; meaning, if fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act upon them, there is no problem, since the necessary concomitant of knowledge of the Shar'i rulings and its purpose is the obligation to act upon them. Thus the obligation to act upon the rulings is among the necessary concomitants of knowledge of them. And the definition of fiqh is not a hadd (i.e., a definition by the essential properties of fiqh) but rather a definition by rasm (description). And definition by rasm is only by means of a necessary concomitant. So this is a definition of fiqh by its necessary concomitant, which is the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings. So when the mujtahid knows the rulings from their evidences, acting upon them is obligatory for him by his knowledge, and acting upon the muqallid is obligatory by his (the mujtahid's) statement and his declaration of the obligation to act upon him. And the detail of the objection that the author pointed to by his saying 'And in it is something' is in two aspects: First: I do not concede that knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings is a necessary concomitant of knowledge of them, because the muqallid obtains knowledge of the rulings from the statement of the mujtahid, but he does not obtain knowledge of the obligation to act upon them merely by knowledge of the rulings. So if it were a necessary concomitant, he would also obtain knowledge of the obligation to act upon them. Second: I concede that knowledge of the obligation to act upon the rulings is a necessary concomitant of knowledge of them, but it is not a necessary concomitant that permits definition by it, because the necessary concomitant is of two types: one that is predicated on the necessary concomitant's subject, such as 'man is laughing', and one that is not predicated on it, like writing and laughing; they are not derived from man. Definition is only by the predicated necessary concomitant, because the definiens of a thing is what is predicated on it by derivation or by accordance, like 'rational animal' for man. And the obligation of knowledge of the Shar'i rulings is not predicated on knowledge of the Shar'i rulings, so it is not correct to say: 'Knowledge of the Shar'i rulings is knowledge of the obligation to act upon them.'
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from 'Izalat al-Rahbut' explaining the definition of fiqh and the crucial distinction between the mujtahid and the muqallid in obligations of acting on conjecture. The author begins by emphasizing what is established with certainty from the mujtahid, making it clear that for the muqallid, his own conjecture (zann) is not a valid basis for action — rather, he must rely on the statement of his mujtahid. This guards against a common error: mistakenly equating the muqallid's conjecture with the mujtahid's conjecture, when the mujtahid's conjecture is binding by consensus (ijma) because it is his ijtihad that he is obliged to follow. The key syllogism is presented: the mujtahid's conjecture is obligatory to act upon, and this is proven by ijma. The muqallid, being a transmitter (naqil), cannot use his own conjecture; his proof is the mujtahid's statement. Then the definition of fiqh is addressed: if fiqh were defined as knowledge of the rulings derived from conjectural evidences, it would necessitate that fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act, not just knowledge. The commentator defends this by stating that this is a definition by rasm (description) rather than hadd (essential definition), and it is permissible to define something by its necessary concomitant (lazim). Two objections follow: First, the knowledge of obligation is not necessarily concomitant to knowledge of the rulings for the muqallid. Second, even if it is, it may not be a predicated lazim suitable for definition. The commentator shows that the muqallid's knowledge of rulings comes from the mujtahid's statement, but he does not automatically know the obligation to act — this must be declared by the mujtahid. The second objection is that a lazim must be predicated (hamil) to be used in definition, but 'obligation to act' is not predicated on 'knowledge of rulings'. The discussion underscores the Hanafi position that fiqh is ultimately about obligation to act, not mere knowledge, and that definitions can be by rasm using necessary concomitants.

Key Terms
رسم (rasm): A definition by accidents or necessary concomitants, as opposed to hadd (essential definition).
لازم (lāzim): A necessary concomitant that is inseparable from the defined thing but not part of its essence; can be used in rasm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of fiqh when understood as a rasm?
A) Knowledge of the Shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidences.
B) Knowledge of the obligation to act upon the Shar'i rulings.
C) The practice of ijtihad.
D) The sum of all rulings.

Key Takeaway
Fiqh can be defined by its necessary concomitant (obligation to act) using rasm, which is permissible in Hanafi usul even if not a hadd.

The Hanafi Definition of Fiqh: Knowledge of Obligation to Act
Defending the definition of fiqh as knowledge of obligation to act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of fiqh as knowledge of the obligation to act (al-ilm bi wujub al-amal) and defend it against two epistemological objections from the Mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Fiqh is certain in its obligation, speculative in its source — the Hanafi definition holds both together.

How can fiqh be called 'knowledge' (ilm) when its rulings are derived from speculative evidences? This passage from al-Rahbut defends the classical Hanafi definition by redefining fiqh as knowledge of the obligation to act, not merely knowledge of rulings. We will explore two objections and the Hanafi responses, culminating in a broader understanding of 'ilm that includes speculative assent (zann).

Guided Path
1
Understand the shift in definition
The author states that because evidences are speculative, fiqh cannot be mere knowledge of rulings; it must be knowledge of the obligation to act. This is a definition by description (rasm), not essence.
Note the distinction between 'ilm bi al-ahkam and 'ilm bi wujub al-amal.
2
Analyse the first objection and response
The first objection: the obligation to act is not a necessary consequent of knowing a ruling, especially for a muqallid. The author responds that the definition uses a necessary resultant (lazim) that is adequate for rasm, even if not inseparable.
Examine the two types of lazim: predicated (like 'laughing') and non-predicated (like 'writing').
3
Analyse the second objection and responses
The second objection: fiqh is speculative, so it cannot be called 'ilm (certain knowledge). The author gives two responses: first, the obligation to act is certain (qati); second, 'ilm literally includes speculative assent (zann) in its broader usage.
The Hanafi position: 'ilm includes four types of tasdiq: yaqin, zann, jahl murakkab, taqlid.
4
Identify the epistemological crux
The real dispute is whether 'ilm is restricted to certain belief (mutakallimun) or includes speculative belief (Hanafis). The author uses linguistic usage to defend the broader definition.
Cross-reference: This is a core ikhtilaf in usul al-fiqh between Hanafis and Mutakallimun.

إزالة الرهبوت عن مشكلات مسلم الثبوت
الأمارات والأدلة الظنية يلزم أن ألا يكون الفقه هو العلم بالأحكام الشرعية، فلا يكون هذا التعريف تعريفاً للفقه، بل يكون تعريف الفقه هكذا : هو العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية.

فإذا علم المجتهد الأحكام من أدلتها، وجب العمل بها عليه بعلمه، ووجب العمل على مقلّده بقوله واظهاره وجوب العمل عليه.
-٣- وتفصيل الاعتراض الذى اشار إليه المصنف بقوله : " وفيه ما فيه على وجهين : الأول : أنا لا أسلّم أنّ العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية لازم للعلم بها؛ لأن المقلّد يحصل له العلم بالأحكام من قول المجتهد، ولا يحصل له العلم بوجوب العمل بها بمجرد العلم بالأحكام، فلو كان لازماً الحصل له العلم بوجوب العمل بها أيضاً. والوجه الثاني: أني سلمت أن العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام لازم للعلم بها، ولكن ليس بلازم جاز التعريف به؛ لأن اللازم على نوعين:

Removing the fear from the difficulties of Muslim al-Thubut. The indications and speculative evidences necessitate that fiqh should not be knowledge of legal rulings, so this definition is not a definition of fiqh; rather, the definition of fiqh is thus: it is knowledge of the obligation to act upon legal rulings. -2- Detailed answer to this problem: that the necessary consequence of the definition of fiqh being knowledge of the obligation to act upon legal rulings is not prohibited, meaning if fiqh were knowledge of the obligation to act upon them, there is no harm. For the necessary consequence of knowledge of legal rulings and its purpose is the obligation to act upon them. Thus, the obligation to act upon rulings is among the necessary consequences of knowledge of them. And the definition of fiqh is not a definition by essence (had), but rather a definition by description (rasm). Definition by description is by a necessary consequence. This is a definition of fiqh by its necessary consequence, namely the obligation to act upon legal rulings. Therefore, when a mujtahid knows the rulings from their evidences, acting upon them becomes obligatory for him by his knowledge, and acting becomes obligatory for his follower by his statement and his declaration of the obligation to act upon him. -3- Detailed objection that the author referred to with his statement: 'And in it there is what is in it' in two aspects: First: I do not concede that knowledge of the obligation to act upon legal rulings is a necessary consequence of knowledge of them; because the follower acquires knowledge of rulings from the statement of the mujtahid, and he does not acquire knowledge of the obligation to act upon them merely through knowledge of the rulings. So if it were a necessary consequence, he would have acquired knowledge of the obligation to act upon them as well. Second aspect: I concede that knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings is a necessary consequence of knowledge of them, but it is not a necessary consequence that permits definition by it; because the necessary consequence is of two types: What is predicated on the subject, like 'man is laughing'. And what is not predicated on it, like writing and laughter; they are not predicated on man by derivation. Definition is only by the necessary consequence that is predicated; because the identifier of something is what is predicated on it by derivation or by congruence, like 'rational animal' for man. And the obligation of knowledge of legal rulings is not predicated upon knowledge of legal rulings, so it is not valid to say: Knowledge of legal rulings is knowledge of the obligation to act upon them. And the author said in explanation of his statement: 'And in it there is what is in it': that knowledge of the obligation to act is a concomitant of knowledge of rulings, but not of the essence of knowledge; rather the concomitant of the essence is predicated, and the obligation to act is not part of the essence of fiqh; otherwise the definition of the knowledge of fiqh would not be complete without mentioning act. Thus, knowledge of the obligation to act, despite being a necessary consequence, is not a definer of the knowledge of fiqh. And when another problem was raised against the definition of fiqh, the author referred to it and to the answer with his statement: And from here (because what is meant by evidences are indications and speculative evidences, and fiqh is the knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings, and it is certain, you know the repelling of what was said in the objection: that fiqh is from the realm of conjectures, so how can it be knowledge? Then he pointed to the other answer) and said: Rather, 'knowledge' is used literally for something (in knowledge) that is not a concept as well. This is the second problem raised against the second definition of fiqh, and the author answered it also in two ways: -1 Detailed problem: that defining fiqh as knowledge is incorrect; because fiqh is not knowledge at all; for 'knowledge' in the terminology of the theologians is certain, fixed belief corresponding to reality and the actual state, and fiqh is not like that; for it is knowledge of speculative matters, not certain belief, so it is not knowledge; thus it is not valid to define it as knowledge. -2 Detailed first answer: that fiqh is knowledge of the obligation to act upon rulings, and it is certain, not speculative; and this definition is a definition by description as previously. -3 Detailed second answer: that 'knowledge' is used literally for concepts, and it is also used literally for what is not a concept (i.e., assent in its four types), so knowledge includes the four types of assent: namely conjecture, compound ignorance, blind following, and certainty. What is meant by 'knowledge' mentioned in the definition of fiqh is conjecture (zann) by the indication of the indications intended from the evidences.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage from al-Rahbut commentary on Muslim al-Thubut deals with the definition of fiqh (jurisprudence) and addresses two objections. The author begins by stating that if the evidences for legal rulings are merely speculative (zanni), then fiqh cannot be defined simply as 'knowledge of legal rulings' because that would imply certainty. Rather, fiqh must be defined as 'knowledge of the obligation to act upon legal rulings' (al-ilm bi wujub al-amal bi al-ahkam al-shariyyah). This is a definition by description (rasm) using a necessary concomitant (lazim), not a definition by essence (had). The first objection argues that the obligation to act is not necessarily concomitant with knowledge of the rulings, especially for a layperson (muqallid) who may know the ruling from a mujtahid but does not automatically know the obligation. The author responds that the concomitant is not from the essence but is adequate for a definition by description. The second objection is that fiqh deals with speculative matters, so it cannot be called 'ilm (certain knowledge). The author responds in two ways: first, the obligation to act is certain (qati), even if the ruling itself is speculative; second, the term 'ilm is broader in usage and includes speculative assent (zann) in the context of fiqh. This reflects a Hanafi position that allows 'ilm to include zann, contrary to the mutakallimun who restrict 'ilm to certain belief. The passage is a careful defense of the classical Hanafi definition of fiqh against epistemological objections.

Key Terms
الفقه (al-fiqh): العلم بوجوب العمل بالأحكام الشرعية (knowledge of the obligation to act upon legal rulings)
العلم (al-ilm): In this context, includes certainty and also speculative assent (zann) as a type of assent (tasdiq).
الظن (al-zann): Speculative belief that is stronger than doubt but not certain; it is considered a type of 'ilm in the definition of fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does the Hanafi definition of fiqh respond to the objection that fiqh deals with speculative matters?
A) By denying that fiqh deals with speculative matters.
B) By asserting that the obligation to act is certain, and 'ilm includes speculative assent.
C) By redefining fiqh as certain knowledge of rulings.
D) By rejecting the need for 'ilm in the definition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of fiqh as knowledge of the obligation to act (al-ilm bi wujub al-amal) resolves the tension between speculative evidences and certain knowledge by focusing on the certainty of the obligation and the broader usage of 'ilm.

Rejecting the Unification of Tasawwur and Tasdiq in Definitions
Refutation of an objection regarding definitions of sciences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical error in an objection that conflates two senses of 'knowledge' and apply the distinction between real knowledge and mental image to defend definitions of sciences.

Core Idea
Definitions of sciences do not unify tasawwur and tasdiq because knowledge as mental image avoids the identification.

This passage tackles a critical objection: if concepts serve as definitions of sciences, they would unify the two distinct mental acts of conceptualization (tasawwur) and affirmation (tasdiq). The commentator shows that this objection fails because 'knowledge' has two senses—real knowledge and mental image. Understanding this distinction is essential for clarifying how definitions function in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that if concepts are definitions (hudud) of sciences, then this would necessitate the unification of tasawwur and tasdiq, which is invalid. Therefore, concepts cannot be definitions.
This is the argument that the commentator sets out to refute.
2
Understand the two meanings of 'knowledge'
The passage considers two interpretations of the defined 'knowledge' (al-‘ilm): either it refers to the problems (masa'il) themselves or to the affirmation (tasdiq) of them. On each interpretation, the objection appears to hold—the definition (tasawwur) becomes identical with the defined.
The objector assumes a single meaning of knowledge, which leads to conflation.
3
Recognize the commentator's key move
The commentator points to a phrase 'so reflect' (fatafakkar) and introduces a distinction: 'knowledge' can also mean the mental image (al-surah al-‘ilmiyyah), as stated in al-Sullam. In this sense, knowledge is unified with the known without being real knowledge.
This distinction is crucial: definition uses knowledge as mental image, not as real disclosure.
4
Apply the distinction to refute the objection
With the mental-image sense, the definition (concept) remains distinct from the defined (affirmation). No unification occurs, so the objection fails, and concepts can still be definitions of sciences.
The possibility of definitions is preserved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : نعم يلزم اتحاد التصور والتصديق الخ: لما لم يصح الاعتراض السابق (لزوم تعدد الذاتي عند المصنف جاء باعتراض آخر ( على تقدير كون تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم ) أي إذا كانت تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم المدوّنة يلزم اتحاد التصور والتصديق وهو باطل، فكونها حدوداً أيضاً باطل، فقال المصنف في دليل بطلان اللازم (اتحاد التصور والتصديق مع أنهما نوعان (متباثنان) تحقيقا أي حقيقة، وأشار إلى بيان اللزوم بقوله (فتفكر). وحاصل دليل اللزوم أنّ العلم المعرف إما عبارة عن نفس المسائل، أو عن التصديق بها، فعلى التقدير الثاني (كون العلم عبارة عن التصديق بالمسائل) لزوم الاتحاد بين التصور والتصديق ظاهر، لأنّ الحدّ عبارة عن تصور المحدود، والمحدود هو العلم الذى هو نفس التصديق، فلزم اتحاد التصور (الحد) واتحاد التصديق (المحدود) وعلى تقدير الأول (كون العلم نفس المسائل) يكون الحد متحداً مع العلم، وهو نفس المسائل، والمسائل متحدّة مع الادراكات المتعلقة بها؛ لأجل ضرورة الاتحاد بين العلم والمعلوم، فصار الحد متحداً مع التصديق المتعلّق بالمسائل، فلزم اتحاد التصور والتصديق على التقدير الأول أيضاً. ويمكن أن يكون في قوله ( فتفكر) إشارة إلى اعتراض، وهو أن العلم في صورة اتحاد العلم والمعلوم يكون بمعنى الصورة العلمية، لا بمعناه الحقيقى (ما به الانكشاف لأنه (المصنف) قال في السّلّم : " إن العلم في مسألة الاتحاد بمعنى الصورة العلمية فالعلم بمعنى نفس المسائل أو التصديق بها لا يكون متحدّاً مع المعلوم، بل العلم بمعنى الصورة العلمية يكون متحداً مع المعلوم، ففي صورة كون تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم لا يلزم الاتحاد بين العلم والمعلوم، ولا بين التصور والتصديق، فبقى الجواز والإمكان تادا. (إمكان كون تلك المفهومات حدوداً للعلوم)
His statement: 'Yes, the unification of tasawwur and tasdiq is necessary...' when the previous objection (the multiplicity of essential attribute according to the author) was not valid, another objection came on the assumption that those concepts are definitions of the sciences. That is, if those concepts are definitions of the codified sciences, it would necessitate the unification of tasawwur and tasdiq, which is false; thus their being definitions is also false. So the author said in proof of the falsehood of the necessary consequent: 'The unification of tasawwur and tasdiq despite them being two completely distinct types in reality.' And he pointed to the explanation of the necessary entailment by saying: 'So reflect.' The summary of the proof of entailment is that the defined knowledge is either an expression of the problems themselves, or of the affirmation of them. On the second assumption (knowledge being the affirmation of the problems), the unification between tasawwur and tasdiq is apparent, because the definition is a conceptualization of the defined, and the defined is knowledge, which is itself affirmation, so the unification of tasawwur (the definition) and the unification of tasdiq (the defined) is necessitated. On the first assumption (knowledge being the problems themselves), the definition becomes one with knowledge, which is the problems themselves, and the problems are one with the perceptions related to them due to the necessity of unification between knowledge and the known; thus the definition becomes one with the affirmation related to the problems, so unification of tasawwur and tasdiq is necessitated on the first assumption as well. And it is possible that in his saying 'so reflect' there is an allusion to an objection: that knowledge in the case of unification of knowledge and the known is in the sense of the mental image, not in its real meaning (that by which disclosure occurs). Because the author said in al-Sullam: 'Knowledge in the issue of unification is in the sense of the mental image.' So knowledge in the sense of the problems themselves or affirmation of them is not unified with the known; rather knowledge in the sense of the mental image is unified with the known. Therefore, in the case where those concepts are definitions of the sciences, there is no necessary unification between knowledge and the known, nor between tasawwur and tasdiq, so permissibility and possibility remain. (The possibility that those concepts are definitions of the sciences).
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the matn, addressing an objection about the definitions of sciences. The author explains that if concepts (mufahim) were definitions (hudud) of the sciences, it would lead to the unacceptable unification of two distinct mental acts: tasawwur (conceptualization) and tasdiq (affirmation). The proof works on two interpretations of 'knowledge': either the set of propositions (masa'il) themselves or the tasdiq of them. In both cases, the definition (which is a tasawwur) becomes identified with the defined (which involves tasdiq), causing the two types to merge. However, the commentator points out that the author of al-Sullam distinguishes between real knowledge and the mental image (al-surah al-'ilmiyyah). In the sense of mental image, unification is possible without conflating tasawwur and tasdiq. Thus, the objection fails, and it remains possible for concepts to serve as definitions of sciences.

Key Terms
تصور (tasawwur): Conceptualization, the mental act of forming a concept without judgment.
تصديق (tasdiq): Affirmation, the mental act of assenting to a proposition as true or false.
الصورة العلمية (al-surah al-'ilmiyyah): The mental image; the cognitive representation of an object without the connotation of real disclosure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the argument, why would defining knowledge as tasdiq lead to unification of tasawwur and tasdiq?
A) Because the definition is a tasawwur and the defined is tasdiq, making them identical.
B) Because tasawwur and tasdiq are naturally united in all definitions.
C) Because the author of al-Sullam proved they are the same.
D) Because the problems themselves are both conceptualization and affirmation.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between real knowledge and mental image preserves the possibility that concepts can serve as definitions of sciences without conflating tasawwur and tasdiq.

Definition and Ruling of Al-Nazar (Speculative Reasoning)
Defines al-nazar and proves its obligation via Quran and usul principle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-nazar in Hanafi usul terminology, state its obligatory ruling, and identify the Quranic proof and usul principle used to derive that obligation.

Core Idea
Al-nazar is wajib because it is the necessary means to attain iman, and the means to an obligatory act is itself obligatory.

This lesson introduces the foundational concept of al-nazar (speculative reasoning) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author defines nazar technically and then establishes its legal ruling as obligatory (wajib). Understanding this principle is crucial because it forms the basis for all subsequent reasoning in deriving Islamic rulings. The passage also demonstrates how usul scholars use Quranic verses and logical principles to ground epistemological obligations.

Guided Path
1
Parse the definition
Identify the three key elements: ترتيب (arrangement), المعقول (intelligibles), and تحصل المجهول (acquiring the unknown).
2
Distinguish intelligible types
Differentiate primary and secondary intelligibles with examples from the text (Zayd, human, particular, universal).
3
Locate the ruling
Note that the ruling of nazar is wajib, stated explicitly.
4
Trace the dalil
Identify the two Quranic verses: one implying iman is wajib, the other commanding nazar.
5
Apply the usul principle
Understand that the prerequisite for an obligatory act (iman) is itself obligatory, making nazar wajib.
The principle is: muqaddimat al-wajib wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبدأ الأوّل: تعريف النظر وحكمه : وهو في اللغة إما التوجه إلى الشيئ بعين الوجه ، وإمّا التوجه إليه بعين القلب، وهذا هو المراد هنا وفي الاصطلاح :) هو ترتيب المعقول (المعلومات التصورية أو التصديقة التي تكون من المعقولات الثانية) لتحصيل المجهول (أي لتحصيل التصور المجهول أو التصديق المجهول) واللام في (المعقول للجنس فيشمل القليل والكثير ، وإنما يتحقق الترتيب في الاثنين فصاعداً، فيراد بالمعقول الكثير، وكذلك اللام في (المجهول) للجنس، فإنّ المجهول الذى يُحصل قد يكون قليلاً وقد يكون كثيراً، فيشمل المجهول التصوري والتصديقي كليهما. واعلم أن المعقول على قسمين : أولي : وهو ما حصل في العقل في المرتبة الأولى، كزيد والإنسان . وثانوي، وهو ما حصل في العقل في المرتبة الثانية ككون زيد جزئياً، وكون الإنسان كليّاً، فانّ الإنسان وزيداً يتصوران أولاً، وكون زيد جزئياً يتصور ثانياً، وكذلك مفهوم الإنسان يدخل في الذهن ثانياً و يكون كلياً. واختار لفظ المفرد (المعقول) على الجمع (المعقولات الرعاية سجع (المجهول) فيكون معنى الكلام : أنّ النظر هو ترتيب المعلومات التصورية لتحصيل المجهول التصوري وترتيب المعلومات التصديقية لتحصيل المجهول التصديقي كترتيب الحيوان والناطق لتحصيل الإنسان، وترتيب: العالم متغير، وكلّ متغيّر حادث لتحصيل أن العالم حادث حكم النظر في الأدلة : وحكمه أنه واجب؛ لأنه مقدمة الواجب، أي مايتوقف عليه أداء الواجب وهو الايمان بالله تعالى وصفاته وأفعاله وأحكامه ومعجزات رسوله، قال تعالى: (ومن لم يؤمن بالله ورسوله فانا اعتدنا للكافرين سعيراً) وقوله تعالى : ( فانظروا إلى آثار رحمة الله كيف يحيى الأرض بعد موتها ) ومن المسلّم أن مقدمة الواجب واجبة، فثبت أن النظر واجب .
The first principle: Definition of al-nazar and its ruling. Linguistically, it is either turning one's face toward something or turning one's heart toward it, and this is what is intended here. In terminology: it is the arrangement of the intelligible (the conceptual or assertoric information that are secondary intelligibles) to acquire the unknown (i.e., to acquire the unknown concept or unknown assertion). The lam in 'al-ma'qul' is for the genus, so it includes the few and the many, but arrangement is only realized in two or more, so by 'al-ma'qul' the many is intended. Similarly, the lam in 'al-majhul' is for the genus, for the unknown to be acquired may be little or much, so it includes both the conceptual unknown and the assertoric unknown. Know that the intelligible is of two types: primary: that which occurs in the mind in the first order, like Zayd and human. Secondary: that which occurs in the mind in the second order, like Zayd being particular and human being universal, for human and Zayd are conceptualized first, and Zayd being particular is conceptualized second. Likewise, the concept of human enters the mind secondarily and is universal. He chose the singular form (al-ma'qul) over the plural (al-ma'qulat) to match the rhyme with 'al-majhul'. So the meaning of the statement is: al-nazar is the arrangement of conceptual information to acquire the unknown concept, and the arrangement of assertoric information to acquire the unknown assertion, like arranging animal and rational to acquire human, and arranging 'the world is changing, and everything changing is originated' to acquire that the world is originated. The ruling of al-nazar regarding proofs: its ruling is that it is obligatory (wajib), because it is a prerequisite for the obligatory, i.e., that upon which the performance of the obligatory depends, which is belief in Allah, His attributes, His actions, His rulings, and the miracles of His messenger. Allah said: 'And whoever does not believe in Allah and His Messenger, then indeed We have prepared for the disbelievers a blaze.' And His saying: 'So look at the effects of Allah's mercy, how He revives the earth after its death.' And it is established that a prerequisite for the obligatory is obligatory, so it is established that al-nazar is obligatory.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents the first necessary logical principle: the definition of al-nazar (speculative reasoning/contemplation) and its ruling. The author defines nazar linguistically and technically. Technically, it is the arrangement of intelligible information (al-ma'qul) to reach the unknown (al-majhul). He then explains that 'al-ma'qul' is in the singular to encompass both little and much, but since arrangement requires at least two, it actually refers to the many. He distinguishes between primary intelligibles (first-order concepts like 'Zayd') and secondary intelligibles (second-order concepts like 'particular' or 'universal'). The author clarifies that the definition covers both conceptual and assertoric reasoning, giving examples: to obtain the concept of 'human', you arrange 'animal' and 'rational'; to obtain the assertion 'the world is originated', you arrange 'the world is changing' and 'everything changing is originated'. The ruling: nazar is wajib (obligatory) because it is a prerequisite for iman (faith), which is wajib. He cites Quranic verses as proof: the threat for disbelief and the command to observe God's mercy. The principle that 'whatever is prerequisite for the obligatory is itself obligatory' is applied. Thus, nazar is obligatory for every accountable person. This is a foundational principle in Hanafi usul: the obligation of rational inquiry to attain faith.

Key Terms
النظر (al-naẓar): The arrangement of intelligible information to acquire the unknown (concept or assertion).
المعقول الأولي (al-maʿqūl al-awwalī): Primary intelligible: concepts that occur in the mind first, like individual names and universal genera.
المعقول الثانوي (al-maʿqūl al-thānawī): Secondary intelligible: concepts that occur in the mind second, such as 'particular' or 'universal'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling of al-nazar (speculative reasoning) and why?
A) Mandub, because it is a means to increase knowledge.
B) Wajib, because it is a prerequisite for iman which is obligatory.
C) Mubah, because it is a neutral intellectual exercise.
D) Haram, because it leads to doubts.

Key Takeaway
Al-nazar is wajib because it is the necessary prerequisite for the obligatory act of iman, established by Quranic command and the usul principle that the means to an obligatory is itself obligatory.

Definition and Ruling of Speculative Reasoning (Nazar)
Defining speculative reasoning and establishing its obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define speculative reasoning (nazar) as understood in Hanafi usul and apply the principle of muqaddimat al-wajib to establish its obligation.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning is wajib because it is a necessary precursor to faith, and the requisite of an obligation is itself obligatory.

This passage from a foundational Hanafi usul text defines a key term—nazar, or speculative reasoning—and establishes its legal status. Understanding this concept is fundamental because it explains how Muslims are required to arrive at knowledge of God and His rulings. The author first clarifies the types of intelligibles, then defines nazar as the arrangement of known propositions to reach an unknown conclusion. Finally, he argues that nazar is obligatory using the well-known usul principle that the prerequisite of an obligatory act is itself obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish primary and secondary intelligibles
Identify the two types of al-ma'qool: primary (first-order concepts like Zayd) and secondary (second-order concepts like particularity). Understand that this distinction underlies the definition of nazar.
The choice of singular over plural is for rhetorical effect.
2
Define speculative reasoning (nazar)
According to the text, nazar is the arrangement of known tasawwuri (conceptual) or tasdiqi (affirmational) propositions to obtain an unknown. Work through the examples: animal + rational → human (conceptual); the universe is changing + everything changing is originated → the universe is originated (affirmational).
3
Identify the legal ruling of nazar
The text explicitly states that nazar is wajib (obligatory). Link this to the broader duty of faith (iman), which is also wajib.
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Look at the two verses: the threat for disbelief (Quran 48:13) and the command to look at God's signs (Quran 30:50). How do these support the obligation? Note that the threat indicates the seriousness of faith, and the command implies that nazar is a means to it.
The thubut is qati (Quran), but the dalala is zanni (indirect).
5
Apply the usul principle of muqaddimat al-wajib
Since faith is obligatory, and nazar is a prerequisite for faith (it is the means to acquire knowledge of God), the principle that 'the prerequisite of an obligatory act is obligatory' makes nazar wajib.
This principle is accepted by all schools but applied here specifically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المعقول على قسمين :
أولي : وهو ما حصل في العقل في المرتبة الأولى، كزيد والإنسان .
وثانوي، وهو ما حصل في العقل في المرتبة الثانية ككون زيد جزئياً، وكون الإنسان كليّاً، فانّ الإنسان وزيداً يتصوران أولاً، وكون زيد جزئياً يتصور ثانياً،
وكذلك مفهوم الإنسان يدخل في الذهن ثانياً و يكون كلياً.
واختار لفظ المفرد (المعقول) على الجمع (المعقولات الرعاية سجع (المجهول) فيكون معنى الكلام : أنّ النظر هو ترتيب المعلومات التصورية لتحصيل المجهول التصوري
وترتيب المعلومات التصديقية لتحصيل المجهول التصديقي
كترتيب الحيوان والناطق لتحصيل الإنسان، وترتيب: العالم متغير، وكلّ متغيّر
حادث لتحصيل أن العالم حادث
حكم النظر في الأدلة : وحكمه أنه واجب؛ لأنه مقدمة الواجب، أي مايتوقف عليه أداء الواجب وهو الايمان بالله تعالى وصفاته وأفعاله وأحكامه ومعجزات رسوله، قال تعالى: (ومن لم يؤمن بالله ورسوله فانا اعتدنا للكافرين سعيراً) وقوله تعالى : ( فانظروا إلى آثار رحمة الله كيف يحيى الأرض بعد موتها ))
ومن المسلّم أن مقدمة الواجب واجبة، فثبت أن النظر واجب .
Know that the intelligible is of two divisions: Primary, which is what occurs in the mind in the first order, like Zayd and 'human'. Secondary, which is what occurs in the mind in the second order, like Zayd being particular and 'human' being universal. Indeed, 'human' and Zayd are conceived first, and Zayd being particular is conceived secondarily. Similarly, the concept of 'human' enters the mind secondarily and becomes universal. And he chose the singular word (al-ma'qool) over the plural (al-ma'qoolat) for the sake of rhyme with (al-majhool). So the meaning of the statement is: Speculative reasoning (nazar) is the arrangement of conceptual knowns to obtain the conceptual unknown, and the arrangement of affirmational knowns to obtain the affirmational unknown, like arranging 'animal' and 'rational' to obtain 'human', and arranging 'the universe is changing' and 'everything changing is originated' to obtain that the universe is originated. The ruling of speculative reasoning concerning evidences: its ruling is that it is obligatory, because it is a prerequisite for the obligatory, i.e., that on which the performance of the obligatory depends, namely faith in Allah Most High, His attributes, His actions, His rulings, and the miracles of His messenger. He said: 'And whoever does not believe in Allah and His messenger, indeed We have prepared for the disbelievers a blazing fire' (Quran 48:13); and He said: 'So look at the effects of the mercy of Allah: how He revives the earth after its death' (Quran 30:50). And it is well-known that the prerequisite of the obligatory is obligatory. So it is established that speculative reasoning is obligatory.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines speculative reasoning (nazar) and establishes its obligatory nature. The author first distinguishes two types of intelligibles: primary (like individual concepts) and secondary (like universality). He then defines nazar as the ordering of known propositions—whether conceptual (tasawwuri) or affirmational (tasdiqi)—to arrive at an unknown conclusion. Two examples are given: from animal and rational to human (conceptual), and from change in the universe and the principle that everything changing is originated to the conclusion that the universe is originated (affirmational). The ruling is that nazar is wajib (obligatory) because it is a muqaddimah al-wajib (prerequisite for the obligatory), meaning it is necessary for acquiring faith, which is obligatory. The author supports this with two Quranic verses and the usul principle that the prerequisite of an obligatory act is itself obligatory. No madhhab dispute is present; this is a foundational rule in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
المعقول (al-ma'qool): That which is intellected; divided into primary (first-order conception) and secondary (second-order conception like universality).
النظر (al-nazar): Speculative reasoning: the arrangement of known conceptual or affirmational propositions to obtain an unknown.
مقدمة الواجب (muqaddimat al-wajib): The prerequisite of an obligatory act; whatever the fulfillment of an obligation depends upon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is directly commanded in the Quran without any intermediary.
B) Because it is a prerequisite for faith, and the prerequisite of an obligatory act is obligatory.
C) Because it is a supererogatory act of worship that earns great reward.
D) Because it is necessary for resolving legal disputes.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, speculative reasoning (nazar) is wajib because it is the essential means to attain faith, and the prerequisite of an obligation is obligatory.

Two Definitions of al-Dhātī and the Refutation of the Second
Two definitions of essential and refutation of the second.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two definitions of al-dhātī and explain why the second definition is refuted by the attribute of possibility.

Core Idea
An essential property is part of the essence's understanding; a causeless property may still be accidental.

This passage from the commentary on Muslim al-Thubūt clarifies the logical term 'al-dhātī' (essential), foundational for defining key concepts in usul al-fiqh. Two definitions are presented: the first ties the essential to understanding the essence, the second ties it to being causeless. The author refutes the second definition by showing that possibility (imkān) is causeless yet accidental, not essential. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to evaluate real definitions.

Guided Path
1
State the two definitions
Read the first definition: dhātī is that whose understanding is included in understanding the essence. The second: dhātī is that which has no cause for its existence in the essence.
These definitions come from logic, but are used in usul when defining terms like hukm, mukallaf, etc.
2
Identify the key difference
The first focuses on conceptual inclusion (intension), the second on causal independence (no cause needed). Note that the first is accepted; the second is criticized.
The author indicates weakness of the second by saying 'Nukida' (refuted).
3
Understand the refutation using possibility
Possibility (imkān) is an attribute of all possible beings (mumkin). It exists without a cause—the essence of the possible itself suffices. Yet possibility is accidental, not essential. Thus, the second definition wrongly includes an accidental property.
The author's proof: 'lā imkāna bil-ghayr' means possibility is not caused by another.
4
Grasp the deeper argument (second proof)
If possibility were caused, then when the cause is absent or ineffective, the possible being would lose possibility, becoming necessary or impossible—an absurd overturning of realities. This confirms that possibility is uncaused yet accidental.
This is an indirect proof showing the absurdity of the second definition's implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذاتي: ١- ما يكون فهمه داخلاً في فهم الذات، ٢- وقيل هو ما لا يعلّل ما لا يذكر لثبوته للذات علّة ونُقِضَ (التعريف الثاني بصفة) الإمكان؛ فإنه يصدق عليه أنه ثابت للمكن من غير علّة، إذ لا إمكان بالغير أي لا يكون وجود صفة الإمكان في الممكن لأجل أن صفة الإمكان ليس بذاتى للمكن، بل من عوارضه غيره مع فدخل العرضى في تعريف الذاتى. وبعد ما قال المصنف: "هو التعريف حقيقي إن كان بالذاتيات" أراد أن يعرف الذاتى فقال: والذاتى الخ. ثم ذكر للذاتي تعريفين: وأشار إلى ضعف الثاني بقوله: "ونقض بالإمكان". فحاصل التعريف الأول: أن الذاتي هو الذى يستلزم فهم ذات الشئ فهمه، بحيث تدل الذات نفسها على الذاتى، فكلّما عُلِمت ذات الشئ عُلم ذاتيه. وحاصل التعريف الثاني: أن الذاتي هو ما لا يحتاج في ثبوته ووجوده إلى علة، بل تكفي نفس الذات للدلالة عليه. ثم ذكر النقض الوارد على التعريف الثاني وقال: "ونقض بصفة الإمكان"؛ لأنها ثابتة لذات الممكن من غير الاحتياج إلى علّة، مع أنها عرضى لها، وليس بذاتى للمكن؛ إذ لا يثبت صفة الإمكان بدليل آخر غير اقتضاء الذات اياه، نعم، إلا أن يقال: إن اقتضاء الذات علة لثبوت الإمكان، ولكن يَرُدُّ هذا الاقتضاء الدليل الثاني الآتى. وهذا نقض إجمالي سنده (دليله) قوله: "اذ لا إمكان بالغير"، والدليل الثاني أن الإمكان لوكان لأجل العلة، فيلزم عند عدم تاثير العلة أو عدمها أن لا يكون الممكن ممكناً لذاته، بل إمّا أن يكون واجباً، وإما أن يكون ممتنعاً، وهذا قلب الحقائق وهو محال؛ لأن كل مفهوم يتصوره العقل لا يخلو عن الأحوال الثلاث الإمكان أو الوجوب أو الامتناع، فالممكن باعتبار الذات يحتمل إحدى هذه الثلاث، فإذا لم يبق ممكناً باعتبار الذات، فلا يخلو عن الاثنين الباقيين الواجب أو الممتنع.
And the essential (al-dhātī): 1- It is that whose understanding is included in the understanding of the essence. 2- And it was said: it is that which is not caused—that no cause is mentioned for its existence in the essence. And the second definition was refuted by the attribute of possibility (al-imkān); for it is true that it is established for the possible being without a cause, for there is no possibility by another—that is, the existence of the attribute of possibility in the possible being is not because the attribute of possibility is essential for the possible being, but rather it is among its accidents, so the accidental entered into the definition of the essential. After the author said: "It (the definition) is real if it is by the essential properties," he intended to define the essential, and said: "And the essential..." Then he mentioned two definitions for the essential, and indicated the weakness of the second by his saying: "And it was refuted by possibility." The summary of the first definition: that the essential is that which understanding of the essence of a thing entails understanding it, such that the essence itself indicates the essential, so whenever the essence of a thing is known, its essential is known. The summary of the second definition: that the essential is that which does not need a cause for its existence and being, but rather the essence itself suffices to indicate it. Then he mentioned the refutation upon the second definition and said: "And it was refuted by the attribute of possibility"; because it is established for the essence of the possible being without needing a cause, despite it being an accident for it, and not essential for the possible being; since the attribute of possibility is not established by another proof other than the essence's own exigency. Yes, except if it is said that the exigency of the essence is a cause for the existence of possibility, but this exigency is rebutted by the second proof that follows. And this is an overall refutation whose proof is his statement: "for there is no possibility by another." And the second proof: that if possibility were because of a cause, then when the cause is ineffective or absent, it would follow that the possible being is not possible by itself, but rather either necessary or impossible, and this is an overturning of realities, which is impossible; because every concept that the mind conceives is not free from the three states: possibility, necessity, or impossibility. So the possible being, by virtue of its essence, admits one of these three. If it no longer remains possible by virtue of its essence, it is not free from the remaining two: the necessary or the impossible.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage deals with the definition of the essential (dhātī) in logic and usul al-fiqh. Two definitions are given. The first defines dhātī as a property whose understanding is included in understanding the essence itself; e.g., 'animal' is essential for 'human' because understanding human includes understanding animal. The second defines it as that which does not require an external cause for its existence in the essence; the essence itself suffices. The author then refutes the second definition using the example of 'possibility' (imkān). Possibility is an attribute that belongs to a possible being (mumkin) without an external cause, yet it is not essential but accidental. If the second definition were true, possibility would be essential, which is false. Further, if possibility were caused, then when the cause is absent, the possible being would become either necessary or impossible—an absurdity. This refutation is important in Hanafi usul because definitions of legal concepts often rely on essential vs. accidental distinctions, e.g., in defining 'mukallaf' or 'hukm'.

Key Terms
الذاتي (al-dhātī): An essential property that is understood when the essence is understood, and does not depend on an external cause.
الإمكان (al-imkān): The attribute of a possible being (mumkin) that it is neither necessary nor impossible; it is an accident, not essential.
العرضى (al-‘araḍī): An accidental property that does not stem from the essence but is acquired externally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the attribute of possibility (imkān) used to refute the second definition of the essential?
A) Because possibility is a cause for other accidents
B) Because possibility is established without a cause yet is an accident, not an essential
C) Because possibility is essential for possible beings
D) Because possibility requires an external cause

Key Takeaway
An essential property is defined by conceptual inclusion in the essence, not by lack of external cause; possibility is a clear counterexample.

The Three Meanings of Husn and Qubh
Defining the three meanings of husn and qubh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three meanings of husn and qubh and distinguish the contested meaning from the agreed ones.

Core Idea
The dispute on good and evil is only about the third meaning: deserving reward or punishment.

In Usul al-Fiqh, the terms 'good' (husn) and 'evil' (qubh) are used in multiple senses. This passage, from the commentary 'Izalat al-Rahbūt', clarifies that not all meanings are disputed. Only the third meaning—whether an action inherently deserves praise/reward or blame/punishment—is the point of contention among theological schools. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for grasping how the Hanafi school approaches moral reasoning and the role of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings
Carefully read the passage to extract the three meanings of husn and qubh as listed: (1) attribute of perfection vs. deficiency, (2) compatibility with worldly purpose vs. opposition, (3) deserving praise/reward vs. blame/punishment.
These three are clearly marked in the Arabic text with numbers.
2
Recognize the agreed meanings
The passage states 'There is no dispute' (lā nizā‘) regarding the first two meanings. Confirm that no school denies these aspects.
The first meaning is often called 'ontological goodness', the second 'utilitarian goodness'.
3
Locate the contested meaning
The third meaning is introduced with 'but rather the dispute only occurs when…'. This is the point of ikhtilaf.
This meaning relates to moral accountability (istihqāq al-madḥ wa al-dhamm).
4
Understand the significance
Reflect on why this third meaning is theologically loaded: it touches on whether moral values exist independently of revelation or are purely divine constructs. This connects to the nature of the Lawgiver (al-Ḥākim).
The passage begins by affirming that the only Lawgiver is Allah, which frames the debate.

Removing Fear from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut
103
From the First Chapter: On the Lawgiver
There is no ruling except from Allah Almighty: i.e., no ruling for the worship of the Lord, belief in His attributes and names, complying with His commands, avoiding His prohibitions, acting by His law, obeying His religion, and refraining from worshipping others or associating partners with Him, except by Allah Almighty through His messengers and prophets, because He is the Creator of everything and its Disposer. So His ruling is considered, not the ruling of anyone else, in legal and cosmic matters, due to His saying: 'The ruling is not except for Allah; He has commanded that you not worship except Him,' and His saying: 'Indeed, His is the creation and the command.' Thus, the Lawgiver over creatures is the Creator, not others.
There is no dispute that an act is good and evil by reason (1) in the sense of the attribute of perfection and deficiency, (2) or in the sense of compatibility with worldly purpose and its opposition, (3) but rather in the sense of deserving His praise and reward and their opposites. (He said:) There is no dispute that the act of the obligated person is good (it may be good) and evil (it may be evil) by reason in the sense of the attribute of perfection and deficiency, (2) or in the sense of compatibility with worldly purpose and its opposition, (3) but rather the dispute only occurs when the good is in the sense of deserving His praise in this world and His reward in the afterlife, and evil is in the sense of deserving His blame and punishment in both; this is the meaning of his statement: and their opposites.
The purpose of this phrase is to explain three things:
1. Explanation of the three meanings of good and evil.
2. Identification of the contested meaning.
3. Explanation of the difference between the Maturidis, Ash'aris, and Mu'tazilites.

104
So he indicated the three meanings by his statement: 'There is no dispute,' and he indicated the identification of the contested meaning by his statement: 'Rather, the dispute only occurs...' and he indicated the difference among the three groups by his statement:
'For the Ash'aris, it is revealed' i.e., only by His decree. Whatever He commanded is good, and whatever He forbade is evil, and if the command were reversed, the matter would be reversed. And according to us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazilites, it is rational, i.e., its knowledge does not depend on revelation.
'For the Ash'aris, it is revealed' i.e., only by His decree, i.e., good in the sense of deserving praise and reward is by the decree of the Lawgiver alone; similarly, evil in the sense of deserving blame and punishment is revealed by the decree of the Lawgiver alone. And he said in explaining the decree of the Lawgiver: Whatever He commands is good, and whatever He forbids is evil. If the command were reversed by prohibiting what was commanded through abrogation and commanding what was forbidden through abrogation, the matter would be reversed (so that the previously commanded becomes evil and the previously forbidden becomes good). (2) And according to us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazilites, good is rational, and similarly evil, i.e., the perception of good and evil does not depend on revelation (as the Ash'aris say), but rather good and evil are perceived even before the arrival of revelation.
Explanation of the difference between us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazilites
After agreeing that both are rational.
But according to us, it does not entail a ruling upon the servant; rather, it becomes a cause for deserving the ruling from the Wise One who does not prefer the inferior. So as long as He has not ruled, there is no ruling. And from here we have stipulated the reaching of the call in obligation.
'But according to us, rational good or evil does not entail a ruling upon the servant (before the arrival of revelation),' rather, good or evil becomes a cause for deserving the ruling and deserving its necessity by command or prohibition from the Wise Lawgiver who does not prefer the inferior and does not rule by it. So as long as the Wise Lawgiver has not ruled (after perceiving good or evil rationally), there is no ruling (obligatory by mere reason).
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary 'Izalat al-Rahbūt' on the usul work 'Muslim al-Thubūt'. It begins by establishing that the ultimate Lawgiver (al-Hākim) is Allah alone, as per Quranic proofs. Then it delves into the philosophical-theological debate about the moral value of actions: husn (goodness) and qubh (evil).

The author identifies three meanings of good and evil: (1) as attributes of perfection vs. deficiency, (2) as conducive vs. repugnant to worldly aims, and (3) as deserving praise/reward vs. blame/punishment. The first two are universally agreed upon; the dispute is only about the third meaning.

The passage then outlines the positions of the three major theological schools: Ash'aris hold that an action's moral value depends solely on divine command (it is good because Allah commanded it). Maturidis (the Hanafi theological school) and Mu'tazilites both hold that good and evil are discernible by reason independently of revelation. However, they differ on the consequences: Mu'tazilites argue that rational good and evil impose obligations on humans even before revelation, whereas Maturidis maintain that although reason can perceive good and evil, it does not create a binding obligation until the Lawgiver issues a command or prohibition. This is a key difference between the Maturidi and Mu'tazili positions, despite their agreement that moral values are rational.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-ḥusn): Goodness, in three senses: (1) perfection, (2) beneficial for worldly purpose, (3) deserving praise and reward.
القبيح (al-qubḥ): Evil, in three senses: (1) deficiency, (2) harmful to worldly purpose, (3) deserving blame and punishment.
الحاكم (al-ḥākim): The Lawgiver, who is Allah alone; His ruling is the source of legal obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which meaning of husn and qubh is agreed upon by all schools?
A) That actions deserve praise or blame
B) That actions are perfect or deficient
C) That actions are compatible or incompatible with worldly purpose
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Only the third meaning of husn and qubh—deserving praise/reward or blame/punishment—is contested among schools; the first two meanings are universally accepted.

The Dispute on Rational Good and Evil: Ash'ari, Maturidi, and Mu'tazili
Comparing the three schools on rational good and evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the positions of the Ash'aris, Maturidis, and Mu'tazilites on the third meaning of husn and qubh, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Maturidis and Mu'tazilites.

Core Idea
Reason perceives good and evil, but only revelation creates obligation – the Maturidi middle path.

Building on the three meanings of husn and qubh, this passage focuses on the disputed third meaning. It contrasts the Ash'ari view that moral value is purely determined by divine command with the common rationalist view of Maturidis and Mu'tazilites. However, it also distinguishes the Maturidi (Hanafi) position from the Mu'tazili: while both affirm rational perception of good and evil, only the Mu'tazilites hold that rational perception itself creates obligation. The Maturidi position is that obligation requires a command from the Lawgiver. This subtle difference has profound implications for how the Hanafi school approaches moral reasoning and the role of revelation in legal obligation.

Guided Path
1
State the Ash'ari position
Identify from the passage that the Ash'aris hold that good and evil are 'shar‘ī' (revealed): only by divine decree. Whatever Allah commands is good; whatever He forbids is evil. If He reversed the command, the moral values would reverse accordingly.
This is often called divine command theory.
2
Identify the common ground of Maturidis and Mu'tazilites
Both groups affirm that good and evil are '‘aqlī' (rational): they can be perceived by reason independently of revelation. The passage says 'according to us and the Mu'tazilites, it is rational'.
This is the point of agreement.
3
Locate the Maturidi-Mu'tazili difference
The passage explicitly says: 'But according to us, it does not entail a ruling upon the servant'. The Maturidis hold that rational perception does not create an obligation; the Mu'tazilites hold that it does.
The word 'laysa yastalzimu' (does not entail) indicates the key difference.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The practical difference: For a Mu'tazili, an act rationally known to be good is obligatory even before revelation. For a Maturidi, it is not obligatory until the Lawgiver commands it. This affects whether a person is accountable before revelation reaches them.
The passage mentions that the Maturidis stipulate 'bulūgh al-da‘wah' (reaching of the call) for obligation.

إزالة الرهبوت عن مشكلات مسلّم الثبوت
١٠٤
فأشار إلى المعاني الثلاثة بقوله : "ولا نزاع"، وإلى تعيين المعنى المتنازع فيه بقوله:
بل إنما يكون النزاع الح، وأشار إلى اختلاف الفرق الثلاث بقوله :
فعند الأشاعرة شرعى، أي يجعله فقط، فما أمر به فهو حسن، و ما نهى عنه فهو قبيح ، و لو انعكس الأمر لانعكس الأمر، و عندنا و عند المعتزلة عقلى ، أي لا يتوقف (معرفتهما) على الشرع
فعند الأشاعرة شرعى أي بجعله فقط ، أي الحس بمعنى استحقاق المدح والثواب يكون بجعل الشارع فقط، وكذا القبح بمعنى استحقاق الذم والعقاب يكون شرعيا بجعل الشارع فقط، وقال في بيان جعل الشارع فما أمر به فهو حسن، وما نهى عنه فهو قبيح، ولو انعكس الأمر بالنهي عن المأمور به نسخا، والأمر بالمنهي عنه نسخا لانعكس الأمر (بأن يصير المامور به السابق قبيحا، والمنهي عنه السابق حسنا) (۲) وعندنا (الماتريدية) وعند المعتزلة (حسن الحسن) عقلى، وكذا قبح القبيح) أي لا يتوقف إدراك الحسن و القبح على الشرع (كما يقول به الأشاعرة، بل يدرك الحسن و القبح قبل ورود الشرع أيضا).
بيان الفرق بيننا (الماتريدية وبين المعتزلة
بعد الاتفاق فى كونهما عقليين
لكن عندنا لا يستلزم حكماً في العبد، بل يصير موجباً لاستحقاق الحكم من الحكيم الذي لا يرجح المرجوح، فما لم يحكم ليس هناك حكم، ومن ههنا اشترطنا بلوغ الدعوة في التكليف

Removing Fear from the Problems of Muslim al-Thubut
103
From the First Chapter: On the Lawgiver
There is no ruling except from Allah Almighty: i.e., no ruling for the worship of the Lord, belief in His attributes and names, complying with His commands, avoiding His prohibitions, acting by His law, obeying His religion, and refraining from worshipping others or associating partners with Him, except by Allah Almighty through His messengers and prophets, because He is the Creator of everything and its Disposer. So His ruling is considered, not the ruling of anyone else, in legal and cosmic matters, due to His saying: 'The ruling is not except for Allah; He has commanded that you not worship except Him,' and His saying: 'Indeed, His is the creation and the command.' Thus, the Lawgiver over creatures is the Creator, not others.
There is no dispute that an act is good and evil by reason (1) in the sense of the attribute of perfection and deficiency, (2) or in the sense of compatibility with worldly purpose and its opposition, (3) but rather in the sense of deserving His praise and reward and their opposites. (He said:) There is no dispute that the act of the obligated person is good (it may be good) and evil (it may be evil) by reason in the sense of the attribute of perfection and deficiency, (2) or in the sense of compatibility with worldly purpose and its opposition, (3) but rather the dispute only occurs when the good is in the sense of deserving His praise in this world and His reward in the afterlife, and evil is in the sense of deserving His blame and punishment in both; this is the meaning of his statement: and their opposites.
The purpose of this phrase is to explain three things:
1. Explanation of the three meanings of good and evil.
2. Identification of the contested meaning.
3. Explanation of the difference between the Maturidis, Ash'aris, and Mu'tazilites.

104
So he indicated the three meanings by his statement: 'There is no dispute,' and he indicated the identification of the contested meaning by his statement: 'Rather, the dispute only occurs...' and he indicated the difference among the three groups by his statement:
'For the Ash'aris, it is revealed' i.e., only by His decree. Whatever He commanded is good, and whatever He forbade is evil, and if the command were reversed, the matter would be reversed. And according to us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazilites, it is rational, i.e., its knowledge does not depend on revelation.
'For the Ash'aris, it is revealed' i.e., only by His decree, i.e., good in the sense of deserving praise and reward is by the decree of the Lawgiver alone; similarly, evil in the sense of deserving blame and punishment is revealed by the decree of the Lawgiver alone. And he said in explaining the decree of the Lawgiver: Whatever He commands is good, and whatever He forbids is evil. If the command were reversed by prohibiting what was commanded through abrogation and commanding what was forbidden through abrogation, the matter would be reversed (so that the previously commanded becomes evil and the previously forbidden becomes good). (2) And according to us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazilites, good is rational, and similarly evil, i.e., the perception of good and evil does not depend on revelation (as the Ash'aris say), but rather good and evil are perceived even before the arrival of revelation.
Explanation of the difference between us (Maturidis) and the Mu'tazilites
After agreeing that both are rational.
But according to us, it does not entail a ruling upon the servant; rather, it becomes a cause for deserving the ruling from the Wise One who does not prefer the inferior. So as long as He has not ruled, there is no ruling. And from here we have stipulated the reaching of the call in obligation.
'But according to us, rational good or evil does not entail a ruling upon the servant (before the arrival of revelation),' rather, good or evil becomes a cause for deserving the ruling and deserving its necessity by command or prohibition from the Wise Lawgiver who does not prefer the inferior and does not rule by it. So as long as the Wise Lawgiver has not ruled (after perceiving good or evil rationally), there is no ruling (obligatory by mere reason).
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary 'Izalat al-Rahbūt' on the usul work 'Muslim al-Thubūt'. It begins by establishing that the ultimate Lawgiver (al-Hākim) is Allah alone, as per Quranic proofs. Then it delves into the philosophical-theological debate about the moral value of actions: husn (goodness) and qubh (evil).

The author identifies three meanings of good and evil: (1) as attributes of perfection vs. deficiency, (2) as conducive vs. repugnant to worldly aims, and (3) as deserving praise/reward vs. blame/punishment. The first two are universally agreed upon; the dispute is only about the third meaning.

The passage then outlines the positions of the three major theological schools: Ash'aris hold that an action's moral value depends solely on divine command (it is good because Allah commanded it). Maturidis (the Hanafi theological school) and Mu'tazilites both hold that good and evil are discernible by reason independently of revelation. However, they differ on the consequences: Mu'tazilites argue that rational good and evil impose obligations on humans even before revelation, whereas Maturidis maintain that although reason can perceive good and evil, it does not create a binding obligation until the Lawgiver issues a command or prohibition. This is a key difference between the Maturidi and Mu'tazili positions, despite their agreement that moral values are rational.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-ḥusn): Goodness, in three senses: (1) perfection, (2) beneficial for worldly purpose, (3) deserving praise and reward.
القبيح (al-qubḥ): Evil, in three senses: (1) deficiency, (2) harmful to worldly purpose, (3) deserving blame and punishment.
الحاكم (al-ḥākim): The Lawgiver, who is Allah alone; His ruling is the source of legal obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between Maturidis and Mu'tazilites regarding husn and qubh?
A) Maturidis deny rational perception
B) Mu'tazilites deny that rational perception creates obligation
C) Maturidis hold that rational perception does not create obligation without revelation
D) Mu'tazilites hold that good and evil are purely subjective

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi position is that reason perceives good and evil, but only revelation makes them obligatory; this distinguishes it from both Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views.

Rational Obligation in Hanafi Usul: The Qualified Independence of Reason
Presenting the Hanafi view on rational obligation and the qualification of the reported statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position on rational obligation (istiqlal al-'aql) and distinguish the Maturidi view from the extreme Mu'tazili position.

Core Idea
Reason can obligate faith only after reflection—Abu Hanifa's statement is not a blank check for rationalism.

Some Hanafis, especially the Maturidis, hold that reason can independently perceive certain rulings, such as the obligation of faith and the prohibition of disbelief. They support this with a report from Abu Hanifa that no one is excused for ignorance of the Creator. However, the commentator al-Badakhshani restricts this to after a period of reflection, aligning the Hanafi view more closely with the Ash'ari position that revelation is necessary for obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of some Hanafis
Note the statement: reason can independently perceive some of Allah's rulings, making faith obligatory and disbelief prohibited. This is the position of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and some Iraqi Hanafis.
Contrast this with the Ash'ari view that reason cannot obligate anything without revelation.
2
Analyze the report from Abu Hanifa
The report says: 'No one has an excuse for ignorance of his Creator, because of the evidence he sees.' Identify the dalil type (qawl sahabi) and its thubut (zanni) and dalala (zanni).
The evidence is the rational signs of creation, but the obligation is not immediate.
3
Apply al-Badakhshani's qualification
The commentator says 'perhaps the meaning is after the passage of a period of reflection.' Understand that this qualification preserves the need for contemplation and revelation's role.
The period varies because intellects differ, so no fixed time.
4
Compare with the Ash'ari position
The Ash'aris argue that obligation (taklif) is purely from revelation (al-sam'). The Hanafi view as qualified here approaches this by requiring reflection, which is akin to the call of messengers.
Thus the ikhtilaf is narrowed: the dispute is about whether reason alone can obligate after sufficient reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول بعض الحنفية إن الحسن العقلى والقبح العقلى قد يوجبان الحكم ثم من الحنفية من قال: إن العقل قد يستقل في إدراك بعض أحكامه تعالى، فأوجب الإيمان، وحرّم الكفر، وكلما لا يليق بجنابه تعالى، حق على الصبي العاقل وروى عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال لا عذر لأحد في الجهل بخالقه، لما يرى من الدلائل أقول : لعل المراد بعد مضي مدة التأمل فإنه بمنزلة دعوة الرسل في تنبيه القلب ، وتلك المدة مختلفة ، فإن العقول متفاوتة
A statement of some Hanafis that rational good and evil may obligate a ruling. Then among the Hanafis are those who said: Reason can independently perceive some of His rulings, may He be exalted, so it made faith obligatory and disbelief prohibited, and everything that is unbefitting to His majesty is a right upon the rational minor. It is reported from Abu Hanifa that he said: No one has an excuse for ignorance of his Creator, because of the evidence he sees. I say: Perhaps the meaning is after the passage of a period of reflection, for it is like the call of the messengers in awakening the heart, and that period varies, because intellects differ.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a view among some Hanafis, including Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and some Iraqi shaykhs, that reason can independently perceive some of Allah's rulings, such as the obligation of faith and the prohibition of disbelief, even for a rational minor. They support this with a report from Abu Hanifa that no one is excused for ignorance of the Creator due to the evidence they see. However, the commentator (al-Badakhshani) clarifies that this report likely means after a period of reflection, not immediate independent rational obligation. The length of reflection varies because intellects differ. This places the Hanafi view in a narrower context, avoiding the extreme Mu'tazili position.

Key Terms
استقلال العقل (istiqlal al-'aql): Independence of reason in perceiving rulings without revelation.
الصبي العاقل (al-sabi al-'aqil): A minor who has reached the age of discernment and is rationally capable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does al-Badakhshani interpret Abu Hanifa's statement that 'no one has an excuse for ignorance of his Creator'?
A) It means reason alone immediately obligates faith without any delay.
B) It applies only to prophets and messengers.
C) It means after a period of reflection on the evidence.
D) It refers only to adults, not minors.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's statement on rational obligation is conditioned by a period of reflection, preserving the primacy of revelation in the Hanafi usul.
