Partial Free Will and Compulsion in Hanafi Usul
Defining the servant's partial free will and compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on human free will (ikhtiyar juz'i vs jabr) and identify the Quranic evidence used to support partial choice.

Core Idea
Humans have partial free will in physical actions but are compelled in universal intellectual matters — the basis of valid taklif.

This lesson unpacks a foundational usul al-fiqh concept: the Hanafi understanding of human agency. The author, Muhammad Anwar al-Badakhshani, defines the middle path between absolute free will and total compulsion. Grasping this distinction is essential for understanding why taklif (legal responsibility) is valid in shar'i matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two domains of human action
Distinguish between partial physical perceptions (idrakat juz'iyya) and universal intellectual sciences (ulum kulliyya). The servant is free in the former and compelled in the latter.
This is the core duality in the Hanafi theory of action.
2
Understand the relationship between partial perceptions and universal will
Universal will (irada kulliya) emanates from universal perception; partial perceptions are connected to the physical senses. The servant has partial control over the physical means.
Actions like prayer use partial means, so choice exists.
3
Recognize the Quranic evidence for partial choice
Verses like Surah al-Nahl:78 and Surah al-Balad:8-9 show that God grants faculties (hearing, sight, heart) which are means for optional actions, indicating partial free will.
The existence of means implies capacity and therefore choice.
4
Apply the definition to explain the validity of taklif
Since shar'i obligations (prayer, fasting) are partial actions involving bodily movements, the servant can choose to perform them, making reward and punishment meaningful.
This resolves the theological problem of how God can justly hold humans accountable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندى (العبد) مختار بحسب الإدراكات الجزئية الجسمانية، ومجبور بحسب العلوم الكلية العقلية .
وشرح هذا الكلام مكتوب في رسالتي الفطرة الالهية (وإنها تلك الرسالة) لأجدى (أكثر نفعا ) من تفاريق العصا (من قطعات المتفرقة للعصا المكسورة) وهذا الكلام مثل عند العرب وكناية عن كثرة النفع عندهم .
وأحال المصنف في مواضع من كتابه هذا على ثلاثة من مؤلفاته :"السلم". و"الإفادات"، و"الفطرة الإلهية "، ومدح كتابه هذا
شرح كلام المصنف: 
وهناك في محل الاختيار أمران: إدراكات جزئية وإرادة كلية، فعلم أن النوع الإنسان كلي يتعلق بأمور كلية، ويتصرف فيها بإرادة كلية، بخلاف النوع الآخر مثل الحمار والبقر، فإنه ليس لهما إدارك كلى، ولا إرادة كلية، بل إحساسات وإردات جزئية فالإرادة الكلية تنبعث من الإدراك الكلى، وكذلك له إدركات جزئية جسمانية، تتعلق بالحواس القائمة بالجسم، فالعبد مختار باعتبار تلك الإدركات الجزئية الجسمانية، ومجبور بحسب العلوم الكلية العقلية التي تنبعث منها الإدراكات الجزئية، فالأمور الشرعية كالصلاة والصوم والحج أمور جزئية تتعلق بالجسم، ولصدور الأمر الجزئي مباد جزئية كالتخيل والشوق الخاص والإرادة الخاصة، فالعبد فيها مختار بين أن يفعل و يستحق الثواب، وأن لا يفعل فيستحق العقاب، وأما الأمور الكلية التي تتعلق بالإرادة الكلية ففيها العبد مجبور وغير مختار، لأنها من الأمور الطيبعية غير الإختيارية، فهو مختار في استعمال جزئيات تلك الكليات من الإدراك والإرادة ومجبور في قبول تلك العلوم الكلية العقلية والإرادات الكلية المنبعثة منها.
وبما أن الشرائع أمور جزئية اعتقادية وعملية صح التكليف بها بالنظر إلى المبادى والوسائل الجزئية الاختيارية، لأن العبد في أفعاله الجزئية مختار بين أن يفعلها أو يتركها، فباعتبار العلوم الكلية والإرادات الكلية،مجبور فلايكون خالقا لأفعاله، و باعتبار العلوم الجزئية مختار ، فصح، تكليفه وحَسُنَ .
ويمنن الله على عباده بإعطاء العقل والسمع والبصر وجعل لكم السمع والأبصار والأفئدة و قد قال ألم نجعل له عينين و لسانا وشفتين فهذه النصوص وأمثالها تدل على أن للعبد اختياراً جزئيا لاستعمال هذه الوسائل والنعم، فما يترتب ويحصل منها يكون أيضًا اختيار يا لأجل أن أسبابها اختيارية.
And according to me, the servant is free (mukhtar) with respect to partial physical perceptions, and compelled (majbur) with respect to universal intellectual sciences.
And the explanation of this speech is written in my treatise 'al-Fitra al-Ilahiyya' (and it is that treatise) which is more beneficial than scattered pieces of a broken stick (a metaphor among Arabs for abundant benefit).
And the author (Musannif) in places in this book references three of his works: 'al-Sullam', 'al-Ifadat', and 'al-Fitra al-Ilahiyya', and praises this book.
Commentary on the author's words:
In the locus of choice there are two matters: partial perceptions and universal will. So it is known that the human type is universal, connected to universal matters, and acts upon them with a universal will, contrary to other types like the donkey and cow, which have neither universal perception nor universal will, but rather partial sensations and wills. So the universal will emanates from universal perception, and similarly he has partial bodily perceptions connected to the senses located in the body. So the servant is free (mukhtar) in consideration of those partial bodily perceptions, and compelled (majbur) according to the universal intellectual sciences from which the partial perceptions emanate. So the shar'i matters like prayer, fasting, and Hajj are partial matters connected to the body, and for the occurrence of the partial command, there are partial principles like imagination, specific desire, and specific will. So the servant in them is free between doing and deserving reward, or not doing and deserving punishment. As for universal matters connected to universal will, the servant therein is compelled and not free, because they are from natural non-volitional matters. So he is free in using the particulars of those universals of perception and will, and compelled in accepting those universal intellectual sciences and the universal wills emanating from them.
And since the laws (shara'i') are partial matters, both creedal and practical, the charge (taklif) is valid with respect to the partial optional means, because the servant in his partial actions is free to do them or leave them. So by consideration of universal sciences and universal wills, he is compelled and not a creator of his actions, and by consideration of partial sciences, he is free, so his being charged is valid and good.
And God bestows upon His servants by giving intellect, hearing, and sight, and He said: 'He made for you hearing, sight, and hearts' (Surah al-Nahl:78) and He said: 'Did We not make for him two eyes, a tongue, and two lips?' (Surah al-Balad:8-9). So these texts and their like indicate that the servant has partial choice in using these means and blessings, so what results and occurs from them is also optional because their means are optional.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut by Muhammad Anwar al-Badakhshani defines the Hanafi position on human free will. It begins by stating the principle: the servant is free with regard to partial physical perceptions but compelled by universal intellectual sciences. The author then praises his own treatise on the subject, referencing his other works. The detailed commentary explains that humans have two types of perceptions: partial (juz'iyya) related to senses and body, and universal (kulliyya) related to intellect. In shar'i matters like prayer, fasting, and Hajj, which are partial, humans have free choice and deserve reward or punishment. However, universal matters like accepting intellectual principles are beyond choice and are compelled. This nuanced view avoids the extremes of complete free will (as claimed by some) or absolute compulsion. The passage concludes with Quranic evidence from Surah al-Nahl:78 and Surah al-Balad:8-9 to prove that humans have a partial, instrumental choice, but the overall framework is determined by God.

Key Terms
اختيار جزئي (ikhtiyar juz'i): The human capacity to choose in partial, particular actions related to the body and senses.
جبر (jabr): Compulsion; the state of being compelled by universal intellectual principles and divine decree.
إرادة كلية (irada kulliya): Universal will that emanates from universal perception and governs overarching matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the charge (taklif) valid for shar'i acts like prayer?
A) Because humans have complete free will in all matters.
B) Because shar'i acts are universal matters that involve intellectual compulsion.
C) Because shar'i acts are partial, bodily actions for which the servant has partial choice.
D) Because reward and punishment are predetermined by God regardless of choice.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms partial free will (ikhtiyar juz'i) in physical, shar'i actions while maintaining that universal intellectual matters are compelled (jabr), establishing a balanced foundation for legal responsibility.

The Pre-Eternity of Rulings and Limited Knowledge Before Prophethood
Introduces the issue of pre-eternity of rulings and defines hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the pre-eternity of rulings and evaluate the Mu'tazila counter-argument regarding the rational knowability of rulings before revelation.

Core Idea
Rulings are pre-eternal in God's speech but not accessible before revelation.

How can a ruling be eternal yet unknown until a prophet comes? This passage addresses that paradox. It clarifies the Hanafi-Ash'ari view that while God's internal speech is eternal, the specific determination of a ruling for a particular act occurs at the time of revelation. This resolves the problem of accountability before revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
State the question: What is the nature and knowability of rulings before prophethood? The passage frames this as a point of agreement and disagreement.
2
State the area of agreement
Both Hanafis and Mu'tazila agree that rulings are pre-eternal in some sense. Identify the common ground.
3
Compare the two positions
Mu'tazila say rulings are essential attributes; some are rationally known. Hanafis say no specific ruling exists before revelation. Contrast the proofs.
4
Understand the Hanafi proof
The obligator is God's eternal internal speech. Its manifestation occurs through talluq which is temporally originated at prophethood.
Focus on the concept of kalam nafsi and talluq.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: No legal obligation or sin before revelation according to Hanafis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۲ مسألة قدم الحكم وحدوثه وعدم العلم ببعض الأحكام قبل البعثة

لا خلاف في أن الحكم وإن كان في كل فعل قديماً ، لكن يجوز أن لا يعلم قبل البعثة بعض منه بخصوصه
أما عند المعتزلة فلأنه وإن كان ذاتياً، لكن منه ما لا يدرك بالعقل علة الحسن والقبح فيه
أما عند غيرهم فلأن الموجب و إن كان الكلام النفسي القديم ، لكن ربما كان ظهوره بالتعلق و هو حادث بحدوث البعثة ، فلا حكم مشخص قبلها ، فلا حرج عندنا

ولا بد قبل الخوض في غمار هذا البحث من العلم بأمور هي كالمبادي :
١ - الأول: تعريف الحكم : وهو عند علماء الأصول: خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف اقتضاء أو تخييراً.
الخطاب في اللغة : هو توجيه الكلام نحو الغير للافهام، وفي الاصطلاح هو الكلام الموجه إلى المكلف لاصلاح فعله وتفريغ ذقته من الأوامر والنواهي الشرعية .
- والثاني : بيان الحكم عند الفقهاء : وهو ما ثبت بخطاب الله تعالى، ويسمى ذلك بأثر الخطاب، مثل الوجوب والندب والاباحة والحرمة والكراهة .
2. Issue: The pre-eternity of the ruling and its occurrence, and the lack of knowledge of some rulings before the prophethood.

There is no disagreement that the ruling, although it is pre-eternal in every action, yet it is possible that before prophethood some of it in particular is not known.

As for the Mu'tazila, because even though it is essential, yet some of it is not perceived by reason as the cause of goodness or badness in it.

As for others (Ahl al-Sunnah), because even though the obligator is the eternal internal speech, perhaps its manifestation occurs through connection (talluq) which is originated with the advent of prophethood. So there is no specific ruling before it, thus there is no restriction according to us.

And before delving into the thick of this discussion, it is necessary to know matters that are like preliminaries:
1. First: Definition of hukm (ruling): According to the scholars of usul, it is the speech of God Most High connected to the action of the legally responsible person, requiring a choice or permission.
Linguistically, khatab (address) is directing speech towards another for understanding. In terminology, it is speech directed to the responsible person to reform his action and discharge his responsibility from the divine commands and prohibitions.
- Second: The explanation of hukm according to the jurists: It is that which is established by the speech of God Most High, and it is called the effect of the address, such as obligation, recommendation, permission, prohibition, and dislikedness.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is an usul rule that addresses the nature of legal rulings before prophethood. The author first states a consensus: rulings are pre-eternal (qadim) in the sense that they are eternally known to God, but it is possible that some specific rulings were not known to humans before the advent of a prophet. He then contrasts two views:

- Mu'tazila: Rulings are essential (dhati) attributes of actions, but some rulings cannot be known by reason (the cause of goodness or badness is not rationally perceptible). Therefore, knowledge of some rulings requires revelation.
- Ahl al-Sunnah (which includes Hanafis): The obligator (al-mujib) is God's eternal internal speech (kalam nafsi). However, its manifestation (zuhur) occurs through its connection (talluq) to specific acts, which is originated (hadith) at the time of prophethood. Thus, before prophethood, there is no specific ruling (hukm mushakhkhas) for each act, so there is no legal restriction.

The author then begins preliminary definitions needed for this research. He defines hukm (ruling) as God's speech connected to the action of the mukallaf (legally responsible person), requiring either a demand (iqtida) or a choice (takhyir). He also defines khatab (address) linguistically and terminologically, and distinguishes between the usul definition of hukm and the fiqh definition (the effect of the address, i.e., the five legal values).

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): According to usul scholars: God's speech connected to the action of the responsible person requiring a choice or permission. According to fuqaha: the effect of that speech (e.g., obligation).
الخطاب (al-khatab): Linguistically: directing speech towards another for understanding. Terminologically: speech directed to the responsible person to reform his action and discharge his responsibility from commands and prohibitions.
الكلام النفسي (al-kalam al-nafsi): Internal speech of God (eternal attribute) from which rulings derive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason that some rulings are not known before prophethood for Ahl al-Sunnah?
A) Because God's speech is created and temporary.
B) Because the manifestation of the ruling occurs through its connection to the act, which originates at the time of prophethood.
C) Because reason can never know any ruling.
D) Because rulings are not pre-eternal.

Key Takeaway
Rulings are pre-eternal in God's speech but their specific binding force is realized only through revelation, so no accountability before prophethood.

Preliminary Definitions: Hukm and Khatab in Hanafi Usul
Defines hukm and khatab, distinguishing usul and fiqh meanings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define hukm and khatab according to Hanafi usul and distinguish between the usul and fiqh definitions of hukm.

Core Idea
Hukm is God’s address linked to actions; its fiqh effect is obligation or permission.

Before diving into specific usul debates, the author provides foundational definitions. This lesson unpacks the technical meanings of hukm (ruling) and khatab (address), highlighting the dual usage in Hanafi scholarship: the usul definition (God's speech) versus the fiqh definition (the resulting legal value).

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of hukm from usul scholars
Identify the components: God's speech, connected to action, requiring a demand or choice.
Note the phrase 'bi fi'l al-mukallaf'.
2
Understand the definition of khatab
Linguistic versus terminological definitions. The terminological adds purpose: reforming action and discharging responsibility.
3
Compare the usul and fiqh definitions of hukm
Usul: hukm is the address itself. Fiqh: hukm is the effect of the address (e.g., obligation).
This distinction is unique to Hanafi usul.
4
Recognize the fiqh definition components
The effect includes the five ahkam taklifiyya: obligation, recommendation, permission, prohibition, dislikedness.

The Usul Rule: Gratitude (Shukr) is Wajib by Reason and Revelation
Refuting objections to the obligation of gratitude using Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi usul rule that gratitude (shukr) is wajib (obligatory) by both reason ('aql) and revelation (naql), and apply the Quranic proof while distinguishing strong from weak responses to objections.

Core Idea
Gratitude to Allah is wajib because both reason and revelation command it; the Quranic imperative is decisive and not mockery.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text refutes an objection that the obligation of gratitude resembles mockery. It presents two responses: one weak (relying on sincerity) and one strong (directly citing Quranic commands). The lesson extracts the Hanafi rule that shukr is obligatory by both 'aql and naql, and teaches you how to use Quranic istidlal to defend this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and the two responses
Read the passage and locate the objection being refuted (that gratitude resembles mockery) and the two responses given by the author.
The objection is implicit but clear: if gratitude is commanded despite our inability to repay, it seems absurd.
2
Evaluate the first response based on sincerity
The first response argues that sincerity is what matters, not quantity. The author calls this weak. Understand why: it does not directly refute the mockery claim; it changes the subject.
The principle 'innama al-a'malu bi-nniyat' is correct but misapplied here.
3
Analyze the second response using Quranic verses
The second response quotes Quranic commands to be grateful. Identify how these verses directly rebut the objection: if Allah commands gratitude, it cannot be mockery.
Verses like 2:152 and 14:7 are cited as clear proof (dalil qat'i).
4
Extract the usul rule from the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that gratitude is wajib both by reason and revelation. This is the Hanafi rule. Note that reason alone also indicates obligation (shukr al-mun'im).
This dual basis strengthens the obligation.
5
Apply the rule: Quranic amr and rational necessity
Understand that the Quranic imperative (amr) in 'washkuru' yields wujub per Hanafi usul. The rational argument from gratitude to the Benefactor supports it.
No qarina (contextual indicator) exists to relax the amr to mandub.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشكر، قال المصنف في الجواب الأول عنها : وهو ضعيف جدا ؛ فإن المعتبر عند الله (هو) الاخلاص لا كثرة العمل، وقلته . "انّما الأعمال بالنيات " وقال في الجواب الثاني عنها : وأيضا كيف يقال : ان الشرع ورد بوجوب ما يشبه الاستهزاء ؟ ولو كان الشكر مشابها بالاستهزاء لما قال تعالى واشكروا لي ولا تكفرون ، شاكر ا لأنعمه ) ، (لئن شكرتم لأزيدنكم. وفي قوله : ( فتدبر): إشارة إلى دقة المقام، أو إلى أن الحق هو وجوب الشكر عقلا، كما أنه واجب شرعاً .
Gratitude: The author said in the first response regarding it: It is very weak; for what is considered by Allah is sincerity, not abundance or paucity of action. 'Actions are but by intentions.' And he said in the second response: Also, how can it be said that the Law has commanded something that resembles mockery? If gratitude resembled mockery, then Allah would not have said, 'And be grateful to Me and do not deny Me,' and 'Grateful for His favors,' and 'If you are grateful, I will surely increase you.' And in His statement, 'So ponder,' is an indication of the subtlety of the matter, or that the truth is that gratitude is obligatory by reason, just as it is obligatory by revelation.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the obligation of gratitude. It rebuts an objection that the obligation of gratitude could be seen as mockery. The author gives two responses: the first, which he calls weak, relies on the principle that actions are judged by intentions; the second, stronger response, argues that Allah Himself commands gratitude in the Quran (e.g., 2:152, 14:7), so it cannot be mockery. The passage concludes that gratitude is obligatory both by reason and by revelation. This aligns with the Hanafi view that gratefulness to Allah is wajib (obligatory).

Key Terms
شكر (shukr): Gratitude to Allah for His blessings, considered obligatory (wajib) by both reason and revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It relies on a hadith that is not authentic.
B) It does not directly refute the objection that gratitude resembles mockery.
C) It suggests gratitude is not obligatory.
D) It contradicts the second response.

Key Takeaway
Gratitude to Allah is wajib (obligatory) by both reason and revelation; the Quranic imperative decisively refutes any claim that it resembles mockery.

The Two Senses of Hukm: Eternal Attribute vs. Fiqhi Effect
Definition and classification of hukm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two senses of hukm (divine attribute vs. fiqhi effect) and explain why the Hanafi school considers the divine address pre-eternal while the fiqhi rulings are contingent on revelation.

Core Idea
Hukm is either Allah's eternal speech or its temporal effect on actions — only the second is unknown before revelation.

This passage opens with a crucial distinction: the ruling (hukm) has two meanings. Understanding this distinction is fundamental to grasping the entire Hanafi usul approach to divine commands and the role of revelation. Without it, one might conflate the eternal attribute of speech with the temporal rulings derived from it.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
Find where the text defines hukm from the perspective of usul scholars and from the perspective of jurists (preliminaries 1 and 2).
Usul definition: 'address of Allah relating to actions'. Fiqh definition: 'the effect of that address'.
2
Identify the first sense
The first sense is the divine address itself (khatab), which Hanafis and Ash'aris consider an eternal attribute of Allah.
This is the 'hukm' in the phrase 'al-hukmu qadim'.
3
Identify the second sense
The second sense is the result of that address — the five fiqhi rulings (wujub, nadb, ibaha, hurma, karaha).
This is what the Mu'tazila consider intrinsic to actions.
4
Note the theological basis
Preliminary 6 states that for Mu'tazila, the second sense is intrinsic and therefore incidental (hadith). Preliminary 7 states that for Ash'ari/Maturidi, the first sense is eternal, and the second is contingent on revelation.
This distinction frames the entire dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا خلاف في أن الحكم وإن كان في كل فعل قديماً ، لكن يجوز أن لا يعلم قبل البعثة بعض منه بخصوصه أما عند المعتزلة فلأنه وإن كان ذاتياً، لكن منه ما لا يدرك بالعقل علة الحسن والقبح فيه أما عند غيرهم فلأن الموجب و إن كان الكلام النفسي القديم ، لكن ربما كان ظهوره بالتعلق و هو حادث بحدوث البعثة ، فلا حكم مشخص قبلها ، فلا حرج عندنا ولا بد قبل الخوض في غمار هذا البحث من العلم بأمور هي كالمبادي : ١ - الأول: تعريف الحكم : وهو عند علماء الأصول: خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف اقتضاء أو تخييراً. الخطاب في اللغة : هو توجيه الكلام نحو الغير للافهام، وفي الاصطلاح هو الكلام الموجه إلى المكلف لاصلاح فعله وتفريغ ذقته من الأوامر والنواهي الشرعية . - والثاني : بيان الحكم عند الفقهاء : وهو ما ثبت بخطاب الله تعالى، ويسمى ذلك بأثر الخطاب، مثل الوجوب والندب والاباحة والحرمة والكراهة . والثالث : تعيين محل الخلاف : وهو الحكم بالمعنى الثاني : أي الحكم الفقهي وهو أثر خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف من الأحكام الخمسة المذكورة. ٤- والرابع : أن الأحكام الفرعية الخاصة بكل أمة كانت قبل بعثة نبيهم غير موجودة وغير معلومة لهم، وأما الأحكام الاعتقادية العامة من التوحيد والرسالة والقيامة فكانت موجودة ومعلومة لاكثر الناس، وخصوصاً على ساداتهم وكبرائهم ولكن كانوا لا يقبلونها ولا يقولون بها . نعم صارت تلك الأحكام الأساسية العامة تضعف وتخفي عند فترة الرسل، ثم تتجدد بعد مجيئي رسول جديد . ٥- والخامس : قد كان في هذه الأمة ( قبل البعثة) بقايا من ملة ابراهيم موجودة مع تحريف وتغيير وكانوا يُنسبون الى ملّتى اليهود والنصارا ومسائلهما قبل التحريف و بعد التحريف . فالقول بعدم وجود الحكم أو الأحكام قبل البعثة مشكل جداً. ٦ - والسادس : أن الحكم بالمعنى الثاني من ذاتيات فعل المكلف عند المعتزلة فلا يصدر منه فعل لا يكون متصفاً بحكم من الأحكام يعنى الفعل واتصافه بالحكم موجودان مع وجود المكلف . فالحكم حادث عندهم . والسابع : أن الحكم بالمعنى الأول (خطاب الله المتعلق الخ) قديم عند الأشاعرة والماتريدية لأنه صفته تعالى، فهو قديم كسائر صفاته . قال المصنف : لا خلاف بين أهل السنة والجماعة والمعتزلة) في أن الحكم (بالمعنى الأول) وان كان في كل فعل يتعلق به قديماً ، لكن يجوز أن لا يعلم قبل بعثة الرسول بعض من الحكم بالمعنى الثاني) بخصوصه (العدم استقلال العقل بادراك الحسن و القبح الموجبين للحكم، وإنما يعلم بعض الأحكام بعد البعثة وورود الشرع) ١ - أما (عدم الخلاف) عند المعتزلة فلأنه (الحكم) وان كان ذاتياً (للفعل) لكن منه ما لا يُدرك بالعقل علة الحسن و القبح فيه ( فلا يعلم بعض الأحكام قبل البعثة عندهم). ۲ - وأما (عدم الخلاف) عند غيرهم من الأشاعرة والماتريدية) فلأن الموجب للحكم وان كان الكلام النفسى القديم الذي ستمى خطابا) لكن إنما كان ظهوره
There is no disagreement that the ruling, even if it is eternal in relation to every act, it is possible that some part of it is not known before the prophethood. As for the Mu'tazila, because even though it is intrinsic, some of it is not perceived by the intellect regarding the cause of good and evil. As for others (Ash'ari and Maturidi), because even if the cause is the pre-eternal mental speech, its manifestation occurs through correlation, which is incidental upon the occurrence of prophethood, so there is no specific ruling before it, so there is no difficulty with us. And it is necessary before delving into this research to know matters that are like preliminaries: First: Definition of hukm: According to usul scholars: the address of Allah Most High relating to the action of the legally competent person, either as a requirement or a choice. Linguistically, address is directing speech towards another for comprehension. Terminologically, it is speech directed to the legally competent person to rectify his action and discharge his responsibility from divine commands and prohibitions. Second: The explanation of hukm according to jurists: it is what is established by the address of Allah, and it is called the effect of the address, such as obligation, recommendation, permission, prohibition, and dislikability. Third: Determining the subject of disagreement: it is the hukm in the second sense, i.e., fiqhi ruling, which is the effect of Allah's address relating to the action of the legally competent person from the five rulings mentioned. Fourth: The specific subsidiary rulings for each community were non-existent and unknown before the prophethood of their messenger. As for the general creedal rulings of tawhid, prophethood, and resurrection, they were existent and known to most people, especially their leaders and elders, but they did not accept them. Indeed, those basic general rulings weakened and became hidden during the inter-prophet period, then were renewed after the coming of a new messenger. Fifth: In this ummah (before prophethood), there were remnants of the religion of Ibrahim present, with distortion and alteration, and they were attributed to the religions of Jews and Christians and their issues before and after distortion. So the claim of non-existence of rulings before prophethood is very problematic. Sixth: The ruling in the second sense is among the intrinsic qualities of the action according to the Mu'tazila, so no action is devoid of being characterized by a ruling, meaning the action and its characterization by a ruling are both present with the existence of the legally competent person. Thus, the ruling is incidental (hadith) for them. Seventh: The ruling in the first sense (the address of Allah related, etc.) is eternal according to the Ash'aris and Maturidis because it is His attribute, so it is eternal like His other attributes. The author said: There is no disagreement between Ahl al-Sunnah and the Mu'tazila that the ruling (in the first sense) even if it is eternal in relation to every act, it is possible that some of the ruling (in the second sense) in particular is not known before the prophethood of the messenger (due to the intellect's lack of independence in perceiving good and evil that necessitate the ruling, and only some rulings are known after prophethood and the advent of revelation). 1. As for (the lack of disagreement) according to the Mu'tazila, it is because the ruling, although intrinsic to the action, some of it is not perceived by the intellect regarding the cause of good and evil. 2. As for (the lack of disagreement) according to others (Ash'aris and Maturidis), it is because the cause of the ruling, although it is the pre-eternal mental speech called the address, its manifestation is only...
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on the nature of divine rulings. It distinguishes two meanings: (1) hukm as Allah's eternal attribute (kalam nafsi), and (2) hukm as the fiqhi effect (the five rulings). The main thesis is that while the ruling in the first sense is pre-eternal, specific rulings in the second sense are not fully known before prophethood. This conclusion is agreed upon by all schools (Mu'tazila, Ash'ari, Maturidi), though they differ on the reasoning. For Mu'tazila, rulings are intrinsic to actions but some are rationally unknowable; for Ash'ari/Maturidi, the eternal speech manifests only through revelation. The seven preliminary points clarify definitions, the subject of disagreement (fiqhi hukm), and historical context, including that general creedal rulings were known before prophethood but not followed. The passage highlights that Hanafi usul (following Ash'ari/Maturidi) holds the divine address to be pre-eternal, and that intellect alone cannot determine all moral rulings.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): In usul: the address of Allah relating to actions; in fiqh: the effect of that address (the five rulings).
خطاب (khatab): The divine address (kalam nafsi) directed to the mukallaf, requiring or permitting action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about the second sense of hukm (the fiqhi ruling)?
A) It is pre-eternal as an attribute of Allah.
B) It is intrinsic to actions according to both Mu'tazila and Ash'aris.
C) It is the subject of disagreement between the schools.
D) It is known before prophethood through reason.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the divine address (hukm in the first sense) is pre-eternal, while the specific fiqhi rulings (second sense) are contingent on revelation and cannot be known independently by reason.

The Agreement on Pre-Prophethood Ignorance Across Schools
Explaining the disagreement on knowledge of rulings before prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why both Sunnis and Mu'tazila agree that some rulings are unknown before prophethood, despite their differing ontologies of hukm.

Core Idea
All schools agree that specific fiqhi rulings are unknowable before revelation — but they differ on why: intrinsic vs. attributed.

Despite deep theological differences, the passage shows a surprising agreement: no one claims that all rulings are rationally knowable before prophecy. The disagreement is only on the metaphysical status of the ruling itself. Understanding this nuanced consensus is crucial for grasping the Hanafi position on the role of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of agreement
The author states 'لا خلاف في أن الحكم... لكن يجوز أن لا يعلم قبل البعثة بعض منه بخصوصه'. Find this statement and note that it applies to both Mu'tazila and Ash'ari/Maturidi.
The agreement is that some rulings (in the second sense) are not known before prophethood.
2
Find the two reasonings
The text provides separate justifications: for Mu'tazila it is because rulings are intrinsic but not all are rationally perceptible; for Ash'ari/Maturidi it is because the eternal speech manifests only through revelation.
The difference in justification reflects their differing ontologies of hukm.
3
Understand the Hanafi position
Hanafis, following Ash'ari/Maturidi theology, affirm that the divine address is pre-eternal and that specific rulings become known only through revelation.
This is the standard Hanafi usul position.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah)
For Mu'tazila, actions before revelation have an inherent moral value (some good, some evil). For Hanafis, actions are neutral (ibaha asliyya) until revelation.
This affects how one views the status of actions in the pre-revelation era.

There is no disagreement that the ruling, even if it is eternal in relation to every act, it is possible that some part of it is not known before the prophethood. As for the Mu'tazila, because even though it is intrinsic, some of it is not perceived by the intellect regarding the cause of good and evil. As for others (Ash'ari and Maturidi), because even if the cause is the pre-eternal mental speech, its manifestation occurs through correlation, which is incidental upon the occurrence of prophethood, so there is no specific ruling before it, so there is no difficulty with us. And it is necessary before delving into this research to know matters that are like preliminaries: First: Definition of hukm: According to usul scholars: the address of Allah Most High relating to the action of the legally competent person, either as a requirement or a choice. Linguistically, address is directing speech towards another for comprehension. Terminologically, it is speech directed to the legally competent person to rectify his action and discharge his responsibility from divine commands and prohibitions. Second: The explanation of hukm according to jurists: it is what is established by the address of Allah, and it is called the effect of the address, such as obligation, recommendation, permission, prohibition, and dislikability. Third: Determining the subject of disagreement: it is the hukm in the second sense, i.e., fiqhi ruling, which is the effect of Allah's address relating to the action of the legally competent person from the five rulings mentioned. Fourth: The specific subsidiary rulings for each community were non-existent and unknown before the prophethood of their messenger. As for the general creedal rulings of tawhid, prophethood, and resurrection, they were existent and known to most people, especially their leaders and elders, but they did not accept them. Indeed, those basic general rulings weakened and became hidden during the inter-prophet period, then were renewed after the coming of a new messenger. Fifth: In this ummah (before prophethood), there were remnants of the religion of Ibrahim present, with distortion and alteration, and they were attributed to the religions of Jews and Christians and their issues before and after distortion. So the claim of non-existence of rulings before prophethood is very problematic. Sixth: The ruling in the second sense is among the intrinsic qualities of the action according to the Mu'tazila, so no action is devoid of being characterized by a ruling, meaning the action and its characterization by a ruling are both present with the existence of the legally competent person. Thus, the ruling is incidental (hadith) for them. Seventh: The ruling in the first sense (the address of Allah related, etc.) is eternal according to the Ash'aris and Maturidis because it is His attribute, so it is eternal like His other attributes. The author said: There is no disagreement between Ahl al-Sunnah and the Mu'tazila that the ruling (in the first sense) even if it is eternal in relation to every act, it is possible that some of the ruling (in the second sense) in particular is not known before the prophethood of the messenger (due to the intellect's lack of independence in perceiving good and evil that necessitate the ruling, and only some rulings are known after prophethood and the advent of revelation). 1. As for (the lack of disagreement) according to the Mu'tazila, it is because the ruling, although intrinsic to the action, some of it is not perceived by the intellect regarding the cause of good and evil. 2. As for (the lack of disagreement) according to others (Ash'aris and Maturidis), it is because the cause of the ruling, although it is the pre-eternal mental speech called the address, its manifestation is only...

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute on the nature of divine rulings. It distinguishes two meanings: (1) hukm as Allah's eternal attribute (kalam nafsi), and (2) hukm as the fiqhi effect (the five rulings). The main thesis is that while the ruling in the first sense is pre-eternal, specific rulings in the second sense are not fully known before prophethood. This conclusion is agreed upon by all schools (Mu'tazila, Ash'ari, Maturidi), though they differ on the reasoning. For Mu'tazila, rulings are intrinsic to actions but some are rationally unknowable; for Ash'ari/Maturidi, the eternal speech manifests only through revelation. The seven preliminary points clarify definitions, the subject of disagreement (fiqhi hukm), and historical context, including that general creedal rulings were known before prophethood but not followed. The passage highlights that Hanafi usul (following Ash'ari/Maturidi) holds the divine address to be pre-eternal, and that intellect alone cannot determine all moral rulings.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): In usul: the address of Allah relating to actions; in fiqh: the effect of that address (the five rulings).
خطاب (khatab): The divine address (kalam nafsi) directed to the mukallaf, requiring or permitting action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Despite differing ontologies, all schools agree that specific fiqhi rulings are not rationally knowable before revelation — a consensus that underscores the necessity of prophethood.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

Hukm Before Prophethood: Agreement and Dispute
Explains the locus of disagreement and knowledge of rulings before prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two senses of hukm (al-hukm bi al-ma'na al-awwal wa al-thani) and apply the Hanafi-Ash'ari position on the knowability of rulings before prophethood to explain the practical outcome (la haraja).

Core Idea
Fiqhi rulings depend on revelation; without it, actions were not legally binding before prophethood.

A fundamental question in usul al-fiqh is whether actions before prophethood carried legal rulings. This passage from al-Badakhshani's commentary on Musallam al-Thubut clarifies that the answer depends on which sense of 'hukm' we mean. By examining the agreement between Ahl al-Sunnah and Mu'tazila on the practical outcome, we uncover how the Hanafi school integrates theological commitments with legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two senses of hukm
Recognize that 'hukm' is used in two ways: the eternal divine speech (first sense) and the fiqhi ruling (second sense). The passage explicitly defines the second sense as the effect of Allah's address on the mukallaf's action.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the disagreement.
2
Locate the specific subject of disagreement
The author specifies that the dispute concerns the second sense of hukm, i.e., the fiqhi ruling, not the first sense (which is eternal).
The disagreement is not about the existence of the first sense.
3
Compare the metaphysical frameworks
The Mu'tazila view the second sense as essential to the action (contingent), while Ash'aris/Maturidis view it as dependent on the attachment (ta'alluq) of the eternal speech to the action, which occurs only with revelation.
Despite this difference, both agree on the practical outcome.
4
Trace the agreement on knowability
Both camps agree that some rulings in the second sense are not known before prophethood because the intellect cannot independently perceive the causes (husn/qubh) that necessitate rulings.
Thus, before prophethood, actions are not legally constrained (la haraja).
5
Apply the principle to the remnant of Ibrahim's religion
The passage notes that remnants of Ibrahim's religion existed before Islam but with distortion. This shows that even general creedal rulings were known, though not in their original purity.
This nuance prevents overgeneralization about the absence of rulings.

And the third: specifying the subject of disagreement: it is the ruling in the second sense, which is the fiqhi ruling, and it is the effect of Allah's address related to the action of the responsible person from the five rulings mentioned. 4- And the fourth: that the particular subsidiary rulings specific to every nation were non-existent and unknown to them before the mission of their prophet, while the general creedal rulings of tawhid, prophethood, and resurrection existed and were known to most people, especially their leaders and great ones, but they did not accept them nor profess them. Yes, those general fundamental rulings would weaken and become hidden during the interval between prophets, then be renewed after the coming of a new prophet. 5- And the fifth: there were in this ummah (before the mission) remnants of the religion of Ibrahim existing with distortion and alteration, and they were attributed to the religions of the Jews and Christians, their issues before distortion and after distortion. So the statement that there is no ruling or no rulings before prophethood is very problematic. 6- And the sixth: the ruling in the second sense is, according to the Mu'tazila, among the essences of the action of the responsible person, so no action issues from him that is not characterized by a ruling among the rulings, meaning the action and its characterization by the ruling exist with the existence of the responsible person. Thus the ruling is contingent according to them. And the seventh: the ruling in the first sense (Allah's address, etc.) is eternal according to the Ash'aris and Maturidis, because it is His attribute, so it is eternal like His other attributes. The author said: there is no disagreement between Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah and the Mu'tazila that the ruling (in the first sense), even though it is eternal in relation to every action, it is possible that some of the ruling (in the second sense) in particular is not known before the mission of the messenger, due to the intellect's lack of independence in perceiving the good and evil that necessitate the ruling; and some rulings are only known after the mission and the arrival of the Shari'ah. 1- As for (the absence of disagreement) according to the Mu'tazila, it is because the ruling, even though it is essential to the action, some of it is not perceived by the intellect (the cause of good and evil in it), so some rulings are not known before prophethood according to them. 2- And as for (the absence of disagreement) according to others of the Ash'aris and Maturidis, it is because the necessitator of the ruling, even though it is the eternal internal speech which is called 'address', but its manifestation as a ruling is by attachment to the attachment of the necessitating address, and that (attachment) is contingent with the occurrence of the mission, the mission of the messenger and his recitation of the necessitating address; so there is no ruling (in the second sense) specified and determined before it, so there is no restriction according to us (before prophethood) in action and its abandonment.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the concept of hukm (ruling) and its knowability before prophethood in Hanafi usul, responding to potential objections. It opens by specifying the subject of dispute (mahall al-khilaf): the ruling in the second sense (al-hukm bi al-ma'na al-thani), i.e., the fiqhi ruling which is the effect of Allah's address concerning the actions of the mukallaf. The author then clarifies that subsidiary rulings specific to each nation were not known before the prophet's arrival, but general creedal rulings (usul al-din) were known though rejected. He also notes the remnants of Ibrahim's religion among this ummah before the Prophet (ﷺ). The Mu'tazila view is presented: the ruling in the second sense is an essential attribute of the action, so every action necessarily has a ruling, and the ruling is contingent. The Ash'ari/Maturidi view (aligned with Hanafi usul) is that the ruling in the first sense (Allah's address) is eternal as an attribute of Allah, but the second-sense ruling (the specific legal effect) is contingent on the divine address being revealed. Despite these metaphysical differences, the author (al-Badakhshani) asserts that all parties agree that before prophethood, some rulings in the second sense may be unknown due to the intellect's inability to independently perceive good and evil (tahsin wa taqbih). The Mu'tazila agree because even though the ruling is intrinsic, its specific cause (al-husn wa al-qubh) may not be rationally known. The Ash'ari/Maturidi agree because the second-sense ruling depends on the contingent attachment (ta'alluq) of the eternal speech to the action, which occurs only with the prophet's mission and recitation. Therefore, before prophethood, actions are not legally restricted (la haraja).

Key Terms
الحكم بالمعنى الثاني (al-hukm bi al-ma'na al-thani): The fiqhi ruling, which is the effect of Allah's address concerning the action of the mukallaf; one of the five rulings (wajib, haram, etc.)
العدم استقلال العقل (al-'adam istiqlal al-'aql): The intellect's lack of independence in perceiving the goodness or evil that would necessitate a ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical effect (thamarah al-khilaf) that both Ash'aris/Maturidis and Mu'tazila agree upon regarding actions before the mission of a prophet?
A) All actions before prophethood were haram
B) Before prophethood, there is no legal restriction (la haraja) upon action or abandonment
C) Only actions prohibited by reason were forbidden
D) Actions before prophethood were all mubah

Key Takeaway
Before prophethood, subsidiary fiqhi rulings did not exist or were unknown; the intellect alone cannot obligate or prohibit without revelation.

Al-Hukm al-Shari — The Legal Ruling

The Original Status of Actions Before Revelation
Discusses the three positions on original permissibility or prohibition of actions before Shari'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to STATE the three positions on the original status of actions before revelation and EXPLAIN why this dispute is actually post-revelation based on revealed evidences.

Core Idea
The dispute on asl al-af'al is not about pre-revelation rulings but about interpreting revealed texts.

One of the foundational debates in usul al-fiqh is whether actions and objects are originally permissible (ibahah) or prohibited (hazr) before the Shari'ah is revealed. This lesson unpacks the three major positions and the subtle resolution that the disagreement arises only after revelation, from interpreting the revealed evidences themselves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
State the question: What is the original legal status of actions before the arrival of Shari'ah? This sets the stage for the ikhtilaf.
The text poses this question explicitly.
2
List the three madhhabs
Identify the three positions: 1) Pure ibahah (majority Hanafis and Shafi'is), 2) Pure hazr (Maturidi, author of al-Hidayah, Ahl al-Hadith), 3) Ibahah in wealth, hazr in lives (Sadr al-Islam).
Note that the third position is a compromise.
3
Understand the objection and resolution
Trace the objection: Earlier the author claimed no disagreement between Ahl al-Sunnah and Mu'tazila about unknowability of rulings before prophethood, yet here they disagree on ibahah and hazr (both rulings). The resolution: This disagreement is not about pre-revelation knowledge but about post-revelation interpretation of revealed evidences.
Key insight: the dispute is about how to read the revealed texts, not about what existed before revelation.
4
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: Under ibahah, new matters are permissible until proven forbidden; under hazr, they are forbidden until proven permissible. This affects how a mujtahid approaches novel issues.
This has major implications in contemporary fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما هو الأصل في الأفعال (والأشياء) قبل ورود الشرع وبعده؟
و أما الخلاف المنقول بين أهل الحق (أهل السنة والجماعة) أن أصل الأفعال الإباحة ، كما هو مختار أكثر الحنيفة والشافعية ، أو الحظر كما ذهب إليه غيرهم و قال صدر الإسلام : الإباحة في الأموال ، والحظر في الأنفس، فقيل: بعد الشرع بالأدلة السمعية ، أي دلت على أن ما لم يقم فيه دليل التحريم ماذون فيه ، أو ممنوع عنه ، وفيه مافيه
ولما ورد على الكلام الشابق من عدم الخلاف بين أهل السنة والمعتزلة في عدم العلم ببعض الأحكام قبل البعثة، أنهم قد اختلفوا في الأفعال هل هي في الأصل مباحة أو محزمة؟ فكيف قلت : لا خلاف في عدم العلم ببعض الأحكام قبل البعثة ؟ مع أن الإباحة والحرمة حكمان ؟
فأجاب عنه المصنف وقال : وأما الخلاف المنقول بين أهل الحق بأن أصل الأفعال
( فيه ثلاثة مذاهب)
۱ - الإباحة كما هو مختار أكثر الحنفية والشافعية،
٢ - أو الحظر والحرمة) كما ذهب إليه غيرهم (أبو منصور الماتريدي، وصاحب الهداية وعامة أهل الحديث)
-٣- وقال صدر الاسلام الاباحة في الأموال، والحظر والحرمة) في الأنفس. فقيل (في الجواب عن ذلك السئوال: أن هذا الخلاف جاء) بعد الشرع بالأدلة السمعية، أي دلت (الأدلة السمعية) على أن ما لم يقم فيه دليل التحريم (فمباح) ومأذون
What is the original state of actions (and things) before and after the arrival of the Shari'ah? And as for the transmitted disagreement among the people of truth (Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah) that the original state of actions is permissibility, as is the chosen view of most Hanafis and Shafi'is, or prohibition, as held by others, and Sadr al-Islam said: permissibility regarding wealth, and prohibition regarding lives. It was said: after the Shari'ah, by the revealed evidences, i.e., they indicated that whatever no evidence of prohibition has been established for is permitted, or prohibited, and there is much to discuss. And when it was objected to the previous statement that there is no disagreement between Ahl al-Sunnah and the Mu'tazila regarding the lack of knowledge of some rulings before prophethood: Indeed they have disagreed about actions, whether they are originally permissible or forbidden. So how can you say there is no disagreement in the lack of knowledge of some rulings before prophethood, given that permissibility and prohibition are rulings? The author replied and said: As for the transmitted disagreement among the people of truth regarding the original state of actions, there are three madhhabs: 1- Permissibility, as is the chosen view of most Hanafis and Shafi'is; 2- Or prohibition, as held by others (Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, the author of al-Hidayah, and the generality of Ahl al-Hadith); 3- And Sadr al-Islam said permissibility regarding wealth, and prohibition regarding lives. It was said (in answer to that question): that this disagreement arose after the Shari'ah, by the revealed evidences, i.e., the revealed evidences indicated that whatever no evidence of prohibition has been established for is permissible and permitted.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage addresses the well-known usul dispute: what is the original legal status of actions and objects before the revelation of the Shari'ah? The author first poses the question. He then transmits a disagreement among Ahl al-Sunnah. The majority of Hanafis and Shafi'is hold that the original state is permissibility (ibahah). Others, including Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (from whom the Maturidi creed derives) and the author of al-Hidayah (a leading Hanafi fiqh text), hold that it is prohibition (hazr). Sadr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi?) proposes a middle view: permissibility in matters of property, prohibition regarding lives. An objection is raised: earlier the author claimed no disagreement between Ahl al-Sunnah and Mu'tazila about not knowing some rulings before prophethood; yet here they disagree on whether the original ruling is ibahah or hazr — both are rulings! The author clarifies: this disagreement is not about pre-revelation knowledge; rather, it is a post-revelation dispute based on revealed evidences (adillah sam'iyyah). That is, after the Shari'ah came, scholars derived from the texts that what is not explicitly prohibited is permitted (the majority view), or that everything is prohibited unless permitted (the other view). So the dispute is not about whether rulings exist before revelation, but about how to interpret the revealed texts themselves. This resolves the apparent contradiction with the earlier claim of no disagreement on unknowability.

Key Terms
الإباحة (al-ibahah): The original legal status of permissibility; actions and things are considered allowed unless there is evidence of prohibition.
الحظر (al-hazr): The original legal status of prohibition; actions and things are considered forbidden unless there is evidence of permission.
الأدلة السمعية (al-adillah al-sam'iyyah): Revealed evidences from the Quran and Sunnah that serve as the basis for the dispute about original status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the position of Sadr al-Islam on the original status of actions?
A) Pure ibahah (permissibility)
B) Pure hazr (prohibition)
C) Ibahah in wealth, hazr in lives
D) Suspension (waqf)

Key Takeaway
The dispute on asl al-af'al is not about pre-revelation rulings but about interpreting the revealed texts after the Shari'ah arrived.

The Mu'tazila Classification of Actions: Voluntary and Involuntary
Defines and subdivides Mu'tazila action categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Mu'tazila division of actions into voluntary and involuntary, and distinguish the two categories based on the criterion of survival necessity.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, reason can perceive good and evil in actions, generating the five shari' rulings even before revelation.

This lesson introduces the Mu'tazila classification of actions, which serves as a foundation for their rationalist approach to ethics and law. Understanding this division is crucial for comparing it with the Hanafi position that rulings depend on revelation, not independent reason.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary division
The Mu'tazila first divide actions into voluntary (ikhtiyariyya) and involuntary (ghayr ikhtiyariyya). Locate this in the text and note the defining criterion.
Voluntary actions are those one could live without; involuntary are necessary for survival.
2
Analyze the definition of voluntary actions
The author defines voluntary actions as those that can be omitted without affecting life and livelihood. Examine the example of eating fruit when other food exists.
The presence of choice is central.
3
Distinguish voluntary from involuntary
Involuntary actions are those forced by necessity, like eating, drinking, sleep. Understand that one has no real choice to leave them completely.
These are not subject to moral evaluation before revelation.
4
Recognize the sub-division based on perceived good or bad
Voluntary actions are further divided into those where reason perceives a good or bad quality (jihah muhassinah aw muqabbihah) and those where it does not.
This distinction determines whether the action falls under the five rulings or remains neutral.
5
Relate to the five rulings
Actions with a perceived good/bad aspect yield the well-known five categories: wajib, haram, makruh, mandub, mubah. Actions without such perception are not ruled by reason.
This is a purely rational derivation, contrasting with the Ash'ari/Hanafi reliance on revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة فقسموا الأفعال الاختيارية - وهي التي يمكن البقاء والتعيش بدونها، كأكل الفاكهة ، مثلاً- إلى مايدرك فيه جهة محسنة أو مقبحة ، فينقسم إلى الأقسام الخمسة المشهور، وإلى ماليس كذلك
ولهم فيه قبل الشرع ثلاثة أقوال : الإباحة تحصيلاً لحكمة الخلق و دفعاً للعبث ، وربما يمنع الاستلزام ، والحظر لئلا يلزم التصرف في ملک الغير بغير إذنه وقد مرّ
۱ - وأما المعتزلة فقسموا الأفعال (اولاً) إلى الاختيارية وغير الاخيتارية، ثم عرف المصنف الأفعال الاختيارية تعريفاً جديداً: " وهي التي يمكن البقاء والتعيش بدونها أي لا يحتاج الإنسان ولا يضطر اليها في حياته، فله اختيار في الاتيان بها وعدمه، فلايكون مسلوب الاختيار في فعلها أو تركها، كأكل الفاكهة مثلاً ( عند وجود طعام (آخر) وغير الاختيارية : هي التي لا يمكن البقاء و التعيش بدونها . أي لا اختيار للإنسان في تركها، بل يضطر اليها في حياته ... مثل الأكل والشرب والنوم وغيرها. ۲ - ثم قسم الأفعال الاخيتارية إلى نوعين : الى ما يُدرك فيه جهة محسنة أو مقبحة، والى ما لا يدرك فيه الجهتان . - فينقسم ما فيه جهة محسنة أو مقبحة إلى الأقسام الخمسة المشهورة . وهي الواجب والحرام والمكروه والمندوب والمباح، وأما النوع الثاني الذى لا يدرك فيه
And as for the Mu'tazila, they divided voluntary actions - which are those that life and livelihood can continue without, such as eating fruit e.g. - into that in which a good or bad aspect is perceived, which then divides into the well-known five categories, and that which is not like that. And they have regarding this before the Shari'ah three sayings: permissibility to achieve the wisdom of creation and avoid futility, and perhaps the implication is prevented; and prohibition to avoid disposition in the property of another without his permission; and it has passed. 1- And as for the Mu'tazila, they divided actions (first) into voluntary and involuntary. Then the author defined voluntary actions with a new definition: "They are those without which life and livelihood can continue, i.e., a person does not need or is forced to in his life, so he has choice in performing them or not, so he is not forced in doing or leaving them, like eating fruit for example (when other food exists). And involuntary actions: those without which life and livelihood cannot continue, i.e., a person has no choice in leaving them, rather he is forced to them in his life... such as eating, drinking, sleep, etc. 2- Then he divided voluntary actions into two types: into that in which a good or bad aspect is perceived, and that in which both aspects are not perceived. Then what has a good or bad aspect divides into the well-known five categories: obligatory, prohibited, disliked, recommended, permissible. As for the second type, that in which neither aspect is perceived...
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage explains the Mu'tazila classification of actions. They first distinguish voluntary actions (ikhtiyariyya) from involuntary (ghayr ikhtiyariyya). Voluntary actions are those one can live without, like eating fruit when other food exists. Involuntary actions are necessary for survival, like eating and drinking. Then they further divide voluntary actions into those where reason perceives a good or bad quality (mahasin or maqabih) and those where it does not. The former yields the five shari' rulings (wajib, haram, etc.). The latter are neutral before revelation. The Mu'tazila also had three opinions on the original state before Shari'ah: permissibility (to fulfill wisdom), prohibition (to avoid unauthorized use of others' property), and a third view. This is a rationalist approach contrasting with the Ash'ari position.

Key Terms
الأفعال الاختيارية (al-af'al al-ikhtiyariyya): Actions that a person can choose to perform or not without being compelled, because survival is possible without them.
جهة محسنة أو مقبحة (jihah muhassinah aw muqabbihah): A quality that reason perceives as good or evil, leading to a shari' ruling of obligation, prohibition, etc.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility; one of the three pre-revelation opinions among Mu'tazila, meaning actions are allowed until a prohibition comes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Eating fruit when other food exists
B) Sleeping
C) Walking
D) Buying a house

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila distinguish voluntary from involuntary actions by survival necessity, and subdivide voluntary actions based on rational perception of good/bad, yielding the five shari' rulings.

Pre-Revelation Rulings: Ijmali vs Tafsili and the Contradiction Objection
Refutation of objections to rational ibaha/hazr and the ijmali/tafsili distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key arguments in the debate over pre-revelation rulings and apply the ijmali/tafsili distinction to resolve apparent contradictions.

Core Idea
Pre-revelation rulings can be known generally (ijmali) but not in detail; contradictions are resolved by distinguishing between reality and opinion.

In this passage, the author engages with a central usul debate: can reason determine the permissibility or prohibition of actions before divine revelation? Objections are raised based on reason's inability to know good and evil, and the author defends the possibility of both ibaha and hazr through the ijmali/tafsili distinction. He then confronts a further objection of contradiction and offers a nuanced resolution. This lesson will guide you through the logical steps of this argument, enabling you to trace how Hanafi usul handles such epistemic challenges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing positions
First, recognize that the passage discusses two possible rulings for actions before revelation: rational permissibility (ibaha) and rational prohibition (hazr). These are the two 'schools' referred to.
The author does not choose one over the other; both are defended as possible.
2
Understand the first objection
The objection states: How can reason claim ibaha or hazr before revelation, given that reason has no jurisdiction over good and evil before the Shari'ah? This is a fundamental challenge to any rational ruling before revelation.
This objection assumes reason cannot judge at all.
3
The original defense: ijmali vs tafsili
The author responds that while detailed (tafsili) knowledge of the ruling's cause (goodness or evil) is absent before revelation, general (ijmali) knowledge is possible. Thus, the absence of detailed knowledge does not negate the possibility of a general rational judgment.
Example: one can know in general that lying is evil even before a specific text prohibits it.
4
Grasp the second objection (contradiction)
The author then raises a further objection: if one scholar claims ibaha and another hazr for the same act, this implies contradictory rulings in reality (nafs al-amr). The act cannot be both permissible and prohibited simultaneously. This challenges the validity of both positions.
The objection argues that the ijmali/tafsili distinction does not resolve the contradiction because the act remains one.
5
Trace the ultimate resolution
The commentator resolves this by noting that in cases where ibaha and hazr conflict, one ruling is in reality (nafs al-amr) and the other is only in the opinion (zann) of the scholar. Thus, no actual contradiction exists. Alternatively, if both rulings mean 'no hardship' (as with obligation and permissibility), they are not truly opposite.
This preserves the possibility of both rational judgments while avoiding logical inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما أورد عليهما أنه كيف يقال بالاباحة أو الحظر العقليين قبل ورود الشرع؟ وقد فُرض أنه لا حكم للعقل في فعل بحسنه أو قبحه قبل البعثة؟ فأجاب المصنف عنه بقوله: ولا يرد عليهما الخ. لأن الغرض المفروض أن لا علم بعلة الحكم (حسنه أو قبحه قبل البعثة تفصيلاً، ولا ينافي ذلك العلم بها قبلها اجمالاً؛ لأن وقت التفصيل بعد وقت الاجمال . ثم أورد المصنف من عنده ايراداً آخر على ذينك القولين أو المذهبين ، فقال : "أقول يرد عليهما أنه يلزم جواز اتصاف فعل لا يدرك جهة حسنه أو قبحه) بحكمين متضادين في نفس الأمر ؛ لأنه إذا كان حكم ذلك الفعل الاباحة والحل عند البعض، فمن الممكن أن يكون حكمه الحظر والحرمة عند بعض آخر ، وكذا إذا كان حكمه عند بعض آخر الحظر والمنع، فمن الاحتمال أن يكون حكمه في الحقيقة الاباحة والحل؛ لأن العقل لم يدرك حسنه أو قبحه، حتى يكون حكمه مطابقاً للواقعي ثم رد على الجواب ( بالاجمال والتفصيل) فقال : ولا ينفع الإجمال والتفصيل لأن اختلاف العلة لا يرفع التناقض ، فتأمل ففقال : ولا ينفع الاجمال والتفصيل: لأن اختلاف العلة (علة الحكم) بالاجمال والتفصيل، لا يرفع التناقص . لأنه لو كانت علة الإباحة إجمالاً، وعلة الحرمة تفصيلاً لا يلزم أن يكون المفروض - وهو الفعل اثنين حتى يرفع التناقص . قوله ( فتأقل) : فيه إشارة إلى الجواب عن ايراده؛ حيث قال في تعليقه على هذا القول : فيه إشارة إلى أن مبنى الايراد على زعم أهل المذهبين (مذهب الاباحة ومذهب الحظر، وهو العلم بالحكم المطلق (الاجمالي) مع عدم العلم بالحكم المخصوص (التفصيل : نظر الشارع نظرا إلى خصوصية الفعل، ويمكن أن يجاب عن أصل (الايراد بأن الحكم الاجمالي موقت إلى ورود الشرع بالخصوص (بالحكم التفصيل) فكان كالاجتهاد إذا كان خطأ فهناك يجب العمل به إلى ظهور الضواب مع عدم اتصاف الفعل بالحكمين . وخلاصة الجواب : أنّ لزوم اجتماع المتضادين ليس بموجود في بعض الصور، كما إذا كان الوجوب والاباحة بمعنى مالا حرج في فعله، ففي هذه الصورة يمكن أن يكون الفعل واجباً ومباحاً، لأنه ليس في فعلهما ،حرج، وان كان فعل أحدهما لازماً وفي تركه خرج وليس هنا بينهما تضاد . وفي بعض الصور يكون التضاد بينهما موجوداً، كما في الاباحة ،والحرمة ولكن الجواب عن هذا التضاد ممكن، بأن يكون أحدهما في نفس الأمر، والآخر في زعم أهل المذهب، نعم، لو كان كلاهما في نفس الأمر، أو في زعم أهل المذهب يلزم الجمع بين المتضادين، ولكن ليس الأمر كذلك .
And when it was objected to them: How can rational permissibility or prohibition be claimed before the coming of the Law? Given that it is assumed that reason has no judgment on an act's goodness or evil before prophethood? So the author answered it with his statement: And it does not refute them, etc. Because the assumed purpose is that there is no knowledge of the cause of the ruling (its goodness or evil before prophethood in detail, and that does not contradict knowledge of it before prophethood in general; because the time of detail is after the time of generality. Then the author raised another objection of his own upon those two statements or schools, saying: "I say, it is refuted by that it would necessitate the permissibility of an act whose aspect of goodness or evil is not perceived being described by two contradictory rulings in reality; because if the ruling of that act is permissibility and solution with some, then it is possible that its ruling is prohibition and restriction with others. Similarly, if its ruling with others is prohibition and prevention, then it is possible that its ruling in reality is permissibility and solution; because the intellect did not perceive its goodness or evil, so that its ruling corresponds to the actual." Then he refuted the answer (by generality and detail) saying: And generality and detail do not benefit because difference in cause does not remove contradiction, so ponder. He said: Generality and detail do not benefit because the difference in cause (cause of the ruling) by generality and detail does not remove contradiction. Because if the cause of permissibility is general, and the cause of prohibition is detailed, it does not necessitate that the assumed—which is the act—be two so that contradiction is removed. His statement (so ponder): In it is an indication of the answer to his objection; where he said in his commentary on this statement: In it is an indication that the basis of the objection is on the assumption of the people of the two schools (school of permissibility and school of prohibition), which is knowledge of the absolute ruling (general) along with lack of knowledge of the specific ruling (detail: the Lawgiver's consideration considering the specificity of the act). And it is possible to answer the original objection by that the general ruling is temporary until the coming of the Law with the specific (detailed ruling), so it is like ijtihad if it is wrong, then it must be acted upon until the emergence of the correct view, with the act not being described by both rulings. The summary of the answer: That the occurrence of the two opposites together does not exist in some forms, such as when obligation and permissibility mean that which has no hardship in its doing. In this form, it is possible that the act is obligatory and permissible, because there is no hardship in doing them, even if doing one is necessary and in leaving it there is hardship, and there is no opposition between them here. And in some forms, opposition between them does exist, such as in permissibility and prohibition. But the answer to this opposition is possible, by that one of them is in reality, and the other is in the opinion of the school. Yes, if both were in reality, or in the opinion of the school, then combining the two opposites would be necessary, but it is not so.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the debate about whether acts before divine revelation are rationally characterized as permissible (ibaha) or prohibited (hazr). The author, commenting on the matn 'Muslim al-Thubut', first addresses an objection: How can reason claim ibaha or hazr before the Shari'ah, given that reason has no jurisdiction over good and evil? The original answer is that while detailed knowledge of the cause of ruling is absent, general (ijmali) knowledge is possible. The author then raises a further objection: If one person claims ibaha and another hazr for the same act, that implies contradictory rulings in reality (nafs al-amr), since the act cannot be both simultaneously. He then critiques the ijmali/tafsili answer because differing causes (general vs. detailed) do not resolve the contradiction—the act remains one, and the rulings still conflict. The commentator then provides a defense: In some cases (e.g., wujub and ibaha both meaning 'no hardship'), the rulings are not truly contradictory. In other cases (e.g., ibaha vs. hazr), one ruling is in reality, the other only in the mistaken opinion of the school. Thus, the contradiction is avoided. This passage illustrates the Hanafi usul approach of rational argumentation (dalil aqli) to defend the possibility of pre-revelation rulings, and the use of nuanced distinctions (ijmali/tafsili, nafs al-amr/zann) to resolve apparent difficulties.

Key Terms
الاباحة (al-ibaha): The ruling that an act is permissible (mubah) before revelation.
الحظر (al-hazr): The ruling that an act is prohibited (haram) before revelation.
الاجمال (al-ijmal): General, undetailed knowledge of the ruling or its cause.
التفصيل (al-tafsil): Detailed, specific knowledge of the ruling or its cause after revelation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how can the apparent contradiction between ibaha and hazr for the same act be resolved?
A) By accepting that one ruling is in reality and the other only in the mistaken opinion of the school.
B) By asserting that reason cannot judge at all before revelation, so both are invalid.
C) By claiming that the act is both permissible and prohibited simultaneously.
D) By adopting tawaqquf (suspension) as the only valid position.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul tradition defends the possibility of rational knowledge of pre-revelation rulings in general terms, resolving apparent contradictions through the ijmali/tafsili distinction and the distinction between reality and opinion.

Hanafi Classification of Good Acts: Hasan li Nafsihi and Hasan li Ghayrihi
Classification of good acts and debate on absolute command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four subcategories of hasan (good acts) according to Hanafi usul, classify given acts into these categories, and explain the criterion of choice in annexation.

Core Idea
Hanafi usul divides good acts into those good in themselves and those good for another, with further subdivisions based on fallibility and choice.

In Hanafi legal theory, not all good acts are alike. By induction (istiqra'), scholars classified acts into categories that determine their legal characteristics. This lesson unpacks the four subcategories of hasan: unceasing and ceasing good-in-itself, and annexed and non-annexed good-for-another. Understanding this classification is crucial for analyzing the nature of commands and obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of good acts
From the passage, note that Hanafis divide the good act (hasan) into two primary types: good in itself (hasan li nafsihi) and good for another (hasan li ghayrihi).
This division is made by induction (istiqra') across all acts.
2
Subdivide hasan li nafsihi
Within good in itself, the passage identifies two subtypes: those that do not accept falling (e.g., faith/iman) and those that accept falling (e.g., prayer/salat, which is prohibited at disliked times and invalidated by menstruation).
Faith never falls, even under coercion.
3
Subdivide hasan li ghayrihi
Good for another is also split into two: annexed to the first category (mulhaq bi al-awwal) and non-annexed. The annexed type applies when the servant has no choice in the aspect of goodness (e.g., zakat, fasting, Hajj). Non-annexed examples include jihad, hadd punishments, and funeral prayer, which depend on external causes like disbelief or sin.
Annexation occurs because the goodness is realized through the act itself, not through the servant's choice.
4
Understand the criterion for annexation
The passage stresses that annexed acts are those where the servant has no choice in the aspect of goodness. The goodness is intrinsic to the act's purpose (e.g., zakat relieves need, fasting subdues the soul).
If the servant could choose the goodness, it would not be annexed.
5
Note the debate on the absolute command
The passage raises a question: is the absolute command (amr mutlaq) good in itself and unceasing (as Shams al-A'imma held) or good for another (as in al-Badi')? The answer indicates that the goodness of the commanded act is required, so the lower degree is established.
This debate affects whether the obligation remains even when the act is not performed.

Attention to the division of the good act and the evil act. The Hanafis divided the act by induction into that which is good in itself and does not accept falling, like faith, or accepts falling, like prayer which is prohibited in disliked times; and into that which is good for another, annexed to the first, and this is in what the servant has no choice in, like zakat, fasting, and Hajj, which were legislated with regard to need, self-reform, and the House of God; or not annexed to it, like jihad, hadd punishments, and funeral prayer, for they are due to disbelief, sin, and the Muslim's death. And similarly the divisions of evil. The absolute command stripped of contextual indicators: is it good in itself not accepting falling, as chosen by Shams al-A'imma, or for another, as in al-Badi'? Because the goodness in the commanded thing is required, so the lower is established. 1- Sections of the good act: The Hanafis divided the good act by induction into: (1) What is good in itself, and it is of two types: what does not accept falling at all, like faith (i.e., internal belief, for it does not fall, even under coercion); and what accepts falling, like prayer, for it is prohibited in disliked times and during menstruation and postpartum bleeding. (2) And into what is good for another, and this is also of two types: -1- Annexed to the first, which is good in itself, and it only occurs in what the servant has no choice in, i.e., the servant has no choice in the aspect of goodness for another. And this annexation to the first is only for a goodness in which the servant has no choice. An example: zakat, fasting, and Hajj. The goodness of zakat is for repelling the need of the poor; the goodness of fasting is for subduing the soul and reforming it; the goodness of Hajj is for glorifying the Sacred House of God. So these three (zakat, fasting, Hajj) are only...
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of acts according to Hanafi usul. The Hanafis divide acts (by induction) into good (hasan) and evil (qabih). Good acts are further divided into: (1) good in itself (hasan li nafsihi) — this may be unceasing (like faith, which never drops even under coercion) or ceasing (like prayer, which can be invalidated by menstruation or prohibited times). (2) good for another (hasan li ghayrihi) — this is further split into two: (a) annexed to the first category (like zakat, fasting, Hajj), where the servant has no choice in the aspect of goodness; these are legislated for social need or self-reform. (b) not annexed (like jihad, hadd punishments, funeral prayer), which depend on external causes like disbelief, sin, or death. The passage also raises a question: Is the absolute command (amr mutlaq), stripped of contextual cues, good in itself and unceasing (as Shams al-A'imma held) or good for another (as in al-Badi')? The answer indicates that the goodness of the commanded act is required, so the lower degree is established. The text ends with a detailed breakdown of the first category.

Key Terms
حسن لنفسه (hasan li nafsihi): An act that is good inherently, without consideration of external factors
ملحق بالأول (mulhaq bi al-awwal): An act that, though good for another, is annexed to the category of good in itself because the servant has no choice in the goodness aspect
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Inductive examination of acts to arrive at their classification

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which of the following is an example of a good act that accepts falling (i.e., may be invalidated)?
A) Iman (faith)
B) Prayer (salat)
C) Zakat
D) Jihad

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of acts as hasan li nafsihi or hasan li ghayrihi, and their subdivisions, is foundational for understanding the nature of obligations and the effect of intention.
