The Hanafi Classification of Good Actions (Hasan)
Classifying good actions into hasan li-nafsihi and hasan li-ghayrihi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of hasan (hasan li-nafsihi and hasan li-ghayrihi) and their subtypes, and apply the classification to new examples.

Core Idea
Good actions are either inherently good or good due to an external cause; the latter may be annexed to the former only when the cause is beyond human choice.

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi inductive categorization of good actions (hasan) as presented in the text. Understanding this classification is essential for determining the basis of obligation and for interpreting the absolute command (amr mutlaq). We will see how actions like faith and prayer are good in themselves but differ in permanence, while actions like zakat and jihad derive goodness from external factors with varying connections to human choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of hasan
The text divides good actions into 'good in itself' (hasan li-nafsihi) and 'good due to another' (hasan li-ghayrihi). This is the first-level distinction.
This division is based on the source of goodness: inherent or external.
2
Analyze hasan li-nafsihi subtypes
Within hasan li-nafsihi, there are two subtypes: those whose goodness does not accept falling away (e.g., faith) and those that do accept falling away (e.g., prayer, which is prohibited during menses and disliked times).
Faith is the inner belief that cannot be invalidated, even under coercion.
3
Analyze hasan li-ghayrihi subtypes
Hasan li-ghayrihi is divided into two: 'annexed' (mulhaq) to the first category, where the servant has no choice in the cause (e.g., zakat due to the poor's need), and 'not annexed' (ghayr mulhaq), where the cause is within human action (e.g., jihad due to disbelief).
Annexed actions are treated like hasan li-nafsihi because the cause is not in the servant's control.
4
Understand the concept of 'annexed'
The annexed type is considered 'like the first' (hasan li-nafsihi) precisely because the goodness does not depend on the servant's will. For example, the goodness of fasting comes from subduing the soul, which is beyond choice.
This has implications for whether the obligation remains or can be overridden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه إلى تقسيم الفعل الحسن والفعل القبيح الحنفية قسموا الفعل بالاستقراء إلى ماهو حسن لنفسه لا يقبل السقوط، كالإيمان، أو يقبل كالصلاة التي مُنعت في الأوقات المكروهة، وإلى ماهو لغيره ملحق بالأول، وهو فيما لا اختيار للعبد فيه ، كالزكوة والصوم والحج، شُرِعَت نظراً إلى الحاجة و إصلاح النفس وبيت الله ، أو غير ملحق به كالجهاد والحد وصلاة الجنازة، فإنها بواسطة الكفر والمعصية وإسلام الميت، وهكذا أقسام القبيح، الأمر المطلق مجرداً عن القرائن هل للحسن لنفسه لايقبل السقوط، كما اختاره شمس الأئمة، أو لغيره، كما في "البديع" لثبوت الحسن في المامور به اقتضاء فيثبت الأدنى -١ أقسام الفعل الحسن : الحنفية قسموا الفعل الحسن بالاستقراء إلى : (۱) ما هو حسن لنفسه وهو على نوعين : مالا يقبل السقوط (قط)، كا لايمان (أي التصديق القلبي فانه لا يسقط، ولو بالاكراه وما يقبله كالضلاة، فانها منعت في الأوقات المكروهة، وعند الحيض والنفاس (۲) والى ما هو حسن لغيره، وهذا ايضاً على نوعين : -١- ملحق بالأول الحسن بنفسه وهو إنما يكون فيما لا يكون للعبد فيه اختيار أي لا يكون للعبد اختيار في وجه الحسن لغيره (وهو) أي الالحاق بالأوّل إنما يكون لأجل حسن لا يكون للعبد فيه اختيار ) مثاله : كالزكاة والصوم والحج، فحسن الزكاة لأجل دفع حاجة المساكين، وحسن الضوم لأجل قهر النفس وإصلاحها، وحسن الحج لأجل تعظيم بيت الله الحرام، فإن هذه الثلاث الزكاة والصوم والحج) إنما شرعت نظراً إلى الحاجة والنفس والبيت بيت الله ) فالعلل الثلاث ليست في اختيار العبد. -۲ و غير ملحق به بالأوّل) كالجهاد و (اجراء ) الحد ، وصلاة الجنازة ، فإنّ محاسن هذه الثلاث بواسطة الكفر (في (الأول) والمنع عن المعصية (في الثاني) واسلام الميت ( في الثالث) ثم أشار إلى تقسيم قبح الأفعال وقال: وهكذا أقسام القبيح يعنى يكون القبيح أيضاً على نوعين : قبيح لذاته، وقبيح لغيره والقبيح لغيره على نوعين : ملحق وغير ملحق . ١ - مثال القبيح لعينه الذى لا يزول قبحه كالشرك والرنا . ٢ - ومثال القبيح لعينه الذى يحتمل الروال والشقوط (زوال القبح وسقوطه) كأكل الميتة، فانه تزول الحرمة عند المخمصة والاضطرار، ٣- ومثال القبيح لغيره الذي هو غير ملحق بالقبيح لعينه، كصوم يوم العيد فانه قبيح للزوم الاعراض عن الضيافة ( ضيافة الله)، وكالبيع وقت النداء، فانه مكروه وقبيح لأجل خوف فوت صلاة الجمعة
Alert to the division of the good act and the bad act: The Hanafis divided the act by induction into what is good in itself, which does not accept falling away, like faith, or accepts it, like prayer which is prohibited in disliked times; and into what is good for other than itself, annexed to the first, in which the servant has no choice, like zakat, fasting, and hajj, legislated with regard to need, reform of the soul, and the House of God; or not annexed to it, like jihad, hadd, and funeral prayer, for they are by means of disbelief, sin, and the death of the Muslim. And thus the divisions of the bad. The absolute command stripped of contextual indicators: is it for good in itself that does not accept falling away, as chosen by Shams al-A'immah, or for good due to another, as in al-Badi'? The first section: The Hanafis divided the good act by induction into: (1) what is good in itself, which is of two types: that which does not accept falling away, like faith (i.e., inner belief, it cannot fall even under coercion), and that which accepts it, like prayer, for it is prohibited in disliked times and during menstruation and postpartum. (2) And into what is good due to another, this also has two types: first, annexed to the first (good in itself), which only occurs where the servant has no choice in the aspect of goodness due to another; its example: zakat, fasting, and hajj, for the goodness of zakat is for repelling the need of the poor, the goodness of fasting is for subduing the soul and reforming it, and the goodness of hajj is for glorifying the Sacred House of God; these three were only legislated with regard to need, the soul, and the House of God, so the three causes are not in the servant's choice. Second, not annexed to the first, like jihad, execution of hadd, and funeral prayer, for the goodness of these three is by means of disbelief (in the first), prevention of sin (in the second), and the death of the Muslim (in the third). Then he pointed to the division of bad acts, saying: And thus the divisions of the bad, meaning the bad is also of two types: bad in itself and bad due to another; and bad due to another is of two types: annexed and not annexed. 1. Example of bad in itself whose badness does not disappear: like polytheism and adultery. 2. Example of bad in itself that admits removal and falling away (removal and falling of badness): like eating carrion, for prohibition is removed during dire need and duress. 3. Example of bad due to another that is not annexed to bad in itself: like fasting on the day of Eid, for it is bad due to abandoning God's hospitality; and like selling at the time of the call to prayer, for it is disapproved and bad due to fear of missing Friday prayer.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and classification. The author begins by stating that the Hanafis, through induction (istiqra), divided actions into good (hasan) and bad (qabih). Good actions are further divided into good in themselves (hasan li-nafsihi) and good due to an external reason (hasan li-ghayrihi). Good-in-itself actions are either those that cannot be overridden (like faith, which remains obligatory even under coercion) or those that can be overridden by preventers (like prayer, which is prohibited in disliked times, during menstruation, etc.). Good-due-to-another actions are of two types: those annexed to good-in-itself (meaning the goodness comes from a cause outside the servant's choice, such as zakat due to the need of the poor, fasting due to subduing the soul, hajj due to glorifying the House of God) and those not annexed (like the goodness of jihad due to the cause of disbelief, hadd due to sin, funeral prayer due to the Muslim's death). Similarly, bad actions are divided into bad in themselves (like polytheism and adultery, whose badness never disappears) and bad due to another (like fasting on Eid due to abandoning God's hospitality, or selling at the time of the call due to fear of missing Friday prayer). Bad-due-to-another is also divided into annexed (like usurpation, which is bad due to the right of the owner) and not annexed. The author then transitions to the question of the absolute command (amr mutlaq) and what it indicates, mentioning the disagreement between Shams al-A'imma (who says it indicates good-in-itself) and the author of Badi' al-Nizam (who says it indicates good-due-to-another). This classification is fundamental in Hanafi usul for understanding the nature of commands and the basis of obligation.

Key Terms
حسن لنفسه (hasan li-nafsihi): An action that is good inherently, not due to an external cause.
حسن لغيره (hasan li-ghayrihi): An action that is good due to an external reason or benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in the text, which of the following best describes the subtype of hasan li-nafsihi that accepts falling away?
A) Faith (iman)
B) Prayer (salah) prohibited in disliked times
C) Zakat
D) Jihad

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of good actions distinguishes inherent goodness (hasan li-nafsihi) from acquired goodness (hasan li-ghayrihi), with further subdivisions based on permanence and the role of human choice.

The Hanafi Classification of Bad Actions (Qabih)
Classifying bad actions into qabih li-aynihi and qabih li-ghayrihi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of qabih (qabih li-aynihi and qabih li-ghayrihi) and their subtypes, and apply the classification to examples.

Core Idea
Bad actions are either inherently bad or conditionally bad; the latter can become permissible when the external cause of badness is removed.

This lesson continues the Hanafi inductive classification, now focusing on bad actions (qabih). The text divides bad actions into those that are inherently bad (qabih li-aynihi) and those that are bad due to an external circumstance (qabih li-ghayrihi). Understanding when badness is removable under necessity is crucial for rulings on emergencies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories of qabih
The text divides bad actions into 'bad in itself' (qabih li-aynihi) and 'bad due to another' (qabih li-ghayrihi). This is analogous to the good actions classification.
This division is based on the source of badness: inherent or external.
2
Analyze qabih li-aynihi subtypes
Within qabih li-aynihi, there are two subtypes: those whose badness does not disappear (e.g., shirk and zina) and those whose badness can be removed (e.g., eating carrion, which becomes permissible in dire need).
Polytheism and adultery are never permissible, while carrion is forbidden but becomes halal under duress.
3
Analyze qabih li-ghayrihi subtypes
Qabih li-ghayrihi is also divided into annexed (mulhaq) and not annexed (ghayr mulhaq). The text gives examples: not annexed includes fasting on Eid (bad due to abandoning God's hospitality) and selling during the Friday call (bad due to missing prayer).
The annexed type would be like usurpation, which is bad due to violating another's right. Though not explicitly mentioned in the excerpt, the text indicates the division.
4
Understand the implications of removable badness
If an act is qabih li-aynihi but accepts removal, the prohibition remains in effect until the removing cause (like necessity) arises. For qabih li-ghayrihi, the badness is entirely dependent on the external cause; if the cause ceases, the act is no longer bad.
For example, selling during Friday call is only bad if one fears missing Jumu'ah; if one can attend later, it may be permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Alert to the division of the good act and the bad act: The Hanafis divided the act by induction into what is good in itself, which does not accept falling away, like faith, or accepts it, like prayer which is prohibited in disliked times; and into what is good for other than itself, annexed to the first, in which the servant has no choice, like zakat, fasting, and hajj, legislated with regard to need, reform of the soul, and the House of God; or not annexed to it, like jihad, hadd, and funeral prayer, for they are by means of disbelief, sin, and the death of the Muslim. And thus the divisions of the bad. The absolute command stripped of contextual indicators: is it for good in itself that does not accept falling away, as chosen by Shams al-A'immah, or for good due to another, as in al-Badi'? The first section: The Hanafis divided the good act by induction into: (1) what is good in itself, which is of two types: that which does not accept falling away, like faith (i.e., inner belief, it cannot fall even under coercion), and that which accepts it, like prayer, for it is prohibited in disliked times and during menstruation and postpartum. (2) And into what is good due to another, this also has two types: first, annexed to the first (good in itself), which only occurs where the servant has no choice in the aspect of goodness due to another; its example: zakat, fasting, and hajj, for the goodness of zakat is for repelling the need of the poor, the goodness of fasting is for subduing the soul and reforming it, and the goodness of hajj is for glorifying the Sacred House of God; these three were only legislated with regard to need, the soul, and the House of God, so the three causes are not in the servant's choice. Second, not annexed to the first, like jihad, execution of hadd, and funeral prayer, for the goodness of these three is by means of disbelief (in the first), prevention of sin (in the second), and the death of the Muslim (in the third). Then he pointed to the division of bad acts, saying: And thus the divisions of the bad, meaning the bad is also of two types: bad in itself and bad due to another; and bad due to another is of two types: annexed and not annexed. 1. Example of bad in itself whose badness does not disappear: like polytheism and adultery. 2. Example of bad in itself that admits removal and falling away (removal and falling of badness): like eating carrion, for prohibition is removed during dire need and duress. 3. Example of bad due to another that is not annexed to bad in itself: like fasting on the day of Eid, for it is bad due to abandoning God's hospitality; and like selling at the time of the call to prayer, for it is disapproved and bad due to fear of missing Friday prayer.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and classification. The author begins by stating that the Hanafis, through induction (istiqra), divided actions into good (hasan) and bad (qabih). Good actions are further divided into good in themselves (hasan li-nafsihi) and good due to an external reason (hasan li-ghayrihi). Good-in-itself actions are either those that cannot be overridden (like faith, which remains obligatory even under coercion) or those that can be overridden by preventers (like prayer, which is prohibited in disliked times, during menstruation, etc.). Good-due-to-another actions are of two types: those annexed to good-in-itself (meaning the goodness comes from a cause outside the servant's choice, such as zakat due to the need of the poor, fasting due to subduing the soul, hajj due to glorifying the House of God) and those not annexed (like the goodness of jihad due to the cause of disbelief, hadd due to sin, funeral prayer due to the Muslim's death). Similarly, bad actions are divided into bad in themselves (like polytheism and adultery, whose badness never disappears) and bad due to another (like fasting on Eid due to abandoning God's hospitality, or selling at the time of the call due to fear of missing Friday prayer). Bad-due-to-another is also divided into annexed (like usurpation, which is bad due to the right of the owner) and not annexed. The author then transitions to the question of the absolute command (amr mutlaq) and what it indicates, mentioning the disagreement between Shams al-A'imma (who says it indicates good-in-itself) and the author of Badi' al-Nizam (who says it indicates good-due-to-another). This classification is fundamental in Hanafi usul for understanding the nature of commands and the basis of obligation.

Key Terms
قبيح لعينه (qabih li-aynihi): An action that is bad inherently, with permanent badness.
قبيح لغيره (qabih li-ghayrihi): An action that is bad due to an external reason (e.g., fasting on Eid).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in the text, which of the following best describes the example of a bad action that is qabih li-aynihi and its badness does not disappear?
A) Eating carrion
B) Fasting on Eid
C) Polytheism (shirk)
D) Selling during the Friday call

Key Takeaway
Bad actions are either inherently bad or conditionally bad; conditional badness can be removed when its external cause ceases, a principle that underpins rulings on necessity.

The Rational Proof for Goodness of Commanded Acts in Hanafi Usul
Rational proof that commanded acts have minimal acquired goodness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi argument that a commanded act possesses minimal goodness by implication, and distinguish between intrinsic goodness (husn dhati) and acquired goodness (husn li-ghayrihi).

Core Idea
A commanded act is good because a wise commander cannot command evil — goodness is implied by the command itself.

This lesson unpacks a concise rational argument from the Hanafi usul tradition: why every act that Allah commands must have some goodness. The argument, attributed to the author of al-Badi', uses the premise that the Commander is wise and free from commanding evil. The conclusion is that the commanded act possesses minimal goodness — specifically, 'goodness due to something else' (al-hasan li-ghayrihi). This distinction is foundational for understanding how moral value is assigned to legal obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
What is the author trying to prove? He wants to establish that a commanded act has goodness.
2
Identify the premise
The argument starts with the attribute of the Commander: He is wise and does not command evil (fahsha').
This is a rational premise about divine attributes.
3
Understand the implication (iqtida')
Because the Commander cannot command evil, the command itself implies that the commanded act has some goodness. This is not direct evidence but an inference.
The term 'iqtida'' means implied inclusion.
4
Determine the level of goodness
The goodness established is only to the minimal extent necessary. Hence, it is 'goodness due to something else' (al-hasan li-ghayrihi), not intrinsic goodness.
Intrinsic goodness would be al-hasan li-dhatihi.
5
Apply the distinction
This argument shows that commanded acts derive their moral value from the command, not from inherent properties. This has implications for understanding why some acts are obligatory.
For example, acts like prayer are good because commanded, not necessarily because they are inherently good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ذكر المصنف دليل صاحب "البديع " وقال : لثبوت الحسن في المأمور به اقتضاة، فيثبت (بقدر الضرورة) الأدنى منه، وهو الحسن لغيره . وإنما يثبت الحسن في المأمور به اقتضاة لأن الأمر حكيم، وهو لا يأمر بالفحشاء والمنكر، وإنما يأمر بما فيه الحسن ضرورة، فيتقدر بقدر الضرورة، وتتحقق الضرورة بأدنى مرتبة الحسن، وهو الحسن لغيره .
Then the author mentioned the proof of the author of al-Badi' and said: For the establishment of goodness in the commanded act is by implication, so it is established (by the extent of necessity) the lowest of it, which is goodness due to another. And goodness is established in the commanded act by implication only because the commander is wise, and He does not command obscenity and evil; rather He commands only that in which there is goodness necessarily, so it is measured by the extent of necessity, and necessity is realized by the lowest level of goodness, which is goodness due to another.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a proof argument from the commentary. The author cites the proof of the author of al-Badi' to establish that commanded acts possess goodness. The reasoning is: the command implies the goodness of the commanded act because the Commander is wise and does not command evil. Therefore, the goodness is established by implication (iqtida'), not intrinsically. However, the goodness is only at the minimal level necessary, which is 'goodness due to something else' (hasan li-ghayrihi) — that is, the act is good because it is commanded, not because it is inherently good. This distinguishes between obligatory acts that are intrinsically good (such as gratitude to the Creator) and those that become good due to the command, like certain devotional acts whose rationale is not apparent. In Hanafi usul, this distinction is important for understanding the basis of moral value in law.

Key Terms
الحسن (al-husn): Goodness or beauty of an action from a legal perspective
الحسن لغيره (al-hasan li-ghayrihi): Goodness that an action acquires due to being commanded, not intrinsic
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida'): Implied inclusion; the command implies the goodness of the commanded act

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the act is always physically beneficial.
D) Because the act is recommended by the community.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the goodness of a commanded act is not intrinsic but is implied by the command itself, just enough to avoid evil.

The Hanafi Definition of Hukm
Defining hukm as Allah's address requiring or giving choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hukm, identify its two components (iqtida' and takhyeer), and distinguish a ruling from a non-ruling divine address.

Core Idea
Hukm is Allah's address that requires or permits an action of the mukallaf—nothing else qualifies.

In this lesson, we dissect the Hanafi definition of hukm (legal ruling). The passage from Musallam al-Thubut presents the technical definition as understood in the Hanafi school. The definition is precise: it includes only those divine addresses that impose a requirement or grant a choice regarding the actions of the legally responsible person. Understanding this definition is foundational to all later discussions in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its scope
Recognize that 'al-hukm' is being defined as a technical term in usul al-fiqh. The passage states the definition according to the Hanafi school ('indana).
2
Break down the definition into its components
The definition has two main parts: iqtida' (requirement) and takhyeer (choice). Requirement means the address demands an action or its abandonment; choice means the address leaves the matter optional.
Requirement covers obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and abomination. Choice covers permission.
3
Understanding the requirement component
Requirement (iqtida') is further divided into two: request to do (talab al-fi'l) leading to obligation or recommendation, and request to refrain (talab al-tark) leading to prohibition or abomination.
The passage says 'the first is for obligation and what follows it, the second for prohibition and what follows it.'
4
Understanding the choice component
Choice (takhyeer) means the mukallaf is free to do or leave the action without any demand. This corresponds to permissibility (ibaha).
5
Apply the exclusion clause
The definition excludes addresses that do not relate to the mukallaf's action with requirement or choice, such as verses describing Allah's attributes or actions (e.g., 'And Allah created you'). These are not rulings.
This shows that not every Quranic verse is a legal ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني في الحكم و هو عندنا خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف، اقتضاء أو تخييرا ، فنحو ( والله خلقكم وما تعملون ليس منه . (۱) وقد مر تعريف الخطاب لغةً وصطلاحاً، فلا حاجة إلى اعادته . (۲) والمراد بالتعلق اقتضاء التعلق لطلب الفعل عن المكلّف أو لطلب الترك عنه، فالأول للوجوب وما بعده، والثاني للحرمة وما بعدها . (۳) ومعنى التعلق تخييراً إعطاء الاختيار للمكلف بين أن يفعله أو يتركه، أي ليس اتيانه ضرورياً، ولا تركه لازماً، وهو إشارة إلى الإباحة . فالخطاب في نحو قوله تعالى (والله خلقكم وما تعملون ليس من الحكم، لأنه لا يتعلّق بفعل المكلّف اقتضاء أو تخييراً، وإنما يتعلق بأفعاله إيجاداً.
The second chapter on the ruling. And it is according to us the address of Allah the Most High related to the action of the responsible person, requiring or giving a choice. So like (And Allah created you and what you do) is not from it. (1) The definition of address has already passed lexically and technically, so there is no need to repeat it. (2) And by 'related' is meant requiring: the requirement of requesting the action from the responsible person or requesting its abandonment; the first is for obligation and what follows it, and the second for prohibition and what follows it. (3) And the meaning of 'related as a choice' is giving the responsible person the choice between doing it or leaving it, that is, its performance is not necessary nor its abandonment binding, and it is an indication of permission (ibaha). So the address in the like of His statement (And Allah created you and what you do) is not part of the ruling, because it is not related to the action of the responsible person requiring or giving a choice; rather it is related to His actions in terms of origination.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a formal definition of the technical term 'hukm' (legal ruling) according to the Hanafi school. It begins by stating that 'hukm' is the address of Allah that relates to the actions of the legally responsible person (mukallaf) in one of two ways: either as a requirement (iqtida') or as a choice (takhyeer). Requirement includes both commands (obligation and recommendation) and prohibitions (impermissibility and abomination). Choice is the category of permissibility (ibaha). The definition is then clarified by giving an example of what is not a ruling: the verse 'And Allah created you and what you do' is not a ruling because it describes Allah's creative act, not a directive to the mukallaf. The commentator also clarifies that the word 'address' has already been defined elsewhere, so no repetition is needed. This definition excludes so-called 'declaratory rulings' (ahkam wad'iyya) like conditions, causes, and preventers, which are not directly addressed by the definition. The footnotes provide further elaboration on the meaning of 'relating' as requirement or choice.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): Allah's address related to the actions of the mukallaf requiring or giving a choice
المكلف (al-mukallaf): A legally responsible person, sane and mature
اقتضاء (iqtida'): Requirement; the address demands either doing or not doing
تخيير (takhyeer): Giving choice; the address leaves the matter optional (ibaha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The command to pray (obligation).
B) The prohibition of stealing (prohibition).
C) The verse 'And Allah created you and what you do'.
D) The permission to eat (ibaha).

Key Takeaway
Hukm is specifically Allah's address that commands, prohibits, or permits—excluding descriptive statements about Allah's actions.

Defining Hukm and the First Objection on Wad'i Rulings
Defining hukm and defending its exclusion of wad'i rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of hukm, identify the first objection regarding wad'i rulings, and explain the two responses.

Core Idea
Hukm is Allah's address to the mukallaf as demand or choice; wad'i rulings are included implicitly via general demand.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of hukm in Hanafi usul and addresses a critical objection: does the definition exclude wad'i (declaratory) rulings such as cause, condition, and sign? The author presents the definition and two responses that ensure wad'i rulings are part of the legal framework.

Guided Path
1
State the definition
Identify the three components: address of Allah, relation to mukallaf's action, and the nature (demand or choice).
The definition is from the Hanafi school.
2
Note the excluded example
Explain why verses like 'And Allah created you...' are not hukm because they describe Allah's actions, not a demand or choice for the mukallaf.
3
Present the first objection
The definition is not co-extensive (la yan'akis) because it excludes wad'i rulings like cause, condition, and sign, which have no demand or choice.
This is the first research (al-bahth al-awwal).
4
Explain the first response
Some scholars add the term 'wad'' to the definition, making it: 'as a demand, choice, or declaratory'.
5
Explain the second response
Demand is general (amm) and includes implicit demand, which covers wad'i rulings. For example, the verse 'Establish prayer at the decline of the sun' implicitly demands the condition of time. The objection that stories would then enter is refuted: a story as such has no demand.
This response preserves the definition's simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثاني في الحكم
و هو عندنا خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف، اقتضاء أو تخييرا ، فنحو (والله خلقكم وما تعملون) ليس من الحكم.
-١- تعريف الحكم : وهو عندنا أهل السنة خطاب الله تعالى المتعلّق بفعل المكلف اقتضاء أو تخييراً.
(1) وقد مر تعريف الخطاب لغةً وصطلاحاً، فلا حاجة إلى اعادته . (2) والمراد بالتعلق اقتضاء التعلق لطلب الفعل عن المكلّف أو لطلب الترك عنه، فالأول للوجوب وما بعده، والثاني للحرمة وما بعدها .
(3) ومعنى التعلق تخييراً إعطاء الاختيار للمكلف بين أن يفعله أو يتركه، أي ليس اتيانه ضرورياً، ولا تركه لازماً، وهو إشارة إلى الإباحة .
فالخطاب في نحو قوله تعالى (والله خلقكم وما تعملون) ليس من الحكم، لأنه لا يتعلّق بفعل المكلّف اقتضاء أو تخييراً، وإنما يتعلق بأفعاله إيجاداً.
الأبحاث والإشكالات الأربعة
على تعريف الحكم والجواب عنها
وههنا أبحاث : الأول : أنه لا ينعكس، فإنه يخرج منه الأحكام الوضعية فمنهم من زاد أو وضعاً ومن لم يزد، فتارة يمنع خروجها عن الحد، فإن الاقتضاء أعم من الصريحي والضمني، والقصة من حيث هي لا اقتضاء فيه
و ما في التحرير : أن الوضع مقدم عليه لا يضر، لصدق الأعم، وتارة
يمنع كونها من المحدود ، فإنا لا نسمى حكماً ، وإن سمى غيرنا ولا مشاحة
-١- البحث الأول : أن تعريف الحكم لا ينعكس، أي لا يصدق على جميع أفراد المعرّف (الحكم) كما أنّ المعرّف يصدق على جميع أفراد المعرّف (بكسر الراء) فانه يخرج منه ( من التعريف الأحكام الوضعية كالشرط ، والسبب، والعلامة ونحوها؛ اذليس فيها اقتضاء ولا تخيير؟
والجواب : وقد أجيب عن هذا البحث بأجوبة مختلفة :
١ - فمنهم من زاد في التعريف قيد وضعاً ، أي الحكم خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف اقتضاء أو تخييراً أو وضعاً، ففي الاقتضاء والتخيير إشارة إلى الحكم التكليفي، وفي وضعاً إشارة إلى الحكم الوضعى .
هذا الجواب عند من سلّم الإشكال، ومن لم يسلّم ذلك البحث والإشكال قال المصنف في الجواب عنه ۲ - ومنهم من لم يرد ذلك القيد فتارة قال بمنع خروج الأحكام الوضعية عن التعريف، فإنّ الاقتضاء المذكور في التعريف يدل على اقتضاء وطلب الأحكام الوضعية أيضاً، فانه أعم من الاقتضاء الصريحي والضمني، ففي قوله تعالى (اقم الصلاة لدلوك الشمس) فقد اقتضى الله من المكلف أمرين : الصلاة، وهي حكم تكليفي، وكون الصلاة عند دلوك الشمس والزوال، وهذا حكم وضعي، فصرح بالصلاة وأشار إلى وقتها وهو الزوال، وهذا هو المراد من قوله : فإن الاقتضاء أعم من الصريحي والضمني. ولما ورد على تعميم الاقتضاء إشكال بأنه يلزم منه عدم كون الحد مانعاً عن دخول الغير في التعريف، فإن القصص دخلت في تعريف الحكم؛ لأن في القصة أيضا يكون طلب ضمني عن عدم الإتيان بمثل ما أتى به المجرمون المهلكون . أجاب عنه بقوله : والقصة من حيث هي قصة لا اقتضاء فيها، لا صراحةً ولا ضمنا.
وحاصل الجواب : أن القصّة لها اعتباران
Chapter Two on Hukm (Ruling)
And it is, according to us, the address of Allah Almighty related to the action of the accountable person, as a demand or as a choice. So an example like (And Allah created you and what you do) is not part of hukm.
1 - Definition of Hukm: And it is, according to us Ahl al-Sunnah, the address of Allah Almighty related to the action of the accountable person, as a demand or as a choice.
(1) The definition of address has already been given linguistically and technically, so there is no need to repeat it. (2) What is meant by 'related' is the relation of demanding for the action from the accountable person or demanding for the avoidance from him. The first is for obligation and what follows it, and the second is for prohibition and what follows it.
(3) And the meaning of 'related as a choice' is giving the choice to the accountable person between doing it or leaving it, i.e., its performance is not necessary, nor is its abandonment mandatory. And this is an indication of permissibility.
So the address in the like of His statement (And Allah created you and what you do) is not part of hukm, because it does not relate to the action of the accountable person as a demand or a choice; rather, it relates to His actions as a creation.
The Four Research Points and Objections on the Definition of Hukm and the Responses to Them
And here are research points: The first: That it does not reverse, i.e., it excludes the wad'i (declaratory) rulings. Some added the term 'wad' (declaratory) and some did not. Sometimes one prevents their exclusion from the definition, because demand is more general than explicit and implicit. And a story, as such, has no demand in it.
And what is in al-Tahrir: That the declaratory is not harmful, due to the truth of the more general. And sometimes one prevents that they are among the defined, for we do not call it a ruling, even if others call it so, and there is no dispute.
- 1 - The First Research: That the definition of hukm does not reverse, i.e., it does not apply to all individuals of the defined (hukm), as the definer applies to all individuals of the defined. So it excludes (from the definition) the wad'i rulings like condition, cause, sign, etc., because there is no demand and no choice in them.
And the Response: And it has been answered to this research with different responses:
1 - Some added in the definition the condition 'or declaratory', i.e., hukm is the address of Allah related to the action of the accountable person as a demand, or as a choice, or as a declaratory. So in the demand and choice there is an indication of the taklifi (obligatory) ruling, and in 'declaratory' there is an indication of the wad'i ruling.
This response is for those who accept the problem. And for those who do not accept that research and problem, the author said in response to him:
2 - And some did not adopt that condition. Sometimes one says by preventing the exclusion of wad'i rulings from the definition, because the demand mentioned in the definition indicates the demand of wad'i rulings as well. For it is more general than explicit and implicit demand. In His statement (Establish prayer at the decline of the sun), Allah demanded from the accountable person two things: prayer, which is a taklifi ruling, and that prayer be at the decline of the sun and the zenith, which is a wad'i ruling. So He explicitly mentioned prayer and pointed to its time, which is the zenith. This is what is meant by his saying: 'For demand is more general than explicit and implicit.' And when an objection was raised against generalizing demand that it would necessitate that the definition does not prevent the entry of others into the definition, for stories would enter into the definition of hukm; because in a story there is also an implicit demand not to do like what the criminals who were destroyed did. He responded to it by his saying: The story, as such, has no demand, neither explicitly nor implicitly.
The summary of the response: That the story has two considerations.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines hukm (shari ruling) according to the Hanafi school and addresses a key objection. The author first states the definition: hukm is Allah's address that relates to the action of a morally responsible person (mukallaf) either as a demand (iqtiḍā')—which includes obligation, recommendation, prohibition, and dislike—or as a choice (takhyīr), which indicates permissibility (ibāḥa). He excludes verses like 'And Allah created you and what you do' because they describe Allah's creative acts, not commands or prohibitions for human actions.

The first research (al-baḥth al-awwal) raises the objection that the definition is not co-extensive (lā yan'akis): it excludes wad'i (declaratory) rulings such as cause (sabab), condition (sharṭ), and sign ('alāma), which contain neither demand nor choice. The author presents two responses. First, some scholars add the term 'wad' (declaratory) to the definition, so that wad'i rulings are included as a third category. Second, others argue that wad'i rulings are already included implicitly because 'demand' is general (āmm) and covers both explicit demand (like a direct command) and implicit demand (the indication of a ruling's condition, cause, or sign). For example, the verse 'Establish prayer at the decline of the sun' explicitly demands prayer (taklifi) and implicitly indicates its time (a wad'i ruling). A further objection arises: if implicit demand is accepted, then historical stories (qiṣaṣ) might also be considered hukm because they implicitly warn against wrongdoing. The author responds that a story considered purely as a story has no demand—neither explicit nor implicit—but it may have a secondary, moral dimension. Thus the definition remains sound.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-ḥukm): The address of Allah related to the action of the mukallaf as a demand (iqtiḍā') or a choice (takhyīr) or declaratory (waḍ').
الاقتضاء (al-iqtiḍā'): Demand; a relation in which the address requires an action or avoidance from the mukallaf. It can be explicit (ṣarīḥ) or implicit (ḍimni).
الحكم الوضعي (al-ḥukm al-waḍ‘ī): A declaratory ruling that establishes something as a cause, condition, or impediment for a taklifi ruling, without directly demanding action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first objection (al-bahth al-awwal) raised against the definition of hukm?
A) The definition is too broad and includes non-legal speech.
B) The definition does not include wad'i rulings because they contain no demand or choice.
C) The definition excludes the actions of Allah.
D) The definition includes only obligatory and forbidden acts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hukm is flexible: it can be expanded with 'wad'' or interpreted broadly to include wad'i rulings through the general concept of demand.

Inclusion of Ahkam Wad'iyyah in the Definition of Hukm
Debate on inclusion of declaratory rulings in the definition of hukm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi responses to the objection that the definition of hukm excludes ahkam wad'iyyah and evaluate the rebuttal to Ibn al-Humam's counter-argument.

Core Idea
Declaratory rulings enter the definition of hukm via implicit demand, not temporal precedence.

The definition of hukm (legal ruling) as 'the speech of Allah relating to the action of the legally responsible person by way of demand, choice, or declaratorily' is contested. Some argue that declaratory rulings (ahkam wad'iyyah) like time, cause, or condition do not involve demand or choice and thus fall outside the definition. This lesson explores the Hanafi position that such rulings are indeed included, either by adding 'wad'an' to the definition or by recognizing the generality of demand, including implicit demand (iqtida' dhimni). We will also examine Ibn al-Humam's objection regarding temporal precedence and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the core objection: the definition of hukm does not include ahkam wad'iyyah because they lack demand (iqtida') and choice (takhayyur).
2
First answer: adding 'wad'an'
Understand the first solution: some scholars added the term 'wad'an' (declaratorily) to the definition to explicitly include declaratory rulings.
This is a direct expansion of the definition.
3
Second answer: generality of demand
Analyze the second solution: demand is more general than explicit demand (iqtida' sarihi) and includes implicit demand (iqtida' dhimni), which covers declaratory rulings like the time for prayer implied by the command to pray.
Proof: Quran 17:78 'Aqimi al-salah li-duluk al-shams' demands both prayer and its time.
4
Ibn al-Humam's objection
Examine the counter-argument: implicit demand cannot include time because time (wad'i) precedes the obligation (taklifi), leading to impossibility of demanding something before its cause exists.
This challenges the logical consistency of the second answer.
5
Rebut the objection
Review the Hanafi rebuttal: precedence does not negate inclusion because the general meaning of 'demand' applies irrespective of temporal order. The objection confuses the semantic scope of the term 'demand' with real-world chronology.
The author states: 'Precedence does not harm, due to the truth of the more general.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمنع كونها من المحدود، فإنا لا نسمى حكماً، وإن سمى غيرنا ولا مشاحة.

البحث الأول: أن تعريف الحكم لا ينعكس، أي لا يصدق على جميع أفراد المعرف (الحكم) كما أن المعرّف يصدق على جميع أفراد المعرف (بكسر الراء) فإنه يخرج منه (من التعريف الأحكام الوضعية كالشرط، والسبب، والعلامة ونحوها؛ إذ ليس فيها اقتضاء ولا تخيير.

والجواب: وقد أجيب عن هذا البحث بأجوبة مختلفة:

1 - فمنهم من زاد في التعريف (قيد وضعاً، أي الحكم خطاب الله تعالى المتعلق بفعل المكلف اقتضاء أو تخييراً أو وضعاً، ففي الاقتضاء والتخيير إشارة إلى الحكم التكليفي، وفي (وضعاً) إشارة إلى الحكم الوضعي. هذا الجواب عند من سلّم الإشكال، ومن لم يسلّم ذلك البحث والإشكال قال المصنف في الجواب عنه:

2 - ومنهم من لم يرد (ذلك القيد) فتارة (قال) بمنع خروج الأحكام الوضعية عن التعريف، فإنّ الاقتضاء المذكور في التعريف يدل على اقتضاء وطلب الأحكام الوضعية أيضاً، فإنه أعم من الاقتضاء الصريحي والضمني، ففي قوله تعالى (أقم الصلاة لدلوك الشمس) فقد اقتضى الله من المكلف أمرين: الصلاة، وهي حكم تكليفي، وكون الصلاة عند دلوك الشمس والزوال، وهذا حكم وضعي، فصرح بالصلاة وأشار إلى وقتها وهو الزوال، وهذا هو المراد من قوله: "فإن الاقتضاء أعم من الصريحي والضمني." ولما ورد على تعميم الاقتضاء إشكال بأنه يلزم منه عدم كون الحد مانعاً عن دخول الغير في التعريف، فإن القصص دخلت في تعريف الحكم؛ لأن في القصة أيضاً يكون طلب ضمني عن عدم الإتيان بمثل ما أتى به المجرمون المهلكون. أجاب عنه بقوله: والقصة من حيث هي قصة لا اقتضاء فيها، لا صراحةً ولا ضمناً.

وحاصل الجواب: أن القصة لها اعتباران: فباعتبار أنها إخبار عما وقع في الماضي، وليس فيها لحاظ الزجر والمنع عما فعله العصاة الذين نزل عليهم العذاب لا تكون داخلة تحت الاقتضاء ولا يشملها تعريف الحكم. وباعتبار أنها تكون للزجر والمنع عما فعله المجرمون المعذبون الذين نزلت عليهم العقوبة يشملها الاقتضاء الضمني، وتكون داخلة في تعريف الحكم، وفرد من أفراده، فلا ضرر في تعميم الاقتضاء، بل فيه فائدة؛ لشمول التعريف إياها. وأشار المصنف إلى الحيثية الأولى والاعتبار الأول بقوله: (والقصة من حيث هي قصة لا اقتضاء فيها.)

والجواب عن إشكال ابن الهمام في التحرير:

وقال المصنف في الجواب الثاني عن الاعتراض الأول: فتارة يمنع خروجها عن الحد، فإن الاقتضاء أعم من الصريحي والضمني أي الاقتضاء الضمني يشمل الأحكام الوضعية.

واعترض ابن الهمام في التحرير على هذا التعميم وشمول الاقتضاء الضمني الأحكام الوضعية بأنا لا نسلّم شمول الاقتضاء الضمني الحكم الوضعي، ويلزم اشتمال المقدم (وهو الوقت السبب) على المؤخر (وهو وجوب الصلاة) ويلزم التكليف بالصلاة قبل الوقت، فإن الحكم الوضعي (الوقت) مقدم على الاقتضاء وطلب أداء الصلاة، فوجوب الصلاة متأخر الوجود عن دخول الوقت الذي هو علامة الوجوب وسببه فكيف يستلزم الاقتضاء الذي يطلب به وجوب الصلاة وأدائها الوقت الذي هو مقدم على وجوب الصلاة وأدائها؟

فردّ عليه المصنف بقوله: وما في التحرير لابن الهمام أن تقديم الوضع (الحكم الوضعي) عليه (على الاقتضاء) لا يضر (في اشتمال الاقتضاء الضمني الأحكام الوضعية) وقال في دليل عدم الضرر: لصدق الأعم.

وحاصل الدليل: أن الاقتضاء على نوعين: اقتضاء خاص، وهو الصريحي
It prevents it from being among the defined (al-mahdud), for we do not call it a ruling (hukm), although others may call it, and there is no dispute over terminology.

First investigation: That the definition of hukm is not convertible (inverse), i.e., it does not apply to all individuals of the defined (al-mu'arraf) as the definer applies to all individuals of the defined. For it excludes from the definition the declaratory rulings (al-ahkam al-wad'iyyah) such as condition, cause, sign, and the like, because they contain neither demand nor choice.

The answer: This investigation has been answered with various answers:

1. Some added to the definition the restriction 'wad'an' (declaratorily), i.e., hukm is the speech of Allah Most High relating to the action of the legally responsible person by way of demand, choice, or declaratorily. So in 'demand' and 'choice' there is a reference to the prescriptive ruling (al-hukm al-taklifi), and in 'declaratorily' a reference to the declaratory ruling. This answer is for those who accept the problem. As for those who do not accept that investigation and problem, the author said in answer:

2. Some did not accept that restriction. Sometimes they denied the exclusion of declaratory rulings from the definition, because the demand mentioned in the definition indicates a demand and request for declaratory rulings as well. For it is more general than explicit demand and implicit demand. In His statement (Establish prayer at the decline of the sun), Allah demanded from the morally responsible two things: prayer, which is a prescriptive ruling, and the fact that prayer is at the decline of the sun and midday, which is a declaratory ruling. So He explicitly mentioned prayer and indicated its time, which is midday. This is what is meant by his statement: "For demand is more general than explicit and implicit." When an objection was raised against the generalization of demand, that it would necessitate that the definition not be a preventer of the entry of others into the definition, because stories (al-qisas) would enter into the definition of hukm, since in the story there is also an implicit demand not to do like what the criminals did who were destroyed, he answered with his statement: "The story, inasmuch as it is a story, has no demand in it, neither explicitly nor implicitly."

The summary of the answer: The story has two aspects. Insofar as it is a report about what happened in the past, and there is no consideration of deterrence and prohibition from what the sinners, upon whom punishment descended, did, it is not included under demand and the definition of hukm does not cover it. Insofar as it is for deterrence and prohibition from what the criminals, who were punished, did, implicit demand covers it and it enters into the definition of hukm, being one of its individuals. So there is no harm in generalizing demand; rather there is benefit in it, because the definition includes it. The author pointed to the first aspect and the first consideration with his statement: "And the story, inasmuch as it is a story, has no demand in it."

And the answer to the objection of Ibn al-Humam in al-Tahrir:

The author said in the second answer to the first objection: Sometimes he denies its exclusion from the definition, for demand is more general than explicit and implicit, i.e., implicit demand includes declaratory rulings.

Ibn al-Humam in al-Tahrir objected to this generalization and the inclusion of declaratory rulings by implicit demand, saying: We do not accept that implicit demand includes the declaratory ruling. It would necessitate that the antecedent (which is time, the cause) includes the consequent (which is the obligation of prayer), and it would necessitate obligation of prayer before the time. For the declaratory ruling (time) precedes the demand and request to perform prayer. So the obligation of prayer is later in existence than the entry of time, which is the sign of obligation and its cause. So how can the demand, which seeks the obligation and performance of prayer, include the time that precedes the obligation and performance of prayer?

The author replied to him with his statement: What is in al-Tahrir of Ibn al-Humam, that the precedence of the declaratory ruling over it (over demand) does not harm (the inclusion of declaratory rulings by implicit demand). And he said in proof of the absence of harm: due to the truth of the more general.

Summary of the proof: Demand is of two types: specific demand, which is explicit...
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage addresses the objection that the definition of hukm (legal ruling) does not include ahkam wad'iyyah (declaratory rulings) because they lack demand or choice. The author presents two answers. The first adds 'wad'an' to the definition. The second argues that demand is general and includes implicit demand, which covers declaratory rulings. An objection from Ibn al-Humam is rebutted by noting that 'precedence' does not prevent inclusion because the general term 'demand' applies. The author concludes by distinguishing between explicit and implicit demand.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): The speech of Allah Most High relating to the action of the legally responsible person by way of demand, choice, or declaratorily.
الاقتضاء الصريحي (al-iqtida' al-sarihi): Explicit demand that is clearly stated, such as the command to pray.
الاقتضاء الضمني (al-iqtida' al-dhimni): Implicit demand that is understood from context, such as the indication of time from the command to pray.
الحكم التكليفي (al-hukm al-taklifi): A ruling that involves demand or choice regarding an action, such as obligation or prohibition.
الحكم الوضعي (al-hukm al-wad'i): A ruling that sets conditions, causes, or signs without direct demand or choice, like the time for prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second answer in the passage, how do declaratory rulings (ahkam wad'iyyah) enter into the definition of hukm?
A) By adding the word 'wad'an' to the definition.
B) Because they contain explicit demand (iqtida' sarihi).
C) Through implicit demand (iqtida' dhimni) which is more general.
D) They are not included in the definition at all.

Key Takeaway
Declaratory rulings are included in the definition of hukm because the term 'demand' is general enough to encompass both explicit and implicit demand, as demonstrated by the Qur'anic command to pray at the decline of the sun.

The Child's Actions and the Definition of Hukm
Objection and Hanafi response on child's rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi response to the objection that a child's actions fall under the definition of hukm, by distinguishing between direct address (khitab) and mediated rulings.

Core Idea
A child's actions are not directly addressed; rulings flow via guardian or reason.

The definition of hukm as 'related to the action of a mukallaf' seems to exclude a child's actions. But objections arise: a child's prayer is recommended, his sale valid, and obligations on his dhimma. How can these rulings not relate to the child? The Hanafi response defends the definition by shifting the subject—either to the guardian or to rational conformity. This lesson unpacks that defense and its implications.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
Identify the specific rulings on a child's actions that appear to contradict the definition: mandubiyya of prayer, validity of sale, obligations on dhimma.
The objector uses these to show the definition is not comprehensive.
2
Examine the Hanafi response
The response has two parts: (1) Rulings like mandub and obligation relate to the guardian, not the child. (2) Validity (sihha) of sale is a rational matter, not a revealed ruling.
Note the key concept: 'No address (khitab) to the child.'
3
Analyze the guardian's role
The address (khitab) is to the guardian: incitement (tahrid) to make the child pray, reward for the guardian, obligation to pay on behalf of the child.
Thus, the ruling still relates to a mukallaf (the guardian).
4
Understand rational validity
The child's sale is valid because it conforms to the permitted sale authorized by the guardian. This conformity is known by reason, not by a revealed command.
Rational validity is not a hukm shari in the same sense.
5
Recognize the subtlety
The author hints that the child is rewarded for his prayer but not punished. This suggests some relation to the child, but the Hanafi response maintains the definition by focusing on the subject of the address.
This nuance shows the complexity of the dispute.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : الحد منقوض بأحكام أفعال الصبي من مندوبية صلاته، وصحة بيعه ووجوب الحقوق المالية في ذمته أولاً، وأجيب بأنه لاخطاب للصبي، وإنما للولي ،التحريض، وله الثواب، وعليه الأداء، والصحة أمر عقلي، لأنها تتم بالمطابقة، وفيه مافيه - البحث الثالث: أن الحد (تعريف الحكم) منقوض غير جامع لأفراده) بسبب خروج (أحكام أفعال الصبي من مندوبية صلاته، وصحة بيعه، ووجوب الحقوق المالية في ذمته أولاً ووجوب أداء الولى عنه (ثانياً) كثمن المبيع الذي اشتراه وكنفقة زوجته. ومنشأ النقض هو قول المصنف المتعلق بفعل المكلف) فإن الندب والصحة ووجوب الحقوق هنا متعلقه بافعال الضي غير المكلف. وأجيب عن هذا البحث والاعتراض) بأنه لا خطاب للضبى. و حاصل الجواب : أن الخطاب (أي الحكم لا يتعلق بفعل الضبي، بل إنما يتعلق بفعل وليه، فلا يتعلق بفعل الصبي حكم، حتى يلزم خروجه عن تعريف و يصير التعريف منقوضاً و من أجل هذا قال المصنف : وإنما للولى الذى يتعلق الحكم بفعله التحريض (تحريض الصبي على الصلاة) وله الثواب، وعليه الأداء (أداء الحقوق المالية الواجبة على الضيي). وأما الصحة (صحة بيعه) فأمر عقلى يعرف بالعقل دون الشرع والخطاب، لأنها تتم بالمطابقة، أي لأن صحة بيع الضبي إنما تتم بسبب موافقته البيع الشرعي الذي كان بأمر وليه، وإنما يُعلم موافقته البيع الشرعي المأذون فيه بالعقل، وهذا هو وجه كون صحة بيعه عقلياً، والا فنفس كونه بيعاً ومصداقاً لقوله تعالى ((وأحل الله البيع)) يمنع كونه حكماً عقلياً فقط. قوله : وفيه ما فيه : أي وفي ذلك الجواب ما فيه من الإشكال الذى اشار إليه المصنف في تعليقه بقوله : إشارة إلى ما قيل إن صلوة الصين مما يثاب هو به، ولا يعاقب
Third: The definition is vitiated (breached) by the rulings on the actions of a child: the recommendability (mandubiyya) of his prayer, the validity (sihha) of his sale, and the obligation of financial rights in his debt (dhimma) first. And it was answered: There is no address to the child; rather, to the guardian: incitement, and for him is reward, and upon him is performance. And validity is a rational matter because it is achieved by conformity. And there is something in it. - The third discussion: The definition (of hukm) is vitiated, not comprehensive of its individuals, because the rulings on the actions of a child are excluded: the mandubiyya of his prayer, the validity of his sale, and the obligation of financial rights in his debt first, and the obligation of the guardian to pay on his behalf second, like the price of the sold item he purchased and the maintenance of his wife. The source of the vitiation is the author's statement 'related to the actions of the responsible person (mukallaf).' Indeed, these rulings (mandub, sihha, obligation) are related to the actions of the child, who is not mukallaf. And it was answered: There is no address to the child. The summary of the answer: The address (i.e., the ruling) does not relate to the action of the child; rather, it relates to the action of his guardian. So no ruling relates to the child's action, such that its departure from the definition would occur and the definition would be vitiated. For this reason, the author said: To the guardian, whose action the ruling relates, is incitement (to encourage the child to pray), and for him is reward, and upon him is performance (of the financial rights obligatory on the child). As for validity (the validity of his sale), it is a rational matter known by reason, not by revelation and address, because it is achieved by conformity. That is, the validity of the child's sale is only achieved by its conformity to the prescribed sale that was by the command of his guardian. And its conformity to the permitted prescribed sale is known by reason. This is the way his sale is rational. Otherwise, the fact that it is a sale and an instance of the verse 'And Allah has permitted trade' prevents it from being only a rational ruling. His statement: 'There is something in it' indicates that there is an issue in that answer, which the author referred to in his commentary: 'An indication of what has been said: the child's prayer is something by which he is rewarded and not punished.'
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the definition of hukm as being related to the actions of a mukallaf (responsible person). The objector points out that there are rulings applied to a child's actions: his prayer is considered recommended (mandub), his sale is considered valid (sahih), and financial obligations may be imposed on his behalf (like the price of something he bought). These rulings seem to relate directly to the child, yet the child is not a mukallaf. The answer is that the rulings do not actually relate to the child's actions; rather, they relate to the actions of the guardian (wali). For example, the mandubiyya of the child's prayer is actually an incitement (tahrid) for the guardian to encourage the child; the reward goes to the guardian, and the guardian is obligated to perform the financial duties on behalf of the child. As for the validity (sihha) of the child's sale, it is a rational matter determined by compatibility with the prescribed sale that the guardian permitted. Thus, the definition remains intact because the rulings are linked to the guardian's actions, not the child's. However, the author notes that there is an issue with this answer: if the child is not addressed, how can he be rewarded for his prayer? The commentator indicates that the child's prayer is rewarded, but he is not punished for abandoning it. This is a subtle point that requires further consideration.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A child who has not reached puberty, not a mukallaf.
التحريض (al-tahrid): Incitement or encouragement directed to the guardian to make the child perform acts of worship.
الصحة العقلية (al-sihha al-'aqliyya): Validity determined by reason, based on conformity to a prescribed form.
المكلف (al-mukallaf): The legally responsible person who is addressed by divine commands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the third objection, who is the actual subject of the ruling (khitab) when it comes to a child's prayer being mandub?
A) The child who prays.
B) The guardian who is encouraged to make the child pray.
C) Allah who commands.
D) The community.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi response defends the definition of hukm by showing that rulings on a child's actions are either addressed to the guardian or are rational determinations, preserving the link to a mukallaf.

The Definition of Hukm and Children's Actions: A Hanafi Defense
Refuting an objection that the definition of hukm excludes children's actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi response to the objection that the definition of hukm excludes children's actions and apply the distinction between legal rulings and rational validity.

Core Idea
The legal ruling attaches to the guardian's action, not the child's; child's sale validity is rational, not a legal ruling.

In this passage from the commentary on Musallam al-Thubut, we encounter a standard objection to the Hanafi definition of hukm. The objector argues that children's actions have legal attributes like recommendation and validity, so they should be included. The commentator provides a layered Hanafi response, distinguishing between direct rulings and rational validity, and later considers the issue of reward for children. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of the objection and the sophisticated defense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection and its basis
List the examples the objector uses (child's prayer, sale, financial obligations) and note that the definition 'khatab Allah al-muta'alliq bi fi'l al-mukallaf' seems to exclude children.
The objector assumes every shar'i attribute must correspond to a direct ruling on the actor.
2
State the Hanafi initial response
The commentator asserts that no legal address (khatab) reaches the child; the ruling attaches to the guardian's action, not the child's.
Thus, the definition remains comprehensive because the guardian is mukallaf.
3
Distinguish rational validity from legal ruling
The validity (sihha) of a child's sale is rational (aqli), not a legal ruling. It is based on correspondence with a permitted template, known by reason.
This preserves the definition: rational validity does not need to be a hukm shar'i.
4
Examine the further refinement: reward and potential mukallaf
The commentator addresses the problem of reward for the child's prayer: reward is not contingent on taklif; Allah rewards any good deed. Alternatively, 'mukallaf' in the definition includes potential mukallaf (the child will become mukallaf).
This reinforces the definition without conceding direct rulings on children.
5
Evaluate the strength of the defense
Assess how each objection is rebutted and whether the Hanafi definition remains intact. Note that the response uses both rational and textual arguments (reward, potentiality).
The thamarah al-khilaf is the scope of hukm: Hanafi restricts to actual/potential mukallaf.

The third discussion: That the definition (definition of legal ruling) is defective, not comprehensive of its instances, due to the exclusion of the rulings of the actions of the child: the recommendability of his prayer, the validity of his sale, and the obligation of financial rights in his liability firstly, and the obligation of the guardian's performance on his behalf secondly, like the price of the item he bought and the maintenance of his wife. The source of the objection is the author's statement 'related to the action of the mukallaf,' for recommendation, validity, and obligation here are attached to the actions of the non-mukallaf child. It was answered regarding this discussion and objection that there is no address for the child. The summary of the answer: The address (i.e., the ruling) does not attach to the action of the child, but rather attaches to the action of his guardian. Thus no ruling attaches to the child's action, so that its exclusion from the definition would cause the definition to be defective. For this reason the author said: The guardian, to whose action the ruling attaches, has the incitement (to incite the child to prayer), and he has the reward, and upon him is the performance (of the financial rights obligatory on the child). As for validity (validity of his sale), it is a rational matter known by reason, not by law and address, because it is achieved by correspondence; i.e., the validity of the child's sale is only achieved due to its agreement with the lawful sale that was by the command of his guardian, and its agreement with the permitted lawful sale is only known by reason. This is the aspect of its validity being rational. Otherwise, the fact that it is a sale and an instance of His statement 'And Allah has permitted trade' prevents it from being merely a rational ruling. His statement: 'There is something in it'—that is, in that answer there is a problem to which the author pointed in his commentary, saying: An indication of what was said: The child's prayer is something for which he is rewarded and not punished for its abandonment; so how is his action not recommended? The statement that the child has no reward at all is extremely far-fetched; how? It would necessitate that the prayer of a child who has no guardian is futile. The most apparent is to say: The occurrence of reward is not contingent on being legally responsible (being a praying person who is mukallaf); rather, His custom, exalted is He, is that He does not waste the reward of anyone who does good work, be they child or adult. And it is possible that his statement 'There is something in it' is an indication of the original answer to the original objection: that the mukallaf (the actual) in the definition of legal ruling is more general than being mukallaf actually or potentially (with consideration of what he will become).
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is the third discussion (al-bahth al-thalith) in the commentary, addressing an objection against the definition of hukm (legal ruling) given by the author of Musallam al-Thubut. The objector argues that the definition 'khatab Allah al-muta'alliq bi fi'l al-mukallaf' is not comprehensive because it excludes rulings that apply to actions of non-mukallaf individuals, specifically children. Examples given: the recommendability (nadb) of a child's prayer, the validity (sihha) of his sale, and the obligation (wujub) of financial rights on his liability with the guardian performing them. The commentator responds that the legal address (khatab) does not directly attach to the child's action; rather, it attaches to the action of the guardian. Thus no ruling is excluded from the definition. The incitement and reward belong to the guardian, and the guardian must fulfill financial obligations on behalf of the child. Regarding the validity of the child's sale, the commentator explains this is a rational matter, not a legal ruling, because validity is determined by correspondence with a permitted sale, which reason can discern. The commentator then addresses a subtle problem: if the child's prayer is said to be recommended (mandub), how can it be rational? He suggests reward is not contingent on being mukallaf; Allah rewards any good deed. Alternatively, the definition's 'mukallaf' includes potential mukallaf (one who will become mukallaf), thereby including the child. The passage follows the order: objection, response, and further refinement.

Key Terms
الحكم (al-hukm): The address of Allah related to the actions of the mukallaf
المكلف (al-mukallaf): A legally responsible person (adult, sane, aware of the address)
الندب (al-nadb): Recommendation; an action whose performance is rewarded but not mandatory
الصحة (al-sihha): Validity; the act conforms to the legal requirements

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The child himself
B) The guardian
C) The imam
D) No one; it's not a legal ruling

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of hukm is preserved because children's actions are not directly addressed; rulings attach to the guardian and validity is rational.

Extending Hukm to Potential Mukallaf
Answering an objection about reward for a child's act

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi definition of hukm includes potential legal responsibility (future majority) and apply it to the case of a child's rewarded acts.

Core Idea
A child's act is rewarded even though not mandatory—hukm extends to potential mukallaf.

This passage answers a key objection: if a child is not mukallaf, can his acts be considered legally evaluated? The author clarifies that the definition of hukm in Hanafi usul includes those who are potentially responsible, i.e., will become mukallaf. This expansion allows a child's good acts to be rewarded and certain rulings to apply to them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection states that since a child (sabi) is not mukallaf, his acts cannot be subject to hukm (e.g., considered mandub or rewarded).
This objection is implied by the phrase 'فكيف لا يكون فعله مندوباً'.
2
State the author's refutation
The author refutes by noting that reward is not contingent on being mukallaf; Allah's custom is to reward good deeds regardless of age.
The phrase 'جرت عادته تعالى بأن لا يضيع أجر من أحسن عملاً' is key.
3
Understand the expansion of 'mukallaf'
The author interprets the matn's 'فيه ما فيه' to indicate that 'mukallaf' in the definition of hukm includes both actual and potential responsibility (bi'l-fi'l wa bi'l-quwwa).
This is a broadening of the term to cover those who will become responsible.
4
Apply the principle
Thus, a child's valid acts, like salah, are subject to hukm (e.g., they are rewarded), even though obligations are suspended until majority.
This leads to the ruling that a child's good deeds are rewarded.
5
Recognize the usul implication
This expansion changes the scope of the definition: hukm is not restricted to actual mukallaf but includes potential mukallaf.
This is a typical Hanafi approach to make the definition more inclusive.

The Quran as Establisher (Muthbit) and Other Proofs as Revealers (Kashif)
Explaining why Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas are kashif, not muthbit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Quran's wording as muthbit and Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas as kashif, and explain the Hanafi rationale based on kalam nafsi.

Core Idea
The Quran's very words are treated as Allah's address; other proofs only reveal that address.

In defining hukm shari, the Hanafis restrict it to the direct address (khitab) of Allah. This raises a question: aren't rulings from Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas also hukm? The answer lies in the distinction between proofs that establish (muthbit) and proofs that reveal (kashif). This lesson unpacks that distinction and addresses a key objection about why the Quran's wording itself is not considered a revealer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
State why Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas are excluded from the definition of hukm according to the Hanafis.
They are not the direct address of Allah.
2
Understand the answer
Recognize that these proofs are revealers (kashif) of the Quran's address; the ruling ultimately comes from the Quran.
What is established by them is established by the Quran.
3
Recognize the objection
Consider the objection: why isn't the Quran's own wording also a revealer, since it reveals internal speech (kalam nafsi)?
This seems like an arbitrary preference.
4
Grasp the Hanafi response
Understand that the Quran's wording is treated as identical to the internal speech, so it is given the ruling of the signified and considered an establisher (muthbit), not a revealer.
The signifier is as if it is the signified.
5
Note the subordination point
Observe the note about Qiyas being 'more explicit in subordination' compared to Sunnah and Ijma.
This indicates a spectrum among the revealers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إنه يخرج من الحكم) ماثبت بالأصول الثلثة غير الكتاب والجواب أنها كاشفة عن الخطاب، فالثابت بها ثابت به وأما عدم نظم القرآن منه ( من كاشف الخطاب ( مع أنه كاشف عن الكلام النفسي فلأن الدال كأنه المدلول وما عن الحنفية أن القياس مظهر بخلاف السنة والإجماع فمبنى على أنه أصرح في الفرعية، فتأمل جواب إشكال يرد على كون الثلاثة كاشفة عن خطاب الله تعالى وحاصل الإشكال أنك جعلت الثلاثة السنة والاجماع والقياس) كاشفة عن خطاب الله تعالى، ولم تجعل نظم القرآن (وألفاظه المترتبة المتناسقة كاشفاً، بل جعلت نظم القرآن مثبتاً للأحكام وهذا ترجيح بلا مرجح 10. فاجاب عنه بقوله : وأما عدم عد نظم القرآن من الكاشف عن خطاب الله مثل الأصول الثلاثة) مع أنه (نظم) (القرآن) كاشف عن الكلام النفسى الدال على الأحكام، والمثبت لها، فلأنّ النظم الدال على الكلام النفسى هو عين المدلول ، أعني الكلام النفسي الذي هو خطاب الله تعالى المتعلّق بفعل المكلف، فاعطى للدال (نظم) (القرآن) حكم المدلول وهو الكلام النفسي ، فجعل الدال (نظم القرآن) مثبتاً لا كاشفاً، بخلاف الأدلة الثلاثة، فانها لجعلت كاشفةً ، لا مثبتةً .
The fourth: It excludes from the ruling what is established by the three sources other than the Book. The answer: They are revealers of the address, so what is established by them is established by it. As for the lack of considering the wording of the Quran as a revealer of the address, while it reveals the internal speech, it is because the signifier is as if it is the signified. And what is reported from the Hanafis that analogy is a revealer, unlike the Sunnah and Ijma, it is based on it being more explicit in subordination. Consider. An objection raised on considering the three as revealers of Allah's address. The summary: You made the three (Sunnah, Ijma, Qiyas) revealers of Allah's address, and you did not make the wording of the Quran a revealer, rather you made the wording of the Quran an establisher of rulings, and this is a preference without a preference. So he answered: As for not counting the wording of the Quran among the revealers of Allah's address like the three sources, while its wording reveals the internal speech that indicates rulings and establishes them, it is because the wording that indicates the internal speech is identical to the signified, namely the internal speech which is Allah's address related to the act of the responsible person. So the wording is given the ruling of the signified (the internal speech), so the wording of the Quran is made an establisher, not a revealer, unlike the three proofs, for they are made revealers, not establishers.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is the fourth investigation (bahth) of the commentary. The original Musallam al-Thubut definition of hukm excludes rulings established by Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas because they are not directly the address (khitab) of Allah. The commentator explains that these three are actually revealers (kashif) of the address; the ruling is ultimately from the Quran. An objection arises: Why isn't the wording (nazm) of the Quran considered a revealer, like those three? After all, the wording indicates the internal speech (kalam nafsi) which is the actual address. The answer is that the wording is so closely tied to the internal speech that it is treated as the same: the wording is the signified (i.e., the address itself), so it is considered an establisher (muthbit) of the ruling, not merely a revealer. In contrast, Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas are separate from the Quranic wording, so they function as revealers. A subtle point: the Hanafi position that Qiyas is a 'muzhir' (revealer) is mentioned, but the commentator notes it is more explicit in subordination ('أصرح في الفرعية'). This highlights the Hanafi distinction between the Quran as direct speech and other proofs as indirect indicators.

Key Terms
كاشف (kashif): A proof that reveals the ruling of the Quran.
مثبت (muthbit): A proof that directly establishes the ruling; applied to the Quran's wording because it is identical to internal speech.
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi): The internal speech of Allah, which is the real address.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it is ambiguous and requires interpretation
D) Because it is not a proof for rulings

Key Takeaway
The Quran's wording is the direct address of Allah (muthbit); Sunnah, Ijma, and Qiyas are only revealers (kashif) of that address.

Objection to the Term Khitab in the Definition of Hukm
Objecting that the term khitab in the definition does not apply to eternal speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the objection to the term 'khitab' in the definition of hukm and explain its implication for the definition's validity.

Core Idea
The definition of hukm fails if khitab excludes Allah's eternal speech.

In the Hanafi definition of hukm, the term 'khitab' (address) is used. However, there is a subtle disagreement: does this term apply to Allah's eternal internal speech (kalam nafsi) or only to the revealed words? This passage presents an objection that the definition is invalid due to this ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate the term being challenged: 'khitab' in the definition of hukm. The objection is that there is disagreement (khilaf) in calling Allah's eternal speech 'khitab'.
2
Understand the two interpretations of khitab
The text offers two definitions: 'ma yufham' (that by which understanding occurs) and 'ma yafhim' (that which makes understand). The first applies to eternal speech, the second only to revealed words.
This distinction is subtle but crucial.
3
See the effect on the definition
If khitab is taken as 'ma yafhim', then eternal speech is not khitab, so the definition does not cover rulings in eternity. If as 'ma yufham', it does cover them.
4
Recognize the consequence for validity
Because of this disagreement (khilaf), the definition of hukm using khitab is not universally valid. The commentator notes that the author acknowledged this with the phrase 'thumma fi tasmiyati al-kalami fi al-azali khitaban khilaf'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان اعتراض آخر على تعريف الحكم ثم في تسمية الكلام في الأزل خطاباً خلاف والحق أنه إن فر بما يفهم كان خطابا فيه، وإن فُسَر بما أفهم لم يكن (خطاباً فيه) بل فيما لا يزال، ويبتنى عليه أنه حكم في الأزل أو فيما لا يزال وهو أن في تسمية الكلام النفسى في الأزل خطاباً خلافاً، فلا يصح تعريف الحكم به، وفي عبارة المصنف : "ثم في تسمية الكلام في الأزل خطاباً خلاف إشارة إلى هذا
Statement of another objection on the definition of hukm. Then, there is disagreement in calling the speech in eternity 'khitab'. The truth is that if it is interpreted as 'that by which understanding occurs' (yufham), then it is khitab in that sense; and if interpreted as 'that which makes understand' (yafhim), then it is not khitab in that sense, but rather in the future. Based on this, it is a ruling in eternity or in the future. And this is that there is disagreement in calling the internal speech in eternity 'khitab', so the definition of hukm by it is not valid. And in the author's expression: 'Then, in naming the speech in eternity khitab, there is disagreement' is an indication of this.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents another objection to the definition of hukm. The definition uses 'khitab' (address) of Allah, but does this apply to the eternal speech (kalam nafsi) or only to speech sent down? The author notes a disagreement: if khitab is defined as 'that by which something is understood' (ma yufhim), it applies to eternal speech because it is inherently understandable; if defined as 'that which makes understand' (ma yafhim), it applies only to the actual words revealed. Consequently, the ruling (hukm) could be considered eternal or temporal. The objection is that the definition is not valid due to this ambiguity. The commentator points out that the original author (Sahib al-Musallam) acknowledged this disagreement with the phrase 'ثم في تسمية الكلام في الأزل خطاباً خلاف'. This is a subtle usul point about the nature of divine speech.

Key Terms
خطاب (khitab): Address of Allah; disagree whether it applies to eternal internal speech or only to the revealed words.
كلام نفسي (kalam nafsi): Internal speech of Allah, existing from eternity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Khitab only applies to the Quranic text
D) Khitab is not a term used in usul

Key Takeaway
The definition of hukm using 'khitab' is contested because it is ambiguous regarding Allah's eternal speech.
