The Dispute Over Khitab: Defining Address in Pre-Eternity
Analysis of whether kalam nafsi in pre-eternity is called khitab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to IDENTIFY the two definitions of khitab and explain how each affects the temporal status of the hukm in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Whether khitab is defined as 'understood' or 'causing understanding' determines if the hukm is eternal or contingent.

This passage addresses a subtle but impactful dispute within the Hanafi school regarding the nature of the divine address (khitab) in relation to the internal speech (kalam nafsi). The resolution of this nominal disagreement carries direct consequences for whether the hukm (legal ruling) is considered pre-eternal or contingent, thereby affecting the very definition of hukm in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of khitab
Pinpoint the two contrasting definitions presented in the passage: khitab as 'speech whose meaning is understood' and khitab as 'speech that causes understanding'.
The Arabic terms are 'yufham' (understood) and 'yufhim' (causes understanding).
2
Map each definition to its consequence for kalam nafsi
For each definition, determine whether kalam nafsi qualifies as khitab in pre-eternity. Under the first definition, it does; under the second, it does not because there are no addressees.
This step is purely definitional – no proof citations needed.
3
Trace the thamarah: impact on hukm
Connect the dispute to the temporal status of the hukm. If khitab is eternal, then the ruling (hukm) is eternal; if khitab is not eternal, then the ruling is contingent on the existence of addressees.
This is the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) that matters for usul.
4
Understand the author's resolution
The author states that both definitions are valid (al-haqq). No definitive preference is given; instead, the truth lies in acknowledging the spectrum of views.
Recognise that legitimate ikhtilaf is part of Hanafi scholarship.
5
Apply to the definition of hukm
Consider why definitions of hukm that use the term 'khitab' must be carefully worded to avoid ambiguity. This illustrates the care needed in usul terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم في تسمية الكلام في الأزل خطاباً خلاف والحق أنه إن فر بما يفهم كان خطابا فيه، وإن فُسَر بما أفهم لم يكن (خطاباً فيه) بل فيما لا يزال، ويبتنى عليه أنه حكم في الأزل أو فيما لا يزال وهو أن في تسمية الكلام النفسى في الأزل خطاباً خلافاً، فلا يصح تعريف الحكم به، وفي عبارة المصنف : "ثم في تسمية الكلام في الأزل خطاباً خلاف إشارة إلى هذا الاعتراض، ثم اشار إلى الجواب بقوله : " والحق أنه (أن الخطاب إن قشر بما ( بالكلام الذى) يُفهم معناه (في الحال والمآل ) كان الكلام خطاباً فيه (في الأزل وفيهما لايزال) أي في الماضي الذي لا ابتداء له ، وفي المستقبل الذي لا نهاية له. وإن فسر الخطاب بما بالكلام الذى افهم أي أوقع به الفهم في الماضى أو الحال لم يكن الكلام خطاباً في الأزل لعدم وجود المخاطبين في الأزل، بل يكون الكلام خطاباً فيما لا يزال في المستقبل غير المتناهي) عند وجود المخاطبين . و يبتنى عليه ( على هذا الخلاف) أنه ( أن الكلام النفسي) حكم في الأزل أو فيما لا يزال، فمن يقول إن الكلام النفسي خطاب أزلى، يقول : ان الكلام النفسى أيضاً حكم أزلى، ومن قال انه (الكلام) خطاب فيما لا يزال، قال : الكلام النفسي أيضاً حكم فيما لا يزال.
Then there is a dispute regarding calling speech in pre-eternity 'address'. The truth is that if it is interpreted as 'what is understood', then it is an address in it; if it is interpreted as 'what causes understanding', then it is not an address in it, but rather in what will be. And upon this depends whether it is a ruling in pre-eternity or in what will be. And it is that there is a dispute in calling the internal speech in pre-eternity 'address', so the definition of hukm cannot be sound with it. In the author's expression: 'Then there is a dispute regarding calling speech in pre-eternity address' indicates this objection. Then he pointed to the answer by saying: The truth is that if address is interpreted as 'speech whose meaning is understood' (in the present and the future), then the speech is an address in it (in pre-eternity and in what will be), i.e., in the past that has no beginning and in the future that has no end. If address is interpreted as 'speech that causes understanding' (i.e., brings about understanding in the past or present), then the speech is not an address in pre-eternity due to the absence of addressees in pre-eternity, but rather the speech is an address in what will be (in the infinite future) when addressees exist. And upon this dispute depends that the internal speech is a ruling in pre-eternity or in what will be. So whoever says that internal speech is an eternal address says that internal speech is also an eternal ruling; and whoever says that it is an address in what will be says that internal speech is also a ruling in what will be.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage discusses a key usul dispute about the nature of the divine address (khitab) and its relationship to the internal speech (kalam nafsi) and the ruling (hukm). The issue is whether kalam nafsi in pre-eternity (azal) can be called 'khitab' (address). The author presents two interpretations: if khitab is defined as 'speech that is understood' (yufham), then it can be said that the internal speech is an address in pre-eternity because it is understandable, even if no one understands it yet. If khitab is defined as 'speech that causes understanding' (yufhim), then it is not an address in pre-eternity because there are no addressees to receive it; rather it becomes address when addressees exist in the future. This dispute has a direct consequence for the temporal status of the hukm (legal ruling). Some Hanafis hold that the ruling is pre-eternal (azali) because the kalam nafsi is eternal address; others hold that the ruling is not-eternal (huduth) because the address depends on the existence of addressees. This affects the definition of hukm: if it is defined as 'speech of Allah concerning the actions of the obligated persons', then using 'khitab' in the definition must be unambiguous. The author clarifies the truth (haqq) that both interpretations are viable, leading to different conclusions about the eternity of the hukm.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي (al-kalam al-nafsi): The internal speech of Allah, which is the attribute of speech subsisting in the divine essence.
خطاب (khitab): Speech addressed to someone; here it is disputed whether it can be called address in pre-eternity.
الأزل (al-azal): Pre-eternity, the timeless past with no beginning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only in pre-eternity.
B) Only in the future when addressees exist.
C) In both pre-eternity and the future.
D) Only in the past.

Key Takeaway
The definition of khitab determines whether the hukm is seen as eternal or contingent; Hanafis accept both as valid interpretations.

The Five Legal Rulings: A Hanafi Classification
Definition of the five hukm categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the five legal rulings (wujub, nadb, tahrim, karaha, ibaha) based on the type of demand (iqtida) in the divine address and apply the classification to new cases.

Core Idea
The five rulings flow from the demand: decisive for action (wujub), preponderant for action (nadb), decisive for refrain (tahrim), preponderant for refrain (karaha), and option (ibaha).

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of legal rulings in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author explains how the requirement (iqtida) contained in the divine address generates five distinct categories of hukm taklifi. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for every student of fiqh, as it underlies all subsequent derivations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dimensions of iqtida
Recognize that the divine address's requirement (iqtida) can be either decisive (hatman) or preponderant (tarjihan), and it concerns either performing an act or refraining from it.
This gives four logical combinations: decisive + action, preponderant + action, decisive + refrain, preponderant + refrain.
2
Map each combination to a ruling
Match each combination to the corresponding ruling: decisive+action = wujub, preponderant+action = nadb, decisive+refrain = tahrim, preponderant+refrain = karaha.
Memorize this mapping as the core of the classification.
3
Recognize the case of takhyeer
Note that when the address gives full freedom (takhyeer) without any demand, the ruling is ibaha (permissibility). This is not based on iqtida.
Ibaha is neutral; the mukallaf may do or leave without any preference.
4
Verify correspondence with fiqh terms
Map these usul categories to the familiar fiqh terms: wujub = fard/wajib, nadb = mandub/mustahabb, tahrim = haram, karaha = makruh tahrimi/tanzihi, ibaha = mubah.
In Hanafi terminology, wujub includes both fard and wajib; karaha includes both makruh tahrimi and tanzihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الاقتضاء إن كان حتماً لفعل غير كف فالإيجاب، وهو نفس الأمر النفسي، أو ترجيحاً فالندب، أو حتما لكف، فالتحريم، أو ترجيحاً فالكـراهة، والتخيير الإباحة ولما فرغ المصنف عن أجوبة الأبحاث والاعتراضات الواردة على تعريف الحكم شرع في شرح قيود التعريف والأقسام التي تدل عليها القيود . ۱ - ثم الاقتضاء الذي هو بمعنى الطلب) (۱) ( إن كان لطلب فعل غير كف حتماً ووجوباً فهو الإيجاب. ۲ - وإن كان ذلك الاقتضاء ) ترجيحاً (ترجيح جانب الوجود) فهو الندب. ٣- وإن كان الاقتضاء حتما ووجوباً لكف فعل فهو التحريم - وان كان ترجيحاً (الجانب عدم فعل ) فهو الكراهة. ٥ - والتخيير (جعل المكلف مختاراً في الفعل وتركه) هو الاباحة . فاشتمل تعريف الأحكام الفرعية الخمسة التي هي من الحكم بمعنى أثر الخطاب والثابت به.
Then the requirement, if it is decisive for doing an act not pertaining to refraining, then it is obligation, and it is the very internal command. Or if it is preponderant, then it is recommendation. Or if it is decisive for refraining, then it is prohibition. Or if it is preponderant, then it is abhorrence. And option is permissibility. When the author finished answering the investigations and objections upon the definition of hukm, he began explaining the restrictions of the definition and the categories that the restrictions indicate. 1. Then the requirement, which is in the sense of demand, if it is for demanding an act not pertaining to refraining in a decisive and obligatory manner, then it is obligation. 2. And if that requirement is preponderant (preponderating the side of existence), then it is recommendation. 3. And if the requirement is decisive and obligatory for refraining from an act, then it is prohibition. 4. And if it is preponderant (for the side of non-existence), then it is abhorrence. 5. And option (making the obligated person free to choose between doing and leaving) is permissibility. Thus it includes the definition of the five subsidiary rulings which are from hukm in the sense of the effect of the address and what is established by it.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a systematic definition of the five categories of legal rulings (ahkam shar'iyya) as per Hanafi usul. The author explains that the requirement (iqtida) from the divine address can be either decisive (hatman) or preponderant (tarjihan). If it is a decisive demand to perform an action, it is wujub (obligation). If it is a preponderant demand to perform, it is nadb (recommendation). If it is a decisive demand to refrain, it is tahrim (prohibition). If it is a preponderant demand to refrain, it is karaha (abhorrence). If the address leaves the person with freedom of choice (takhyeer) between doing and leaving, it is ibaha (permissibility). These five correspond to the five rulings known in fiqh: fard/wajib, mandub, haram, makruh, mubah. The author clarifies that iqtida is synonymous with talab (demand). The numbers in parentheses likely refer to footnotes. This is a foundational usul definition that every student of Hanafi fiqh memorizes.

Key Terms
الاقتضاء (al-iqtida): The demand or requirement in the divine address that leads to a ruling.
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The ruling that results from a decisive demand to perform an action.
التحريم (al-tahrim): The ruling that results from a decisive demand to refrain from an action.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): The ruling that results from the option (takhyeer) to do or leave an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wujub (obligation).
B) Tahrim (prohibition).
C) Nadb (recommendation).
D) Ibaha (permissibility).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of rulings rests on the nature of the demand in the divine address: decisive vs preponderant, action vs refrain, versus option.

Hanafi Classification of Hukm: Qat'i vs Zanni Evidence
Usul rule: Hanafi classification based on evidence certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi classification of rulings (fard/haram vs wajib/makruh tahriman) based on the certainty of the evidence, and explain the difference between the views of Imam Muhammad and the two shaykhs regarding the status of makruh tahriman.

Core Idea
A ruling's strength (fard vs wajib) depends on the evidence's certainty (qat'i vs zanni), not just the demand's decisiveness.

The Hanafi school uniquely differentiates between fard and wajib, and between haram and makruh tahriman, based on whether the evidence is certain or speculative. While the outward consequence—punishment for non-compliance—is the same, the theological status differs: denying a fard or haram can lead to disbelief, but denying a wajib or makruh tahriman does not. This passage clarifies the classification and highlights an internal nuance regarding the term 'makruh tahriman'.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two dimensions of hukm
Notice that the passage first mentions the general usuli division into five hukms considering the proof itself. Then it introduces the Hanafi addition: they also look at the evidence's certainty (qat'i vs zanni). Understand that the Hanafis are considering the 'dalil' (the indicator) itself, not just the demand.
The general usuli division is independent of evidence certainty; Hanafis superimpose the evidence dimension.
2
Identify the four Hanafi categories
From the Hanafi perspective: if a decisive demand is established by a certain proof, the ruling is fard (for action) and haram (for abstinence). If established by a speculative proof, the ruling is wajib (for action) and makruh tahriman (for abstinence).
Both pairs involve a decisive demand; the difference is solely the evidence strength.
3
Note the shared consequence and difference in denial
Both pairs incur punishment for violation. However, the denier of a fard or haram may become a disbeliever, whereas the denier of a wajib or makruh tahriman does not. This is why Imam Muhammad's statement that 'every makruh is haram' is metaphorical.
The shared punishment is for the act itself; the disbelief issue is separate.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab nuance
Imam Muhammad said 'every makruh is haram metaphorically'. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf clarified that makruh tahriman is 'closer to haram' but not identical. Understand that this is a semantic clarification: in terms of punishment, they are similar, but in creed, they differ.
The metaphor is in the temporal consequence, not in theological status.
5
Apply the classification to a new ruling
Given a specific fiqh ruling (e.g., obligation of fasting, prohibition of interest), determine whether its evidence is qat'i or zanni. Then classify it as fard/haram or wajib/makruh tahriman. Also evaluate the consequence of denial.
Apply the principle consistently; be aware of differing opinions on the evidence strength of some rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الأصوليين قسموا الحكم بمعنى الخطاب إلى الأقسام الخمسة، المذكورة باعتبار نفس الدليل المشتمل على الاقتضاء أو التخيير. والحنفية لاحظوا حال الدال كونه قطعياً أو ظنياً فقالوا إن ثبت الطلب الجازم بقطعي فالافتراض والتحريم، أو بظني فالإيجاب وكراهة التحريم، ويشار كانهما في استحقاق العقاب بالترك، ومن ههنا قال محمد كل مكروه حرام تجوزاً، والحقيقة ما قالاه: إنه إلى الحرام أقرب. هذا وأما الحنفية فلاحظوا حال الدليل الدال على الحكم بمعنى أثر الخطاب أعنى الحكم الفقهي، فقسموا الحكم الثابت بالخطاب إلى الأنواع الأربعة: فقالوا: ان ثبت الطلب الجازم بدليل قطعي فالحكم هو الافتراض في طلب الفعل. والتحريم في طلب الترك. وان ثبت بدليل ظني فالحكم هو الإيجاب في طلب الفعل. وكراهة التحريم في طلب الترك. وحكم الإيجاب وكراهة التحريم أنها يشاركان الافتراض والتحريم في استحقاق العقاب بترك الواجب وترك الاجتناب عن كراهة التحريم. ومن أجل مشاركة الحرام والمكروه تحريماً في العقاب بسبب ترك الاجتناب عنهما قال محمد كل مكروه حرام تجوزاً (مجازاً)، والحقيقة ما قاله الشيخان (أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف): إنه (المكروه تحريماً) الى الحرام أقرب. خذ هذا واحفظه. فقول محمد مجاز؛ لأن منكر المكروه تحريما لا يكفر، ولو كان حقيقة لزم القول بكفر منكر المكروه تحريما، ولم يقل به أحد.
And know that the Usuliyun divided hukm in the sense of khitab into the five mentioned divisions, considering the proof itself that contains a demand or choice. And the Hanafis observed the condition of the indicator being certain or speculative, so they said: if a decisive demand is established by a certain (proof), then it is fard and haram; or by a speculative (proof), then it is wajib and makruh tahriman, and they share in deserving punishment for leaving. Hence Muhammad said: every makruh is haram metaphorically, and the reality is what the two shaykhs said: that it (the makruh tahriman) is closer to haram. This. And as for the Hanafis, they observed the condition of the proof indicating the hukm in the sense of the effect of the khitab, i.e., the fiqh ruling, so they divided the hukm established by khitab into four types: they said: if a decisive demand is established by a certain proof, then the ruling is fard in demanding the action, and haram in demanding abandonment; and if established by a speculative proof, then the ruling is wajib in demanding the action, and makruh tahriman in demanding abandonment. And the ruling of wajib and makruh tahriman is that they share with fard and haram in deserving punishment for leaving the wajib and for not avoiding the makruh tahriman. And because of the sharing of haram and makruh tahriman in punishment due to leaving avoidance of them, Muhammad said: every makruh is haram metaphorically, and the reality is what the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) said: that it (the makruh tahriman) is closer to haram. Take this and memorize it. So Muhammad's statement is metaphorical; because the denier of makruh tahriman does not disbelieve, and if it were literal, it would necessitate the statement of disbelief of the denier of makruh tahriman, and no one said that.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi classification of rulings based on the strength of the evidence. The author first notes that the general usul scholars divide hukm (in the sense of divine address) into five categories (obligation, recommendation, permissibility, disapproval, prohibition) considering the nature of the proof that contains a demand or choice. The Hanafis, however, add a crucial dimension: they look at whether the proof is certain (qat'i) or speculative (zanni). If the demand is decisive (jāzim) and the evidence is certain, the ruling is fard for actions and haram for prohibitions. If the evidence is only speculative, the ruling is wajib and makruh tahriman. Both pairs share the consequence of punishment for non-compliance, but the denier of a fard or haram may become a disbeliever if they reject it outright, whereas the denier of a wajib or makruh tahriman does not necessarily become a disbeliever. This is why Imam Muhammad said 'every makruh is haram metaphorically'—he meant that in terms of temporal punishment (i.e., deserving Hellfire), they are similar, but in terms of creed, they differ. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf clarified that makruh tahriman is 'closer to haram,' indicating its severity but not equivalence. The passage emphasizes that calling it 'haram' is a metaphor, because denying a metaphorical haram does not entail kufr.

Key Terms
قطعيّ (qat'i): Certain evidence that leaves no room for doubt, such as Qur'anic verses of clear meaning and mutawatir hadith.
ظني (zanni): Speculative evidence that is probable but not certain, such as khabar al-wahid or ambiguous Qur'anic verses.
فرض (fard): An obligation established by certain (qat'i) evidence; denial can lead to disbelief.
واجب (wajib): An obligation established by speculative (zanni) evidence; denial does not cause disbelief.
مكروه تحريماً (makruh tahriman): A prohibition established by speculative evidence; its avoidance is required and violation incurs punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Fard is proven by certain evidence, wajib by speculative evidence.
B) Fard is for actions, wajib for prohibitions.
C) Wajib is proven by certain evidence, fard by speculative evidence.
D) Fard and wajib are identical.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi classification of rulings into fard/haram and wajib/makruh tahriman depends on whether the evidence is qat'i or zanni; both pairs share punishment but differ in the consequence of denial.

Harmonizing Ijaab/Wujoob and Tahrim/Hurma
Addressing an apparent contradiction in hukm terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why usul scholars use ijaab and wujoob interchangeably and evaluate the two Hanafi harmonization strategies.

Core Idea
Ijaab and wujoob are the same reality, differing only in perspective: cause vs. effect, Lawgiver vs. act.

Have you noticed that Hanafi usul texts sometimes list the categories of hukm as ijaab and tahrim, and other times as wujoob and hurma? At first glance this seems contradictory. In this lesson, we will explore two ways that classical scholars resolve this apparent inconsistency, and see how they maintain terminological precision without losing coherence.

Guided Path
1
State the apparent contradiction
Identify the two pairs: ijaab/tahrim vs wujoob/hurma. Explain why the inconsistency arises: the terms are not identical but closely related.
2
Explain the first answer – musamaha
Describe the view that it is a lenient metaphor: ijaab is the cause of wujoob, tahrim the cause of hurma; scholars mention the cause intending the effect.
This is a simple linguistic concession.
3
Explain the second answer – essential unity
Describe the view that they are identical in essence (mutahidan bi al-dhat) but differ in perspective: ijaab is the command attributed to the Lawgiver, wujoob is the same command attributed to the act.
This treats the terms as two aspects of one reality.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection: wujoob is consequent upon ijaab, so they cannot be identical. The response: a thing can be consequent upon itself under different aspects; the same reality considered as cause is ijaab, as effect is wujoob.
This is a refined logical point.
5
Compare the two answers
Note the difference: musamaha treats it as metaphor; essential unity treats it as real but multi-perspectival. Both are accepted in the Hanafi tradition.
Understand the tarjih: the author favors essential unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفع التعارض عن كلام الأصوليين واعلم أنهم جعلوا أقسام الحكم مرة الإيجاب والتحريم، وأخرى الوجوب والحرمة، فحمل بعضهم على المسامحة، وبعضهم على أنهما متحدان بالذات ومختلفان بالاعتبار، فإن معنى افعل إذا نسب إلى الحاكم يسمى إيجاباً، وإذا نسب إلى الفعل يسمى وجوبا. واعلم أنهم جعلوا أقسام الحكم مرة الإيجاب والتحريم، وأخرى الوجوب والحرمة فحمل بعضهم (هذا) على المسامحة، وبعضهم على أنهما متحدان بالذات ومختلفان بالاعتبار، فإن معنى افعل إذا نسب إلى الحاكم يسمى إيجاباً، وإذا نسب إلى الفعل يسمى وجوبا. واعلم أنهم (الأصوليين) جعلوا أقسام الحكم مزة الإيجاب والتحريم، وأخرى الوجوب والحرمة (مع أن الوجوب غير الإيجاب والحرمة غير التحريم)؟ وأجابوا عن هذا التعارض بوجهين: فحمل بعضهم هذا على المسامحة، والمجاز بأن الإيجاب سبب الوجوب والتحريم سبب الحرمة، فذكروا السبب وأرادوا المسبب، وهذا هو وجه المسامحة والمجاز. وحمل بعضهم على أنهما متحدان بالذات ومختلفان بالاعتبار، فيذكر أحدهما في محل الآخر، نظراً إلى الاتحاد الذاتي، ثم أشار إلى اختلافهما اعتباراً، فقال: فإن معنى (افعل) إذا نسب إلى الحاكم (الأمر) سمى إيجاباً، وإذا نسب إلى الفعل فعل المكلف المأمور) ستمى وجوباً. الايراد على الجواب الثاني والجواب عنه وأورد أن الوجوب مترتب على الإيجاب، فكيف الاتحاد؟ ويجاب بجواز ترتب الشيئ باعتبار على نفسه باعتبار آخر.
Resolving the contradiction in the statements of the usuliyun. And know that they made the divisions of hukm sometimes al-ijaab and al-tahrim, and other times al-wujoob and al-hurma. So some interpreted this as leniency (musamaha), and others as that they are identical in essence and different in perspective. For the meaning of 'do' when attributed to the ruler is called ijaab, and when attributed to the act it is called wujoob. And they answered this contradiction in two ways: first, some interpreted it as leniency and metaphor, because ijaab is the cause of wujoob and tahrim is the cause of hurma, so they mentioned the cause and intended the effect; this is the aspect of leniency and metaphor. Second, some interpreted that they are identical in essence and different in perspective, so one is mentioned in place of the other, considering the essential unity. Then he pointed to their difference in perspective, saying: for the meaning of 'do' when attributed to the ruler (the command) is called ijaab, and when attributed to the act (the action of the obligated) it is called wujoob. An objection against the second answer and its response: it was objected that wujoob is consequent upon ijaab, so how can they be identical? And it is answered by the permissibility that a thing may be consequent upon itself under one perspective and upon itself under another perspective.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage deals with an apparent inconsistency in the works of usul scholars: sometimes they list the categories of hukm as 'ijaab and tahrim' (command and prohibition from the Lawgiver's perspective), and other times as 'wujoob and hurma' (obligation and prohibition as qualities of the act). These terms are not identical but closely related. The author presents two resolutions. The first is leniency (musamaha) and metaphor: ijaab is the cause of wujoob, and tahrim is the cause of hurma, so the scholars mention the cause but intend the effect. The second is that they are identical in essence but differ in perspective: ijaab is the same reality as wujoob, considered from the perspective of the Lawgiver, while wujoob is considered from the perspective of the act. The passage then addresses an objection to the second view: how can two things be identical if one is the consequence of the other? The answer is that something can be consequent upon itself under different aspects; e.g., the same command can be seen as the cause from one angle and the effect from another. This shows the sophistication of Hanafi usul in harmonizing diverse terminology.

Key Terms
إيجاب (ijaab): The Lawgiver's act of imposing a command; the source-side of obligation.
وجوب (wujoob): The quality of the action becoming obligatory; the effect-side of ijaab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second answer in the passage, why is ijaab sometimes used in place of wujoob?
A) Because they are entirely different and scholars made a mistake.
B) Because they are identical in essence and differ only in perspective (attribution to Lawgiver vs. act).
C) Because wujoob is the cause of ijaab.
D) Because the terms have no fixed meaning.

Key Takeaway
The apparent contradiction between ijaab/tahrim and wujoob/hurma is resolved by recognizing either a cause-effect metaphor or an essential unity with different perspectives.

Reconciling Ijab/Tahrim and Wujoob/Hurmah in Hanafi Usul
Explains and defends two approaches to reconciling the dual classifications of hukm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi views on the relationship between al-ijab/al-tahrim and al-wujoob/al-hurmah and evaluate the objections and responses.

Core Idea
Al-ijab and al-wujoob are identical in essence but differ in consideration, resolving the terminological paradox.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, scholars sometimes divide the hukm into 'ijab and tahrim' and other times into 'wujoob and hurmah'. At first glance, these appear contradictory. This lesson unpacks the two ways Hanafi scholars have reconciled these terms: some treat it as a metaphor (cause for effect), while others maintain they are identical in essence but distinguished by consideration. We will analyze the objections to the latter view and their elegant resolutions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terminological sets
The passage presents two sets: al-ijab/al-tahrim (command/prohibition) and al-wujoob/al-hurmah (obligation/prohibition). Recognize that these are used interchangeably in usul texts.
2
Examine the first reconciliation view (tasāmuh)
Some scholars consider it a leniency (musamaha) or metaphor: al-ijab is the cause of al-wujoob, so mentioning the cause implies the effect. Understand this as a rhetorical device.
3
Examine the second reconciliation view (essence/consideration)
Others hold that al-ijab and al-wujoob are identical in essence (dhat) but differ in consideration (i'tibar). When attributed to the lawgiver, it is ijab; when to the act, it is wujoob.
4
Analyze the objection from precedence and response
The objection: wujoob follows ijab in existence, so they cannot be identical. The response: precedence is of one consideration over another, not of essence over essence.
5
Analyze the objection from categories and response
Another objection: ijab is from the category of action (fi'l), wujoob from passion (infi'al). The response: different categories can apply to the same thing under different considerations, as per Sayyid al-Sharif.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Resolving the Contradiction in the Speech of the Usuli Scholars: Know that they made the divisions of hukm sometimes as Ijab and Tahrim, and other times as Wujoob and Hurmah. Some explained it as leniency/tolerance (musamaha), and others as that they are identical in essence and different in consideration. Some explained that they are identical in essence and different in consideration, so one is mentioned in place of the other due to essential identity. Then the author raised an objection to the second answer: it was objected that Wujoob is consequent upon Ijab (exists after it), so how can there be identity between cause and effect, and between what precedes in existence (Ijab) and what is delayed (Wujoob)? Similarly for Tahrim and Hurmah in precedence and delay. Then he answered: it is permissible for a thing to be consequent upon itself under one description and aspect from another description and aspect. Then he explained this consequence: it reduces to the order of one consideration over another; it is not the order of the essence of one over the essence of the other, which would be impossible. And it reduces to the order of one consideration over the other. Sayyid (may his secret be sanctified) said: by this is answered what was said that Ijab belongs to the category of action and Wujoob to the category of passion, and the claim of impossibility of applying categories to a thing under considerations is open to discussion. And this is an objection to the second answer in another style: how can there be essential identity between Ijab and Wujoob, Tahrim and Hurmah, when Ijab and Tahrim are from the category of action, and Wujoob and Hurmah are from the category of passion, two different categories? So the author, transmitting the answer from Sayyid al-Sharif, said: by this answer to the second objection of difference of consideration, it is answered what was said in objection in another style, that Ijab is from the category of action and Wujoob from the category of passion, so how can they be identical in essence? The summary of this answer is: it does not necessitate the union of the two different categories, because applying one of them
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage addresses a technical point in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the apparent contradiction in how the scholars classify the divisions of hukm (legal ruling). Sometimes they say 'Ijab and Tahrim' (commanding and prohibiting) and other times 'Wujoob and Hurmah' (obligation and prohibition). The author resolves this by presenting two approaches among the scholars. The first view considers it a leniency or metaphor: Ijab (the act of commanding) is the cause of Wujoob (the attribute of being obligatory), so they mention the cause and intend the effect. The second view holds that Ijab and Wujoob are identical in essence but differ in consideration: when the meaning of 'do' is attributed to the lawgiver, it is called Ijab; when attributed to the act of the obligated person, it is called Wujoob. The author then presents an objection to the second view: Wujoob follows Ijab, so they cannot be identical. The response is that a thing can be considered prior under one aspect and posterior under another; this is not a real precedence of the essence over itself but of one consideration over another. Finally, another objection is raised from the categories (maqulat) of action and passion. The author transmits the answer from Sayyid al-Sharif: since the difference is only in considerations, it is permissible to apply both categories to the same thing under different aspects. Thus, the apparent contradiction is resolved within Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The act of the lawgiver in issuing a command (the verb 'do').
الوجوب (al-wujoob): The attribute of an act being obligatory, which arises from الإيجاب.
التحريم (al-tahrim): The act of the lawgiver in issuing a prohibition.
الحرمة (al-hurmah): The attribute of an act being prohibited, arising from التحريم.
الاعتبار (al-i'tibar): Consideration or aspect under which a thing is viewed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are completely different and unrelated.
B) They are identical in essence but differ in consideration.
C) They are synonyms used interchangeably.
D) They are cause and effect in a literal sense.

Key Takeaway
Al-ijab and al-wujoob are two faces of the same coin: the command from the lawgiver and its property in the act, distinguished only by consideration.

The Three Categories of Declaratory Rulings: Cause, Preventer, Condition
Classification of declaratory address into cause, preventer, and condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given description (waṣf) from the Lawgiver as either a cause, preventer, or condition, and identify which subtype (temporal/conceptual, preventer of ruling/cause, condition of ruling/cause) applies.

Core Idea
A declaratory ruling announces that something is a cause, preventer, or condition—it sets the stage for the prescriptive ruling.

This passage introduces the three categories of declaratory rulings (al-ḥukm al-waḍʿī) in Hanafi uṣūl. While prescriptive rulings (al-ḥukm al-taklīfī) tell us what to do, declaratory rulings tell us what counts as a cause, a preventer, or a condition. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying rulings: a cause triggers an obligation, a preventer blocks it, and a condition makes it effective. We will walk through each category with its subtypes and textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
The text lists cause (sabab), preventer (māniʿ), and condition (sharṭ). Note that these are rulings on descriptions, not the descriptions themselves.
2
Analyze the cause (sabab)
Distinguish temporal (e.g., sunset for prayer) vs conceptual (e.g., intoxication for prohibition). Identify the proof: Quran for temporal (17:78) and hadith for conceptual.
The cause is designated by the Lawgiver; it does not necessarily cause the ruling by nature.
3
Analyze the preventer (māniʿ)
Two subtypes: preventer of the ruling (e.g., paternity blocks retaliation) and preventer of the cause (e.g., debt blocks zakat obligation). Note the effect: the ruling does not apply even if the cause exists.
The preventer removes the ruling or blocks the cause's effectiveness.
4
Analyze the condition (sharṭ)
Two subtypes: condition for the ruling (e.g., ability to deliver for sale) and condition for the cause (e.g., purity for prayer). Conditions are external prerequisites.
Conditions are not part of the cause but are necessary for the ruling or the cause to be effective.
5
Apply the framework to a new description
Given a new description (e.g., sounding the adhan for Friday prayer), determine which category it falls into and why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم خطاب الوضع أصناف :
(۱) منها: الحكم على الوصف بالسببية ، وهي بالاستقراء على نوعين) وقتية كالدلوك لوجوب الصلاة ومعنوية كالإسكار للتحريم (٢) ومنها : الحكم بكونه مانعاً، إما للحكم، كالأبوة في القصاص أو للسبب، كالدين في الزكوة
(۳) ومنها : الحكم بكونه شرطاً للحكم، كالقدرة على تسليم المبيع للبيع، أو للسبب، كالطهارة للصلاة، وسببها تعظيم الباري تعالى، هذا ولما فرغ المصنف عن بيان أقسام الحكم التكليفي شرع في بيان أقسام الحكم الوضعي فقال : ثم خطاب الوضع أصناف :
-١ منها الحكم على الوصف بالسببية بكونه سبباً للحكم التكليفي) وهي (السببية) بالاستقراء على نوعين : ١ ،وقتية، أي يكون السبب (وقتياً ككون الدلوك (زوال الشمس) سبباً لوجوب الصلاة، لقوله تعالى : أقم الصلاة لدلوك الشمس ومعنوية (أي يكون الوصف سبباً معنوياً للحكم) كالاسكار (فانه سبب معنوى وعلة) للتحريم، لقوله عليه السلام : "كل مسكر خمر وكل خمر حرام "وقوله كل مسكر حرام "
-۲- ومنها الحكم بكونه (كون الوصف) مانعاً.
:١ إما أن يكون ذلك الوصف مانعا للحكم، كالأبوة، فانها مانعة عن قصاص الأب فيما إذا قتل الأب ابنه، فانه لا يقتص من الأب لأجل قتل الأبن .
٢ وإما أن يكون مانعاً للسبب عن سببيته كالدين في منع وجوب الزكاة ؛ فإن صاحب النصاب لو كان عليه دين فالدين يكون مانعاً عن وجوب الزكوة فيها
Then the declaratory address has categories:
(1) Among them: the ruling on a description as a cause, and it is, by induction, of two types: temporal like the decline of the sun for the obligation of prayer, and conceptual like intoxication for prohibition.
(2) Among them: the ruling that it is a preventer, either of the ruling, like paternity in retaliation, or of the cause, like debt in zakat.
(3) Among them: the ruling that it is a condition, either of the ruling, like ability to deliver the sold item for sale, or of the cause, like purity for prayer, while its cause is the glorification of Allah Most High. This, when the author finished explaining the divisions of the prescriptive ruling, he began explaining the divisions of the declaratory ruling and said: Then the declaratory address has categories:
-1 Among them: the ruling on the description as being a cause for the prescriptive ruling. And it (causality) is, by induction, of two types: temporal, i.e., the cause is temporal, like the decline of the sun (its setting) being a cause for the obligation of prayer, by His saying: 'Establish prayer at the decline of the sun' [Q. 17:78]; and conceptual, i.e., the description is a conceptual cause for the ruling, like intoxication, for it is a conceptual cause and effective cause for prohibition, by his saying, peace be upon him: 'Every intoxicant is khamr, and every khamr is forbidden' and his saying: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.'
-2 Among them: the ruling that (the description) is a preventer.
[I] Either that description is a preventer of the ruling, like paternity, for it prevents retaliation of the father if he kills his son, so the father is not retaliated against for killing his son.
[II] Or it is a preventer of the cause from its causality, like debt preventing the obligation of zakat; for if the owner of nisab has debt, then the debt is a preventer of the obligation of zakat on it.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines the three categories of the declaratory address (khitāb al-waḍʿ) in Hanafi usul: cause (sabab), preventer (māniʿ), and condition (sharṭ). The author first gives a brief list then expands with evidence. The cause (sabab) is a description made by the Lawgiver as a sign for the prescriptive ruling. It is either temporal (like the decline of the sun for obligatory prayer, citing Q. 17:78) or conceptual (like intoxication for prohibition, citing hadith 'Every intoxicant is forbidden'). The preventer (māniʿ) either blocks the ruling itself (like paternity prevents retaliation of a father for killing his son) or blocks the cause from being effective (like debt prevents the obligation of zakat on wealth, even if nisab is reached). The condition (sharṭ) is either a condition for the ruling (like ability to deliver the sold item for the validity of sale) or for the cause (like ritual purity for prayer, whose cause is glorification of Allah). The passage follows the order: first listing the three, then elaborating each with examples and textual proofs. This classification is crucial for understanding how rulings are established and negated in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A cause: a description that the Lawgiver has designated as a sign indicating a prescriptive ruling, either temporal (like sunset for prayer) or conceptual (like intoxication for prohibition).
مانع (māniʿ): A preventer: a description that either blocks the ruling itself (e.g., paternity blocking qiṣāṣ) or prevents the cause from producing its usual effect (e.g., debt blocking zakāt obligation).
شرط (sharṭ): A condition: a description that is necessary for either the ruling to be valid or the cause to be effective (e.g., ability to deliver for sale, purity for prayer).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the preventer (māniʿ) in the case of a father killing his son, according to this passage?
A) The son's disobedience prevents retaliation.
B) Paternity (abūwa) prevents the ruling of qiṣāṣ against the father.
C) The father's ownership prevents the obligation of diya.
D) The cause of killing is absent.

Key Takeaway
Declaratory rulings identify the legal cause, preventer, or condition that underlies prescriptive rulings; knowing which is which ensures accurate legal reasoning.

Types of Declaratory Rulings: Cause, Preventer, Condition
Enumeration and explanation of three types of declaratory rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of hukm wad'i (sabab, mani', shart) and identify examples of each from the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Declaratory rulings classify causes, preventers, and conditions that shape how obligations apply.

This passage from a Hanafi usul commentary explains that declaratory rulings (hukm wad'i) are not obligations themselves but attributes that affect how obligations arise or are prevented. The author breaks them down into three categories: cause (sabab), preventer (mani'), and condition (shart), each with two subtypes. Understanding these categories is essential for deriving fiqh rulings correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main types
Recognize that the declaratory ruling has three categories: cause, preventer, and condition. Each is defined by how it relates to a hukm taklifi.
These are not rulings that command or prohibit, but rulings that classify relationships.
2
Analyze the subtype of each
Each category has two subtypes. For cause: temporal (e.g., sunset for prayer) and conceptual (e.g., intoxication for prohibition). For preventer: preventer of the ruling itself (e.g., paternity blocking qisas) and preventer of the cause (e.g., debt blocking zakat obligation). For condition: condition for the ruling (e.g., ability to deliver for sale validity) and condition for the cause (e.g., purification for prayer).
The categories are inductive (bil-istiqra') and not exhaustive of all possibilities, but they cover the main types.
3
Examine the proofs cited
The author provides textual evidence for the cause category: Quran 17:78 for temporal cause and hadith 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' for conceptual cause. Note that these proofs establish the existence of cause, not the definition itself.
The proofs are from the primary sources, reinforcing that usul is grounded in revelation.
4
Apply the categories to new examples
Once you understand the three types, practice by thinking of additional examples from fiqh: e.g., travel as a cause for qasr, iddah as a preventer for marriage, or wudu as a condition for prayer.
This solidifies the abstract categories into concrete rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم خطاب الوضع أصناف : (۱) منها: الحكم على الوصف بالسببية ، وهي بالاستقراء على نوعين) وقتية كالدلوك لوجوب الصلاة ومعنوية كالإسكار للتحريم (٢) ومنها : الحكم بكونه مانعاً، إما للحكم، كالأبوة في القصاص أو للسبب، كالدين في الزكوة (۳) ومنها : الحكم بكونه شرطاً للحكم، كالقدرة على تسليم المبيع للبيع، أو للسبب، كالطهارة للصلاة، وسببها تعظيم الباري تعالى، هذا ولما فرغ المصنف عن بيان أقسام الحكم التكليفي شرع في بيان أقسام الحكم الوضعي فقال : ثم خطاب الوضع أصناف : -١ منها الحكم على الوصف بالسببية بكونه سبباً للحكم التكليفي) وهي (السببية) بالاستقراء على نوعين : ١ ،وقتية، أي يكون السبب (وقتياً ككون الدلوك (زوال الشمس) سبباً لوجوب الصلاة، لقوله تعالى : أقم الصلاة لدلوك الشمس ومعنوية (أي يكون الوصف سبباً معنوياً للحكم) كالاسكار (فانه سبب معنوى وعلة) للتحريم، لقوله عليه السلام : "كل مسكر خمر وكل خمر حرام "وقوله كل مسكر حرام " -۲- ومنها الحكم بكونه (كون الوصف) مانعاً. :١ إما أن يكون ذلك الوصف مانعا للحكم، كالأبوة، فانها مانعة عن قصاص الأب فيما إذا قتل الأب ابنه، فانه لا يقتص من الأب لأجل قتل الأبن . ٢ وإما أن يكون مانعاً للسبب عن سببيته كالدين في منع وجوب الزكاة ؛ فإن صاحب النصاب لو كان عليه دين فالدين يكون مانعاً عن وجوب الزكوة فيها عنده من المال فقدر النصاب من المال كان سبباً لوجوب الزكوة، ولكن الدين منع المال عن السببية . -٣- ومنها الحكم بكونه (كون (الوصف) شرطاً . ١ : إما شرط للحكم، كالقدرة على تسليم المبيع والثمن لصحة البيع، فإن البيع حكم شرعي تكليفي، وشرط صحته قدرة البائع على تسليم المبيع وقدرة المشترى على تسليم الثمن، والا فيفسد البيع. ٢ : وإما شرط للسبب كالطهارة فانها شرط للصلاة التي هي سبب تعظيم البارى تعالى فإنّ سبب الصلاة هو تعظيم الله تعالى ولا يتحقق هذا التعظيم الا مع الطهارة، خذ هذا واحفظه . فأصناف الحكم الوضعى ثلاثة، وأقسامه التفصيلة ستة.
Then the declaratory address has categories: (1) It includes: the ruling on the attribute as being a cause, and it (causality) by induction is of two types: temporal like the declination of the sun for the obligation of prayer, and conceptual like intoxication for prohibition. (2) It includes: the ruling on it being a preventer, either of the ruling, like paternity in retaliation, or of the cause, like debt in zakat. (3) It includes: the ruling on it being a condition for the ruling, like the ability to deliver the sold item for the sale, or for the cause, like purification for prayer, whose cause is the glorification of Allah Almighty. This, and when the author finished explaining the divisions of the obligating ruling, he began explaining the divisions of the declaratory ruling, saying: Then the declaratory address has categories: -1. First: the ruling on the attribute as being a cause (being a cause for the obligating ruling). And it (causality) by induction is of two types: 1. Temporal, meaning the cause is temporal, like the declination of the sun (its setting) being a cause for the obligation of prayer, due to His saying: "Establish prayer at the declination of the sun." And conceptual, meaning the attribute is a conceptual cause for the ruling, like intoxication, for it is a conceptual cause and rationale for prohibition, due to his saying (peace be upon him): "Every intoxicant is khamr and every khamr is forbidden," and his saying: "Every intoxicant is forbidden." -2. Second: the ruling on it (the attribute) being a preventer. (a) Either that attribute is a preventer of the ruling, like paternity, for it prevents retaliation from the father when the father kills his son, for the father is not retaliated against for killing the son. (b) Or it is a preventer of the cause from its causality, like debt in preventing the obligation of zakat; for if the owner of a nisab had a debt upon him, the debt is a preventer of the obligation of zakat on the wealth he possesses. For the measure of nisab of wealth was a cause for the obligation of zakat, but the debt prevented the wealth from causality. -3. Third: the ruling on it (the attribute) being a condition. (a) Either a condition for the ruling, like the ability to deliver the sold item and the price for the validity of the sale. For the sale is a legal obligating ruling, and the condition of its validity is the ability of the seller to deliver the sold item and the ability of the buyer to deliver the price; otherwise the sale is corrupt. (b) Or a condition for the cause, like purification, for it is a condition for prayer which is the cause of glorifying Allah Almighty. For the cause of prayer is the glorification of Allah, and this glorification is not realized except with purification. Take this and memorize it. So the categories of the declaratory ruling are three, and its detailed divisions are six.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn's statement about the categories of declaratory ruling (hukm wad'i). The author explains that the declaratory address from the Lawgiver consists of three main types: cause (sabab), preventer (mani'), and condition (shart). Each type is further subdivided. 

First, the ruling on an attribute as a cause. Cause is of two types by induction: temporal causes like the declination of the sun (time of Dhuhr/Asr) which triggers the obligation of prayer; and conceptual causes like intoxication which is the 'illa for prohibition. The proof for the first is the Quranic verse 'Aqim al-salata li-duluki al-shams' (17:78), and for the second the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr and every khamr is forbidden' and 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' 

Second, the ruling on an attribute as a preventer. A preventer can block either the ruling itself or the cause from being effective. For example, paternity (fatherhood) prevents the ruling of qisas (retaliation) when a father kills his son; no retaliation. Debt prevents the cause (nisab) from triggering zakat obligation; a person with nisab but also debt is not obliged to pay zakat. 

Third, the ruling on an attribute as a condition. A condition can be for the ruling itself, like the seller's and buyer's ability to deliver for a valid sale; without it the sale is corrupt (fasid). Or a condition for the cause, like purification (taharah) is a condition for prayer, which itself is the cause for glorifying Allah; glorification is not valid without purification. 

The passage concludes that the categories of wad'i are three, but detailed divisions are six (because each of the three has two sub-types). This is a foundational classification in Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
السببية (as-sababiyya): The quality of an attribute being a cause (sabab) for a legal ruling.
المانع (al-mani'): An attribute that prevents either the ruling itself or the cause from being effective.
الشرط (ash-shart): An attribute that is a condition for the validity of a ruling or for the effectiveness of a cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a preventer of the cause (mani' li-s-sabab)?
A) Fatherhood preventing retaliation (qisas) when a father kills his son.
B) Debt preventing the obligation of zakat on one who possesses nisab.
C) Ability to deliver the sold item as a condition for a valid sale.
D) Intoxication being a cause for prohibition.

Key Takeaway
The declaratory ruling (hukm wad'i) consists of three categories: cause (sabab, with temporal and conceptual subtypes), preventer (mani', of the ruling or of the cause), and condition (shart, for the ruling or for the cause), each essential for understanding how obligations arise and are blocked.

Two Definitions of Wajib: Rational vs. Customary
Defining wajib with two theological variants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two definitions of wajib stated in the passage and explain the theological basis for each, distinguishing rational from customary obligation.

Core Idea
Wajib is defined by rational or customary entitlement; forgiveness of punishment does not negate obligation.

In this passage, the author begins the discussion of substantive rulings (masa'il al-ahkam) by defining the key term 'wajib'. He presents two definitions, each reflecting different theological schools: one based on rational necessity (Mu'tazila) and one based on customary/legal necessity (Ash'ari/Maturidi). Understanding these definitions is crucial because they affect how obligations are known—whether through reason alone or through revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Locate the two definitions of wajib in the passage. The first is introduced with 'huwa ma istaḥaqqa al-ʻiqāb...'. The second is introduced with 'qīla: huwa mā awʻida al-ʻiqāb...'.
The second definition is implied by 'qīla' (it was said).
2
Examine the qualifications
Note that the first definition includes the words 'ʻaqliyyan' (rationally) and 'ādiyyan' (customarily). Understand why both are added: to cover the views of the Mu'tazila (rational) and the Ash'aris/Maturidis (customary).
The Hanafi-Maturidi position is the customary one.
3
Understand the forgiveness clause
The passage states 'al-ʻafw min al-karam' meaning forgiveness of punishment is from Allah's generosity. This clarifies that even if a person is forgiven, the act still retains its wajib nature.
This addresses a potential confusion: forgiveness does not retroactively remove obligation.
4
Link to theological schools
Connect the definitions to the underlying theological principles: Mu'tazila hold that good and evil are known rationally; Ash'aris and Maturidis hold that they are known through revelation. The author includes both to make the definition comprehensive.
The passage is from a Hanafi text, so the preferred view is the customary definition.

Source Text

والآن نشرع في مسائل الأحكام ولنقدم عليها تعريف الواجب، وهو ما استحق العقاب تاركه استحقاقاً عقلياً أو عادياً والعفو من الكرم وقيل : هوما أوعد العقاب على تركه ولا يخرج العفو لأن الخلف في الوعيد جائز دون الوعد يقول المصنف : والآن بعد الفراغ عن تعريف الحكم وبيان أقسامه من الأحكام التكليفية والوضعية نشرع في مسائل الأحكام، ولتقدم عليها على المسائل) تعريف الواجب ثم ذكر له تعريفين : .1 تعريف الواجب الأول: (۱) وهو ما استحق تاركه العقاب استحقاقاً عقلياً، أو عادياً، وأما عدم العقاب والعفو عنه فهو ) من الكرم ، (أي من كرم الله تعالى على عباده). شرح التعريف : ولما كان حسن الأفعال وقبحها عقليين عند المعتزلة، وكانا مستلزمين للحكم عندهم عقلاً، وكان الحسن والقبح شرعيين عند الأشاعرة، وموجبين للحكم بعد ورود الشرع زاد المصنف قيد (عقلياً) ليشمل الواجب عند المعتزلة، وقيد (عادياً) ليشمل الواجب عند الأشاعرة والماتريدية، والماتريدية وان قالوا
And now we begin on the topics of rulings, and we present before them the definition of wajib: It is that which its abandoner deserves punishment, a rational entitlement or a customary entitlement, and forgiveness (of punishment) is from generosity. And it was said: It is that which punishment is threatened for its abandonment, and forgiveness does not exclude it because breaking a threat is permissible, not a promise. The author says: Now after finishing the definition of hukm and the explanation of its divisions—obligating and declaratory—we begin on the topics of rulings, and we present before them (the topics) the definition of wajib. Then he mentioned two definitions for it: 1. The first definition of wajib: It is that which its abandoner deserves punishment, a rational entitlement or a customary entitlement. As for no punishment and forgiveness of it, that is from generosity (i.e., from Allah's generosity to His servants). Explanation of the definition: Since good and bad of actions are rational according to the Mu'tazila, and they necessitate judgment rationally, and good and bad are legal according to the Ash'aris, and necessitate judgment after the revelation of the Law, the author added the qualification 'rationally' to include wajib according to the Mu'tazila, and the qualification 'customarily' to include wajib according to the Ash'aris and Maturidis. And the Maturidis, even if they say...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on substantive rulings (masa'il al-ahkam). The author first provides a definition of 'wajib' (obligatory). Two definitions are given: 

First definition: Wajib is that which its abandoner deserves punishment, either rationally (according to Mu'tazila who hold that good and evil are known by reason independently) or customarily (according to Ash'aris and Maturidis who hold that good and evil are known through revelation). The forgiveness of punishment is from Allah's generosity; it does not negate the wajib nature. 

Second definition (implied by 'qila'): Wajib is that which punishment is threatened for its abandonment. This definition avoids the issue of forgiveness because a threat (wa'id) can be pardoned, unlike a promise (wa'd) which must be fulfilled. 

The commentary explains the theological background. The author adds the qualification 'aqliyyan to cover the Mu'tazila position and 'adiyyan to cover the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions. The Maturidi school (which is the Hanafi theological school) also holds that good and evil are legal, but they accept that reason can know some things. The passage cuts off, but the point is that the definition is comprehensive across theological schools.

Key Terms
الواجب (al-wajib): That which its abandoner deserves punishment, whether by rational or customary entitlement.
استحقاق عقلي (istihqāq 'aqli): Deserving punishment based on rational judgment, as held by Mu'tazila.
العفو من الكرم (al-'afw min al-karam): Forgiveness of punishment is from Allah's generosity, not a negation of obligation.

Key Takeaway
Wajib is defined as that which its abandoner deserves punishment, either rationally or customarily, and forgiveness of punishment does not negate its obligatory nature.

Shart in Hanafi Usul: Condition for the Ruling vs. Condition for the Cause
Illustrates the third type of hukm wad'i: shart, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between the two types of shart (condition) in Hanafi usul: shart for the hukm and shart for the sabab.

Core Idea
A condition may qualify a ruling directly or the cause that triggers it.

In this passage, the author expands on the third pillar of al-hukm al-wad'i: the ruling that a description (wasf) serves as a condition (shart). He distinguishes between two roles: a condition that must be met for the ruling itself to be valid, and a condition that must be met for the cause to be effective. Understanding this duality is key to correctly applying many rulings in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of hukm wad'i
The passage mentions sabab, mani', and shart. Note the example of sabab (nisab for zakat) and mani' (debt preventing causality) to contextualize shart.
2
Distinguish the two subtypes of shart
The author gives two examples: (1) ability to deliver in a sale is a shart for the hukm (sale contract validity), and (2) ritual purity (taharah) is a shart for the cause (prayer as an act of glorification). Recognize the difference in what the condition directly affects.
3
Analyze the example of shart for the cause
Prayer is the cause (sabab) for glorifying God, but that cause only becomes effective when taharah is present. This illustrates that a condition may be attached to the sabab rather than the resulting hukm.
4
Memorize the concluding classification
The author states that the categories of hukm wad'i are three, but the detailed divisions are six (likely including sub-types of sabab, shart, and mani'). This is a mnemonic to retain.
The six detailed divisions are not elaborated here, but other sources break them down further.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنده من المال فقدر النصاب من المال كان سبباً لوجوب الزكوة، ولكن الدين منع المال عن السببية .
-٣- ومنها الحكم بكونه (كون (الوصف) شرطاً .
١ : إما شرط للحكم، كالقدرة على تسليم المبيع والثمن لصحة البيع، فإن البيع حكم شرعي تكليفي، وشرط صحته قدرة البائع على تسليم المبيع وقدرة المشترى على تسليم الثمن، والا فيفسد البيع.
٢ : وإما شرط للسبب كالطهارة فانها شرط للصلاة التي هي سبب تعظيم البارى تعالى فإنّ سبب الصلاة هو تعظيم الله تعالى ولا يتحقق هذا التعظيم الا مع الطهارة، خذ هذا واحفظه . فأصناف الحكم الوضعى ثلاثة، وأقسامه التفصيلة ستة.
With him of wealth, the amount of nisab of wealth was a cause for the obligation of zakat, but debt prevented the wealth from causality. -3- And among them is the ruling that the description is a condition. 1: Either a condition for the ruling, like the ability to deliver the sold item and the price for the validity of the sale, for sale is a religiously binding ruling, and its condition of validity is the seller's ability to deliver the sold item and the buyer's ability to deliver the price, otherwise the sale becomes invalid. 2: Or a condition for the cause, like purity, for it is a condition for prayer which is a cause of glorifying the Exalted God, for the cause of prayer is glorifying God, and this glorification is not achieved except with purity. Take this and memorize it. So the categories of al-hukm al-wad'i are three, and its detailed divisions are six.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage discusses the third category of al-hukm al-wad'i (prescriptive ruling), which is the ruling that a description serves as a condition (shart). The author first gives an example of a preventer (mani') for a cause: if someone has wealth reaching nisab, it is a cause for zakat, but debt prevents that causality. Then he proceeds to condition. There are two types: (1) a condition for the ruling itself, such as the ability to deliver in a sale contract; the ruling (sale) is valid only if both parties can deliver the subject matter and price. (2) a condition for the cause, such as ritual purity (taharah) being a condition for prayer; prayer is a cause for glorifying God, but that glorification is valid only with purity. The author concludes that the categories of hukm wad'i are three (cause, condition, preventer) and their detailed subdivisions are six, without elaborating here.

Key Terms
الحكم الوضعي (al-hukm al-wad'i): A prescriptive ruling that establishes something as a cause, condition, or preventer for a legal ruling.
شرط (shart): A condition that must exist for a ruling to be valid or for a cause to be effective.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ability to deliver in a sale
B) Ritual purity (taharah) for prayer
C) Presence of nisab for zakat
D) Debt preventing zakat

Key Takeaway
A condition (shart) in Hanafi usul can be attached either to the hukm itself or to its cause (sabab), and distinguishing them clarifies how conditions affect validity.
